Summary 0:
Cross-border distribution of collective investment undertakings — cross-border marketing of collective investments (AIFs) and UCITS (Regulation (EU) No 1286/2014)
Transparency and investor protection: cross-cutting rules on marketing communications for collective investments in transferable securities (UCITS) in the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/61/EU on the European Investment Funds Directive (see summary);
Regulation 2011/65/EC on collective investment* undertakings (See summary).
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
Directives 2011/60/EU and 2011/64/EC set out rules on the cross-fostering of collective investing undertakings in the EU (known as collective investment funds) and Regulation (EC) No 1095/2010 on the distribution of alternative investment funds.
The new rules aim to ensure that marketing communications to investors are clearly identifiable as such, and to describe the risks and rewards of purchasing units or shares of an AIF or UCITS in an equally prominent manner.
Regulations (EC, EC, ESMA) 2019/1156 and 2019/1286 introduce new rules for marketing communications.
KEY POINTS
The rules apply to marketing communications of AIFs, including:
marketing communications addressed to investors in a collective investment undertaking (collective investment undertaking) which is managed by a management company (a collective investment company) or by an investment manager (i.e. a company that manages collective investment assets);
communications addressed to retail investors (e.g. investors who buy units of an investment unit of an alternative investment fund) which are marketed to them;
communications to investors who purchase units of a UCITS that are marketed directly to them.
EU countries must ensure that the marketing communications are clearly identified as such and that they contain the following information:
the AIFM, the EuVECA manager, EuSEF manager or the UCITS management company has the right to terminate the arrangements made for marketing.
Marketing communications must contain a description of the AIF’s investment objectives, the risks associated with the investment and the potential rewards of the investment, the investment is likely to entail and the information on the rights of investors.
Investors must also be informed of the rights and obligations of the management company and of the EU country in which the marketing communication is to be placed.
In addition, all information included in marketing communications must be presented in a manner that is fair, clear and not misleading.
Marketing communications must specify where, how and in which language investors can obtain summarised information on investor rights and they must clearly state that:
investors have the right of withdrawal from the marketing agreement;
the management company may terminate the agreement if it does not fulfil the obligations of its management company;
investor rights may be terminated at any time by the investment manager or by the investor if the investment management company fails to fulfil its obligations under the terms of the agreement.
National authorities must publish a central database containing information on national laws and regulations and administrative provisions applicable to marketing communication on their websites in, as a minimum, a language customary in the sphere of international finance, including their non-official summaries. The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) must also publish such summaries on its website.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
   
For more information, see:
Alternative investment funds (European Investment Funds) (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Collective investment undertaking: an investment undertaking is an investment company managed by an individual or a group of individuals.
* Alternative investment fund: a fund that invests in assets such as stocks, bonds, cash, cash-equivalents, fixed income securities, derivatives, real estate and other financial instruments, such as bonds, bonds and real estate securities.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i   The regulation is a delegated act of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) and has to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2021. It repeals and replaces Regulation 2011/62/EU, which had to become applicable by 1 February 2021. The new rules apply from 1 January 2022.

Summary 1:
Market surveillance and compliance of products — market surveillance and controls on non-compliant products
Market surveillance of products— market surveillance of non-compliance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1020 on market surveillance, market surveillance* and control of products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all products on the EU market comply with EU harmonisation legislation and therefore fulfil requirements providing a high level of protection of public interests, such as health and safety in general, protection of consumers, the environment, public security and protection of any other public interests protected by that legislation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products that are subject to EU legislation listed in Annex I of Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the harmonisation of EU legislation on the manufacture, import and export of food, animal feed, medicinal products, products of other than food, and products of plants and animals. It does not apply to products manufactured in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the products covered by the regulation are placed on the market by the competent authorities of the EU country in which they are manufactured and that they comply with the relevant EU legislation. They must inform the European Commission of any products that do not comply with this regulation and inform the Commission of the reasons for this.
The Commission has the authority responsible for enforcing the regulation and the European Rapid Information Exchange System (RAPEXEX), which allows EU countries to exchange information on products placed on their territory. The Commission may also use the Rapid Information System to detect and prevent the placing on the European Union market of products which are not complying with the regulation.
National authorities must cooperate with each other in order to identify products that may be a threat to public health, safety or the environment. They may also cooperate with the European database on medical devices (EUDAMED) and with other organisations representing economic operators or end users.
In addition, EU countries must cooperate in the event of the placing of a product on the territory of an EU country that does not fulfil the requirements of the legislation. If a product is found to be a risk to the public or to the economy, the national authorities must take the necessary measures to remove the product from the market.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
 
    даранска барка  гародна гракода  
It has applied since 25 June 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
  Ибанкаганна 
Directive 2001/95/EC lays down the general safety requirements for all consumer products and provides for specific obligations and powers of the Member States in relation to dangerous products as well as for the exchange of information to that effect through the rapid information exchange system RAPEX.
For more information, see:
‘Market surveillance’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 2:
Online retransmissions of television and radio programmes — copyright and related rights — EU rules for online services and retransmission of television programmes (Directive (EU) 2019/789) — online retranscriptions of TV and radio broadcasts (direct injection of retransmitters)
Online broadcasts of television or radio programmes – EU rules on the exercise of copyright and other related rights
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THE DIRECTIVE DO?
It lays down rules for the licensing of the rights to works and other protected subject matter contained in online broadcasts of broadcasting organisations (i.e. broadcasts of programmes that originate in another EU country) and online services ancillary to those broadcasts.
It aims to ensure that the rights of copyright holders and other rightholders are protected when retransmitting online broadcasts by broadcasting organisations.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
online transmissions of broadcasts of the broadcasting organisations originating in other EU countries (e.g. satellite, cable, satellite, digital terrestrial, terrestrial, mobile or closed circuit IP-based networks, as well as the open internet);
retransmissions by operators of online services aggregating broadcasts of a broadcasting organisation’s own broadcasts, such as simulcasting and catch-up services;
online services that simulcast and/or provide a live or on-demand version of a retransmitted television programme;
live or live-streaming of radio programmes originating in an EU country other than the EU country in which the retransmitter is based.
EU countries must ensure that:
the rights to these online services are protected by copyright or related rights;
the online services do not infringe on the rights granted by the broadcasting organisation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 17 May 2019. EU countries had to incorporate the directive into national law by 17 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report on the implementation of the directive in May 2018.
For more information, see:
‘Access to broadcasting’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 3:
The.eu top-level domain (TLD) — implementation and functioning of the.eu TLD and amending and repealing Regulation (EC) No 733/2002
The.EU top level domain (tLD) is one of the largest country code TLDs (ccTLDs) and is used by the European Union institutions, agencies and bodies, including for European projects and initiatives. The existence of such a domain name is important to strengthen the EU's identity online.
ACT
Regulation (EU) 2019/517 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 19 March 2019 laying down the rules for the implementation, functioning and licensing of the new.eu Top-Level Domain name and repealing the previous regulation.
SUMMARY
The new.EU TLD was established on 19 October 2019 and will be available to all EU citizens, regardless of their place of residence, from 19 October 2021. It will complement the existing ccTLD and allow for the global registration of generic.eu domains.
Registry
The European Commission will, on the basis of an open, transparent and non-discriminatory selection procedure, designate a registry to manage the new domain name. The registry will have the power to allocate.eu domain names to eligible parties, subject to availability.
The Commission will promote cooperation between the Registry, the EU Intellectual Property Office (EUIPO) and other EU agencies, with a view to combating the speculative and abusive registrations of domain names, including cybersquatting, and providing simple administrative procedures, in particular for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
Disputes with regard to the right of the parties to contract with, respectively, the Registry and Registrars will be settled by bodies located in the EU applying the relevant national law, without prejudice to rights and obligations recognised by the Member States or by the Union arising from international instruments.

Summary 4:
The Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) is a multiannual financial framework to accelerate investment in the field of trans-European networks to help connect and integrate the EU and all its islands and regions, including its remote, outermost, peripheral, mountainous and sparsely populated ones. It aims to contribute to EU action against climate change and support environmentally and socially sustainable projects, including, where appropriate, climate change mitigation and adaptation actions.
ACT
Regulation (EU) 2021/1153 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 7 July 2021 establishing the Connecting European Facility.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
   
It sets out the rules for the European Commission’s Multiannual Financial Framework (MFF) for the period 2021-2027.
It aims to accelerate investments in the transport, energy and digital sectors in order to improve the free movement of persons, goods, capital and services across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The MFF is divided into 3 phases:
1.
The 2021 MFF:
contributes 60 % of its overall financial envelope to climate objectives, based on the following coefficients:
100 % for expenditure relating to railway infrastructure, charging infrastructure, alternative and sustainable fuels, clean urban transport, electricity transmission, electricity storage, smart grids, CO2 transport and renewable energy;
40 % for inland waterways and multimodal transport, as well as gas infrastructure, provided that it enables the use of renewable hydrogen or bio-methane to increased.
.
2.
carbon reduction targets
3.
10 % of annual spending under the MFF for biodiversity objectives
7.5 % for biodiversity
4.5% for climate mitigation
5.5.6% for adaptation actions
6.7% for other activities
The CEF is open to all EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) Member States, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1) and Iceland (2) which are excluded from the scope of the CEF.
EU countries must ensure that the projects identified by the Commission are compatible with the EU's climate objectives. The CEF must also be compatible with other EU actions, such as:
the EU's 2030 climate and energy targets and long-term decarbonisation objectives;
the Paris Agreement and the UN Sustainable Development Goals;
EU commitments to implement the Paris Agreement, including the 2030 climate targets and the 2030 decarbonising objective;
its commitments to the Paris agreement and to the UN sustainable development goals.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 14 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the management of the EU-wide Connecting Europeans Facility (the CEF) and for its implementation. It is funded by the EU budget.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 5:
Dissemination of terrorist content online: hosting service providers’ liability
Distributing terrorist content on the internet is a serious threat to the security of the European Union (EU) and has serious consequences for users, citizens and society at large as well as for the online service providers hosting such content. This regulation aims to ensure the smooth functioning of the digital single market in an open and democratic society by addressing the misuse of hosting services for terrorist purposes and contributing to public security across the EU.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) 2021/784 — addressing the dissemination of terrorist material online
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes a legal framework to ensure that hosting services are liable for terrorist material that is disseminated through their services.
It aims to prevent the dissemination by terrorist groups and their supporters to disseminate terrorist material on the Internet.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to hosting services that are used for the purpose of carrying out illegal activities online.
Hosting service providers have a duty to protect their services from misuse by terrorists and to help the authorities to identify terrorist material and to remove it from the internet.
The hosting service provider is liable for any terrorist content that is distributed through their service.
However, the regulation provides for an exemption from liability for hosting services provided by hosting services in the event of a conflict of interest between the service provider and the authorities. This exemption is valid for a maximum of 5 years from the date of entry into force of the regulation and may be reduced to 3 years if the hosting service provides a service to the authorities of an EU country.
EU countries may decide to extend the period of exemption for a further 5 years if:
the hosting service is not a service provider in the EU country in which the terrorist material is hosted;
the terrorist content is a service provided by a third party for the purposes of facilitating and directing terrorist activity;
it is not the responsibility of the host service provider to identify and remove the terrorist content.
In addition, EU countries may exempt hosting services from liability if the content is:
disseminated in a way that does not lead to the detection of terrorist acts;
is not the result of an act of terrorism;
does not lead directly or indirectly to terrorist acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
It has applied since 17 May 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 17 May 2023.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has launched a number of initiatives to combat the spread of terrorism online, including the creation of a European Network of Anti-Terrorism Coordination Agencies (ENATCA).
For more information, see:
‘Countering terrorism online’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 — rules for participation and dissemination
Framework Programmes for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2027 — details for participation, dissemination and funding
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 establishing Horizon Europe – the Framework Programme for research and innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for participating in Horizon Europe, the European Union’s (EU's) multiannual programme for research & innovation (R&I), as well as the financial framework for the programme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme aims to support the creation, better diffusion and transfer of high-quality and excellent scientific and technological knowledge in the EU, attract talent at all levels and contribute to full engagement of the EU's talent pool, facilitate collaborative links and strengthen the impact of R&I in developing, supporting and implementing EU policies.
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote the European research area (ERA) in which researchers, scientific knowledge and technology circulate freely and encouraging it to become more competitive, including in its industry, while promoting all research and innovative activities to deliver on the Union's strategic priorities and commitments, which ultimately aim to promote peace, the union's values and the well-being of its peoples.
To achieve these objectives, the programme supports the creation and better diffusion of high quality and excellent knowledge in Europe, in particular in small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and in society, addressing global challenges, including climate change and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), create jobs, boost economic growth, promote industrial competitiveness and boost the attractiveness of the Union in the field of R &I.
Funding
The European Commission is responsible for administering the programme, which is funded by the European Research Fund (ERF) and the European Investment Bank (EIB). The EIB provides the EIB with the financial support for the activities of the Horizon Europe programme. It also contributes to the overall budget of Horizon Europe.
Programmes
Each year, Horizon Europe receives €2.5 billion from the European Commission, with the remainder coming from the EU countries.
Activities
The activities supported under Horizon Europe are based on the principles of:
open science;
open innovation;
innovation-friendly regulation;
research-friendly funding.
Participation
The EIB supports the participation of EU countries in the programme through a number of mechanisms, including:
participation in the EIF and EIB;
selection of EIB projects;
funding of research-friendly activities;
support for research-related activities.
Beneficiaries
The beneficiaries of the funding are selected on the basis of the following criteria:
the quality of the research;
the impact of the work on the beneficiaries’ interests; and
the potential for the research to have a positive impact on society.
Dispute settlement
The Commission must settle any disputes between EIB and the participating EU countries by means of a dispute settlement procedure.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
  The EU has a long-term commitment to investing at least 3 % of its gross domestic product (GDP) in research and development.
For more information, see:
Horizon Europe (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Open science: the concepts of 'open science', 'open innovation' and ‘open to the public’ are used to describe the way in which research is conducted and disseminated.
* Innovative regulation: a type of regulation that aims to encourage the development of innovative and sustainable solutions.

Summary 7:
EU Space Programme and the European Union Agency for the Space Programme (ESA)
EU Space Program and ESA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/696 establishing the EU Space Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s space programme and the ESA (European Space Agency).
It aims to encourage scientific and technological progress and supports the competitiveness and innovation capacity of space sector industries within the EU, in particular small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), start-ups and innovative businesses.
KEY POINTS
The EU has been developing its own space initiatives and programmes since the end of the 1990s, namely the European Geostationary Navigation Overlay Service (EGNOS) and then Galileo and Copernicus, which respond to the needs of EU citizens and the requirements of public policies.
The ESA is responsible for:
providing the European Commission with information on the activities of the EU space programme;
supporting the development of new technologies in the space sector;
promoting the use and uptake of the data, information and services provided, as well as developing downstream applications based on those data,information and services.
EU countries must ensure that the ESA is autonomous and that it is able to launch and operate its own launch facilities.
It must also ensure that:
the ESA has the necessary resources to carry out its tasks;
it is independent from the Commission and the EU countries’ national authorities;
the EU countries have the necessary financial resources to support its activities;
its work is independent of the national authorities.
ESA is funded by the European Investment Fund for the Development of the European Space Infrastructure (EIFESA), which is managed by the EIFESA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 April 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has been a leading international player in the field of space for over 20 years. The EU is one of the most competitive in the world.
For more information, see:
EU space programme (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
 
Article 189(2) of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) establishing the Union Space Programme of 28 April 2021 (OJ L 170, 12.5.2021, pp. 69-74)
Successive amendments to Article 189 of the TEU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
Digital Europe programme — 2021-2027 — financial rules for the EU’s digital innovation and innovation programme (DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Digital Europe Programme — 2021 - 2027 — funding for the European digital innovation & innovation programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/694 — establishing the Digital Europe programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules for funding the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Parliament (European Parliament and of the Council of the EU) for the period 2021–2027.
KEY POINTS
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the digital Europe programme (the ‘Programme’) for a period of seven years, from 2021 to 2027. It is funded by the EU budget.
The programme is funded through grants, prizes, procurement, indirect management, financial instruments, budgetary guarantees, financial assistance and the reimbursement of external experts. The European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) has the power to carry out administrative investigations, including on-the-spot checks and inspections, with a view to establishing whether there has been fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the Union.
In accordance with Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046, EU countries must cooperate fully with OLAF in the investigation of any offences affecting the EU's financial interests.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLANIMATE APPLYORK?
The regulation (Regulation(s) sets out rules on the funding of the programme.
It applies from 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Digital Europe Programme (MFF) 2021-2020 is the first of the three multiannual financial framework for the 2020–2025 period. The second MFF is the second phase of the European budget, which runs from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2022.
For more information, see:

Summary 9:
Single market programme for the internal market, competitiveness of enterprises, including small and medium-sized enterprises, the area of plants, animals, food and feed, and European statistics (Single Market Programme)
Single market program for the external market, competition of businesses and the production of European statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/690 establishing a programme for EU action in the fields of competitiveness of businesses, especially SMEs, consumer protection, customers and end-users in financial services, policymaking in the financial services and in the plant, animal, feed and feed area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Single Market Programme (SMP) which aims to improve the competitiveness of EU businesses, including SMEs. It also aims to promote fair competition in the EU’s internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The SMP covers the following areas:
Competitiveness of businesses
EU businesses must be able to compete on a level playing field by:
providing high-quality products and services at lower prices for consumers;
creating new products, services, processes and business models;
ensuring that EU businesses can compete on equal terms with non-EU businesses;
promoting the free movement of goods and services between EU countries;
encouraging EU businesses to invest more in research and development;
developing innovative products and processes;
improving the quality of EU services;
increasing the transparency of EU policies;
enhancing the efficiency of the European statistical system (ESS).
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the SMP.
Financing
The programme is financed by the European Union (EU), the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC) and the European Investment Bank (EIB). The SMP has a budget of €2.5 billion for the period 2021-2027.
The EU budget is divided into 3 budget lines:
Internal market budget: €1.8 billion for 2021-2020;
European statistics budget (€1.2 billion for 2027-2028).
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 3 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  The SPM is one of the main pillars of the single market. Since its inception, it has proved a major contributor to growth, competitiveness and employment and it should continue benefitting all citizens and businesses equally.
For more information, see:
‘The Single Market’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Competitiveness: the ability of a business to compete in an open and competitive market. It means that a business can offer a high level of service at a low cost to its customers.

Summary 10:
Control of the acquisition and possession of weapons — EU rules on the sale of firearms and their essential components (Directive (EU) 2021/555)
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the EU’s new rules on control of the access to firearms and essential components
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to increase the traceability of all firearms, essential components and ammunition in order to prevent illegal trade in weapons.
It codifies and repeals Directive 91/477/EEC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 2021.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all firearms or essential components, including handguns, shotguns, rifles, assault rifles, handguns and assault rifles. It does not apply to the carrying of weapons, hunting or target shooting.
Access to the data-filing systems of EU countries
EU countries must ensure that all firearms are marked with a clear, permanent and unique marking and registered in the national data-filtering systems of the country in which they are registered. These systems must contain the name of the manufacturer or manufacturer of the firearm and the year of manufacture of the weapon, as well as the serial number of the receiver and the type of frame.
The data must be stored in a centralised or decentralised computerised system and must be accessible by authorised authorities, including the police, judicial and other authorised authorities.
National authorities must have access to the computerised data-processing systems in which the necessary information regarding each firearm is recorded.
. Access by the authorised authorities must be subject to compliance with Article 8 of the European Convention for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms.
Authorisations for the acquisition or possession of firearms must be issued by a dealer authorised by the competent authority of an EU country. The dealer must provide the authorised authority with all the information necessary to authorise the purchase of a firearm, including:
the name and serial number;
the type of firearm;
a description of the intended use of the firearms;
information relating to the manufacturer, the manufacturer and the country of manufacture;
details of the accessories used to make the firearm.
An EU country may refuse to issue an authorisation to a dealer if it considers that the dealer is not in a position to fulfil those obligations and in so far as those obligations are not fulfilled by the dealer as regards the same underlying transaction.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 6 April 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 6 April 2023.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat the illegal trade of firearms within the EU. These include:
Directive 2001/82/EC on the registration of firearms (see summary);
Regulation (EC) No 596/2003 on the identification of firearms in the European Union (See summary); and
Regulations (EC, EFTA, EU and EFTA) No 696/2004 on the establishment of a European Firearms Information System (see consolidated version).
For more information, see:
‘Traceability of firearms’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 11:
European Maritime Single Window (EMSWe) — harmonised rules for the provision of the information that is required for port calls in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2019/1239) (European Commission)
European Maritime single window (EMSWe) – harmonised guidelines for the processing of the data that ships bring into and leave from EU ports in electronic format (Directive 2010/65/EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out rules for EU countries to ensure that ships coming into and leaving from their respective EU ports transmit their information to each other via an electronic single window.
It aims to reduce the administrative burden on shipping companies by simplifying and harmonising the information procedures for fulfilment of reporting obligations.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that ships arriving in and departing from their EU port of call transmit their data to the maritime national single window in an electronic format in order to facilitate and expedite maritime transport.
The national single windows must be compatible with each other.
Each EU country must set up a common interface module for the use of the common interface software for system-to-system exchanges of information, developed at EU level. The EU country responsible for integrating and managing the interface module is responsible for updating the software regularly and in a timely manner when new versions are provided by the European Commission.
National authorities must ensure that the same data sets can be reported to each maritime National Single Window in the same way.
Declarants must be able to set up optional entry points for reporting and data service providers, such as Port Community Systems, can be exempted from this obligation as long as they do not impose disproportionate administrative burdens on the declarants.
In addition, EU countries must establish a manual interface for the operation of the maritime single window and make available to the user a graphical interface for uploading and uploading data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 25 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
  ‘EMSWe’ is the European Union’s single window for the transmission of information between maritime transport operators, relevant authorities and the providers of port services in the country of call, and other EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘EMSWE’ on the EU's website.

Summary 12:
Interoperable electronic road tolls
Interoperatable electronic road-toll systems
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/520 on the interoperability of electronic toll systems and facilitating cross-border exchange of information on the failure to pay road fees in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that all EU countries’ road toll systems (e.g. tolls) are interoperable so that they can be used across the EU.
It repeals Directive 2004/52/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
vehicles with an on-board electronic vehicle identification number plate (OBE) and a European Electronic Toll Service (EETS) provider’s hardware and software to cover the distance covered by the vehicle (distance-based tolls), or to the vehicle passing a specific point (for example, cordon pricing).
It does not apply to systems where the road fee due is linked to the time spent by the driver on the tolled infrastructure (time-based systems such as vignettes) or to vehicles where the tolling infrastructure is not connected to the use of the infrastructure.
EU countries must ensure that EETS providers are able to make use of, and link to, other hardware, software and communication technologies already present in the vehicle, such as satellite navigation systems or handheld devices.
EETS providers must also be able to provide users with the right to use automatic toll recognition (ANPR) systems to check whether the vehicle is carrying an OBE or not.
Annex II of the directive sets out the procedures for the automatic recognition of OBEs and ANPR systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 19 March 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 19 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘E-Tolls’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 13:
European network of immigration liaison officers (ELEC)
European network on immigration — ELEC
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1240 on the creation of a European network of EU-wide migration liaison officers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Network of Immigration Liaison Officers (ELO) to support the EU’s efforts to prevent and counter illegal immigration, smuggling of migrants and trafficking in human beings.
It lays down the rules for the deployment of ELCs to non-EU countries and the coordination of EU countries’ border management systems.
KEY POINTS
The ELC is a network of liaison officers from EU countries that work closely together with the European Commission (European External Action Service (EEAS) in order to share information on the migration flows of migrants, asylum seekers and applicants for international protection, as well as on the reintegration of migrants.
The role of the ELO is to:
provide the Commission with information on migration flows, including on the composition of migratory flows and the number of people who have applied for asylum;
facilitate cross-border cooperation between EU countries and third countries;
coordinate and optimise the activities of the liaison officers;
ensure the coordination and coordination of border management and border control authorities;
support the return of illegal migrants and their return to their country of origin;
promote the return and resettlement of unaccompanied minors;
encourage the integration of vulnerable people in the EU;
prevent and counter the trafficking of human beings;
assist in illegal immigration and human trafficking.
EU countries must ensure that the ELC operates in line with the national authorities of the countries of origin and transit of illegal immigrants and their family members.
Eligible EU countries must designate an ELC to each of their territory to carry out the tasks set out in Regulation (EC) No 377/2004 (see summary). The ELC will be responsible for:
monitoring the movement of migrants;
providing information on illegal immigration;
cooperating with the relevant national authorities in the field of border control and border management;
advising the Commission and EU countries on the implementation of EU migration policies;
sharing information on legal immigration strategies and channels;
helping the Commission to develop and implement the Global Approach to Migration and Mobility (GAMM);
coordinating with the other EU countries to ensure the effective implementation of all aspects of EU policies on immigration, including the efficient management of migration flows in accordance with Article 2 of Title V of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
Eliminating the risk of illegal immigration
ELCs are responsible for preventing and combating illegal immigration by:
encouraging cooperation between the competent authorities of non-european countries and their nationals;
implementing border control measures to reduce the risks of illegal migration;
developing and implementing the GAMM;
contributing to the effective management of legal immigration and to the resettlement of vulnerable persons;
participating in the return, resettlement and return of asylum seekers;
organising and supporting the return or resettlement of migrants who have been returned to their home country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
    дантарска барка  гракова глаша в  харика
  блакса карна д ганкагальна  
It has applied since 25 July 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 25 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Migrant: a person who is a national of an EU country and who has applied for or intends to apply for asylum in the territory of another Member State.
* Illegal immigration: the illegal crossing of the borders of another country by a non-European Union country to enter or stay illegally in another country, including by means of a false declaration of nationality.

Strengthening the safety and security of national identity and residence cards and for their families
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1157 — security of EU citizens’ identity cards, residence documents and travel documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries issue and renew identity cards or residence documents to EU citizens who have the right to live and work in another EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries to:
ensure that the identity card or residence document issued by an EU country is authentic and that the holder of the card or the residence document has been authorised by the competent authority of the EU country in which the card is issued;
implement a system to detect and prevent identity card and residence document falsification and to penalise those responsible for such fraud.
EU countries must ensure that:
identity cards are issued in a secure manner;
the holder of an identity card is authorised to use the card for the purpose of travel;
identification procedures are in place to identify the identity of the holder and to check that the card has been issued correctly;
authorisation procedures for issuing and renewing the card and the residence card must be in line with national law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  The Schengen area is a free-movement area in which EU citizens can enter and leave other EU countries without any border controls.

‘Schengen’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems in the field of border and visa management
Interoperation of EU data systems in this field
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 — setting up a framework for interoperability between EU Information systems for security, border and migration management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of checks at the EU’s external borders and to contribute to combating illegal immigration and to a criminal justice system.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up a common EU information system for the management of data for borders, security and migration. It aims to ensure that EU countries’ border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the necessary information at their disposal.
The system is designed to:
facilitate the exchange of information between national border guards and customs authorities;
ensure that national authorities have access to the information they need to carry out their duties;
provide information to the competent authorities of the EU countries and to the European Commission.
It does not apply to EU countries that do not have the right to apply for a visa.
EU countries must ensure that:
EU information systems for border management and security are interoperable;
the information they exchange with each other is secure;
national authorities are informed of any changes to their information systems.
National authorities must inform the Commission of any major changes to the system and inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEAS) of the changes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 22 May 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 22 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Interoperability: the ability of an information system to communicate with other information systems, such as databases, in order to exchange information.
* Information system: a system that allows a person to be identified by means of a single set of data. It can be used for a number of purposes, including:
identifying a person who is a threat to public security or public order;
checking that a person is not a danger to public order or public security;
implementing border control measures;
monitoring the movement of people and goods;
detecting criminal offences;
assisting in the investigation of terrorist offences or terrorist attacks.

Summary 16:
Promoting the use of renewable energy
Promoting renewables
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/2001 on the promotion of energy from renewable sources
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions in the EU by encouraging the increased use of renewables in the electricity sector, the heating and cooling sector and the transport sector.
It is part of the EU’s energy and climate policy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland. It does not apply to the EU countries’ obligations under the Paris Agreement on Climate Change (see summary).
It sets binding national targets on the share of renewable electricity and renewable energy in energy consumption and in the transport sectors to be met by 2020.
EU countries may set their own, more ambitious, national targets in order to deliver their planned contributions to the 2030 renewable energy target and exceed them.
The European Commission is responsible for assessing the progress made by EU countries in achieving these targets and for setting national renewable energy targets.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 21 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has committed to reducing greenhouse gas emissions by at least 40 % below 1990 levels by 2030. This includes the EU's binding renewable energy goal of at least 27 % of its total energy consumption by 2030 and the EU 2030 target for renewable energy by 27 %.
For more information, see:
‘Renewable energy’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption of new heavy-duty vehicles
Monitoring & reporting of heavy duty vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/956 — monitoring and reporting on the CO2 and fuel emissions from and new light commercial vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to monitor and report on the greenhouse gas (CO2) emissions and the fuel consumption from new heavy duty (lorries, buses and coaches) vehicles in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all new heavy commercial vehicles registered in the EU between 1 January 2021 and 31 December 2023.
It does not apply to:
light commercial vehicles (cars, vans and minibuses);
heavy commercial vehicles that are already registered in an EU country, such as trucks, buses or coaches;
new commercial vehicles coming into service after 1 January 2023;
vehicles that have already been registered in a non-EU country.
EU countries must notify the European Commission by 31 December 2020 of the total number of heavy commercial vehicle registered in their territory and the number of new commercial vehicles to be placed on the market by 1 January 2022.
The Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) of the information it receives from the EU countries and the EEC.
Each EU country must send the Commission a report every 3 years on the results of the monitoring and the data it receives. The report must be sent to the Commission within 3 months of the end of each calendar year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE ENTER INTO FORCE
  
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has set a binding target of at least a 40 % domestic reduction in economy-wide greenhouse gas emissions by 2030 compared to 1990. This target was reaffirmed at the European Council of 23-24 October 2014 on the EU’s 2030 climate and energy policy framework.
For more information, see:
Heavy-duty vehicle emissions (European Commission).

  The regulation sets out the rules for the monitoring, reporting and data collection of greenhouse gases (GHG emissions) from and the light commercial vehicle (LCV) and LCV fuel consumption (LCCV) data (LFCV) from 1 January 2020 to 31 December 2030.

Summary 19:
Safeguarding the security of gas supply in the EU in the event of a major disruption in the supply of gas (Regulation (EU) 2017/1938) — measures to safeguard the energy security of the EU (SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 on the conclusion of the European Commission’s communication on the short-term resilience of the gas system (COM(2018) 852) — on the European gas system and repealing Regulation (EC) No 994/2010
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The regulation aims to ensure that all the necessary measures are taken to safeguard an uninterrupted supply of natural gas throughout the EU, in particular to protected customers, in case of difficult climatic conditions or disruptions of the natural gas supply.
The decision concludes the communication on behalf of the Commission.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This regulation applies to all EU countries, the EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries. It does not apply to the countries which are not members of the Energy Union (TFEU).
It applies to:
EU countries’ national emergency plans and national preventive action plans;
the national gas supply contingency plans; and
the EU countries' internal gas market contingency plans.
It repeals Regulation (EU), which had to be in place by 31 December 2018.
SUMDARY
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation. It is assisted by the European Parliament and the Council.
Energy security is one of the main objectives of the energy union. It aims to boost solidarity and trust between the Member States and put in place the measures needed to achieve those aims. It also aims to reduce the impact of gas disruptions on the EU economy and the social impact on vulnerable groups of customers.
In order to achieve these objectives, the Commission must:
ensure that:
the gas market operates smoothly and efficiently;
there is no disruption of the internal market for natural gas;
gas supply is guaranteed to be uninterrupted throughout the entire EU, even if there is a partial or complete interruption of the supply from Russia;
all EU countries have the necessary infrastructure to guarantee uninterrupted gas supply to the EU and the EEA countries;
when a gas supply disruption is threatened, EU countries must take all necessary measures to guarantee that the gas market functions smoothly and smoothly;
coordination between the Commission and the other EU countries is in place to ensure the proper functioning of the market;
in the event that the market does not function smoothly and the market is not distorted, the national authorities must ensure that the Commission has the information it needs to make the necessary decisions;
if the market fails to function smoothly or if a gas shortage does occur, it must inform the Commission of the reasons for the failure to operate smoothly and inform the public of the consequences of the failure.
If the Commission considers that the situation is likely to deteriorate, it may decide to suspend or reduce the gas supply for a period of time to allow the market to operate more smoothly. If the market continues to function normally, it will be possible to restore gas supply and the Commission will be able to assess the extent to which the market has been affected.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
Regulation(EU) 2019/1119 has applied since 1 January 2019.
Regulations (EU/1939 has applied from 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The energy union is based on solidarity, enshrined in Article 194 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union, and in particular Article 194(2) thereof.
For more information, see:
‘Energy Union’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 20:
Energy labelling — EU rules for energy-related products — energy efficiency and smart energy appliances
Energy labels — EU policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1369 — framework for energy labelling and repealing Directive 2010/30/EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s framework for the sale of energy-efficient products.
It repeals and repeals the Energy Union Framework Strategy (EU-2020) which aims to reduce energy demand and reduce energy bills.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products placed on the EU market for the first time, including second-hand imported products, which fall under the scope of this regulation. It does not apply to products that are made available on the market for a second or additional time.
Products must bear the following information on their energy consumption:
the energy efficiency of the energy source used;
the amount of energy consumed by the product;
their energy efficiency rating;
how the energy consumption of the product compares to other comparable products on the same market.
The information must be displayed on the product label in a language easily understood by the end consumer.
Manufacturers must ensure that the information on energy consumption is clearly visible on the label and on the packaging of the products and that the label is accompanied by a description of the type of energy used.
EU countries may require that the energy used by a product be labelled in a way that is not misleading.
the label must be accompanied by the manufacturer or by a service provider.
An EU country must notify the European Commission of the country in which the product was placed on its territory and the country where it was placed.
National authorities must inform the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the measures taken to ensure that consumers can make informed choices based on the information provided by the label.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 28 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 28 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  — Energy labelling (European Commission)
KEY TERMS
* Energy Labelling: a label on a product which informs consumers of its energy consumption.
* Smart energy appliances: energy-saving products that can be activated by smart energy monitoring systems and other smart energy management systems, such as smart fridges, that can reduce energy consumption by up to 50% compared to standard appliances. These smart energy monitors can be used to monitor the consumption of different energy sources such as electricity, gas, water and other renewable energy sources. They can also monitor the use of other energy resources such as wind turbines, solar panels and geothermal heat pumps. These products can also be used for other purposes such as heating, cooling and air-conditioning.

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data in the digital age: EU rules on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data and on the free movement of such data (Regulation (EU) 2016/679) (European Union)
Protecting human beings with respect to their personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 95/46/EC (General Data Protection Regulation) — the EU’s data protection rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the principles and rules to be followed by EU countries when processing personal data on behalf of the European Union (EU).
It aims to protect the rights and freedoms of all natural persons, regardless of their nationality or residence, and to ensure the free flow of their data between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the principles of, and rules on how EU countries must protect the personal data of all EU citizens.
EU countries must ensure that:
EU citizens have the right to:
the right to have their data processed only in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
to be informed of the processing and the reasons for the processing;
that the processing is authorised by the competent authority of the EU country in which the data are processed;
the rights and obligations of the competent authorities of EU countries to prevent, detect and remedy any breach of the rights or obligations of a natural person.
National authorities must:
ensure that personal data are only processed in a way that is consistent with the national law of the country where the processing takes place;
not use personal data for the purpose of the purpose for which they were provided;
ensuring that the data is only processed for the purposes of the purposes for which it was provided for under the regulation.
They must also ensure that the processing only takes place within the EU and does not affect the rights of the natural person concerned.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation in the EU countries and for ensuring that it is implemented in line with the principles set out in the European Charter of Fundamental Rights.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 4 May 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 4 May 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to ensure that EU citizens have a high level of protection in relation to personal data.
For more information, see:
‘Personal data protection’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 22:
Harmonised indices of consumer prices and the house price index (HPI)
Harmonomically-integrated EU statistics — the HICP and the HPI
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/792 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 May 2016 establishing a common framework for the development, production and dissemination of harmonised indices* of consumer and house prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the harmonisation of EU countries’ national statistics on consumer and housing prices.
It repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 2494/95.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1). It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United States, Canada, Japan and the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to the EU’s single market, the European Union (EU), the EU countries (1) and the countries of the Schengen area (2) which are not part of the single market.
The European Commission has the right to adopt delegated acts to extend the scope of the regulation to cover the whole of the EU.
EU countries must ensure that:
the harmonised consumer price index is calculated on the basis of the harmonised index of prices at constant tax rates (HICP-CT);
the European Commission publishes an annual report on the results of the collection of the data for each year;
;
the Commission publishes a report every 3 years on the quality, relevance and comparability of the indices.
National authorities are responsible for compiling the data and for publishing the annual report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    дарганарка башка  храка is the abbreviation for the European classification of individual consumption according to purpose (ECOICOPECICOP). It is designed to ensure that all European statistics relating to private consumption are consistent and comparable. It is used by the European System of Central Banks (ESCB) to measure the achievement of the price stability objective under Article 127 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU).
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of EU statistics. These include:
establishing a common methodology for calculating the EU's harmonised price indices;
introducing a harmonised methodology for the calculation of the HCP-CT;
ensuring that EU countries have the same methodology to calculate the HIP-CT.

Summary 23:
Protection against dumped imports from non-market-economy countries (non-EU countries)
Protection from dumped imports (Non-EU country)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1036 on protection against dumped imported imports from countries not members of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 1225/2009 on anti-dumping measures.
It lays down rules on the calculation of the ‘normal value’, the price of imported goods and the procedures for initiating and pursuing an investigation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to imports of steel, aluminium, copper, zinc, lead, lead-acid batteries and certain other products from nonmarket economies* from countries other than the EU. It does not apply to imports from the EU’s main trading partners, such as China, Japan, South Korea, Taiwan and the United States of America.
Importers must notify the European Commission of any imports they consider to be dumped.
The Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee (EEAS) and the Committee of the Regions of the reasons for its decision to launch an investigation and of the measures it intends to impose on the importing country.
If the Commission concludes that the imports are dumped, it must inform EU countries of the reason for the decision. It must also inform the other EU countries involved in the investigation and notify them of its decision.
.
Dumping is defined in the 1994 Anti-Dumping agreement between the EU and its major trading partners (GATTACHTS (General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade 1994).
The Agreement on Implementation of Article VI of the GATT sets out the procedure for initiating an investigation, including the establishment and treatment of the facts, the imposition of provisional measures (suspension of duties) and, where appropriate, the collection and collection of duties, the duration and review of anti-duty measures and the public disclosure of information relating to the investigation. It also lays down a procedure for determining the normal value of the goods to be imported.
In addition, the Commission must provide the EU countries with a list of the factors which may be relevant to determining the level of injury or damage caused by the imports. The Commission must also provide the other countries involved with the investigation with information on those factors.
EU countries must also notify the Commission of:
the date on which they intend to initiate an investigation;
the reasons for initiating the investigation; and
the time limit for the conclusion of the investigation, which must not exceed 6 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The 1994 Anti–Dumping Agreement between the US and the EU is the cornerstone of EU trade policy. It sets out detailed rules for the implementation of the agreement.
For more information, see:
‘Dumping’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  The regulation codifies Regulation (EEC) 1224/2009, which had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 July 2009. It repeals and replaces the previous regulation (Regulation(EC) 1025/2008).
  This regulation was amended several times, most recently in 2016. The most recent amendment was adopted by Regulation (EU), which introduced a new definition for the term ‘non-market economy’ in the annex to the regulation.
last update 14.07.2018



Summary 24:
Protection against subsidised imports from non-EU countries (subsidies and countervailing measures) (Regulation (EU) 2016/1037) — protection against imports from countries not members of the European Union (Subsidies & Countervailing Measures) (codification of Regulation (EC) No 597/2009) (OJ L 176, 30.6.2016, pp. 55-58)
Protection of EU consumers from subsidised imported goods (subsidy and anti-dumping measures).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules for the protection of consumers from imports of agricultural products from countries outside the EU (‘non-EU’) by means of a countervailable duty.
It codifies and clarifies the rules governing the application of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade 1994 (GATT 1994), the Agreement on Agriculture (Agreement on Agriculture) and an Agreement on Subsidies.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the conditions under which EU countries may impose countervailed duties on imports of certain agricultural products originating from non–EU countries. It also defines the types of imports covered by the GATT 1994 and the agreement on agriculture.
Countervailable imports are those that are deemed to have caused material injury or are threatening to cause injury to an EU industry by:
providing a financial contribution by a government or a public body within the territory of a country, or
requiring some form of income or price support within the meaning of Article XVI of the Subsidewinds of the WTO Agreement on subsidies and the Agreement of the same type,
using a benchmark to determine the amount of the subsidy to be calculated by adjusting the terms and conditions prevailing in the country concerned on the basis of actual factors available in that country.
The EU country concerned must notify the European Commission of the volume and price levels of the imports concerned and the information on the EU industry’s interests.
If the Commission considers that the imports are causing injury, it may impose duties on the imports. The Commission must inform the EU countries of the reasons for its decision.
Importers must provide the Commission with all the information necessary to determine whether the imports have caused injury or have threatened to do so. It must also inform the Commission of any other information that may be relevant to the determination of whether a subsidy is to be declared to exist.
In the event that the Commission concludes that a subsidy does not exist, the Commission may impose a duty on the import of the imported goods. The duty may be imposed on imports originating in the EU or originating in a non-European Economic Area (EEA) country. It may also be imposed in the case of imports originating outside the EEA if the imports originate in a country that is not a party to the agreement.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
Regulation(EU) 2017/1036 has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure that EU consumers are protected from the effects of imports of subsidised agricultural products. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EEC) 2015/852 (see summary) on the harmonisation of the rules on imports from certain non-EEA countries (Regulations (EC, EFTA) and (EU, Euratom) (Directive 2015/749) (see consolidated version);
Regulating (EU), Regulation (EFTA) 2016, Regulation (CFTA) 2017 and Regulation (CFA) 2016 on the establishment of a European Commission Anti-Countervailing Mechanism (Council Regulation (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Countervailed imports’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for EU countries’ economic policies
Technical support for the implementation of EU policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/240 establishing a technical support instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a Technical Support Instrument (TSI) to support EU countries in implementing their economic policies in order to achieve the objectives of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
It aims to support the EU’s Member States in their efforts to prepare and implement growth-sustaining administrative and structural reforms, including through assistance for the efficient and effective use of EU funds.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The TSI is designed to support Member States’ efforts to implement economic policies with a view to contributing to the achievement of the broad guidelines that the Council of the EU sets out in Article 174 of the TFEU.
It is a multiannual programme designed to provide technical support for Member States to implement structural reforms. It is funded by the European Commission, upon request from a Member State, and can cover a wide range of policy areas.
Programmes are designed to:
improve the capacity of the Member State to prepare for, implement and respond to economic, social and financial shocks;
provide technical support to the Commission to ensure that national strategies are coherent and coherent;
ensure that the priorities identified by the Commission are implemented in a coherent manner;
coordinate the priorities of the national strategies;
support the coordination of those priorities;
assess the effectiveness of national strategies and monitor the implementation;
contribute to national strategies in line with the Commission guidelines.
DATE OF ENTSI implementation
The regulation entered into force on 1 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the Structural Reform Support Programme (SSPP) for the period 2017-2020. The Structural reform support programme was established in 2017 to help Member States prepare for the COVID-19 outbreak in early 2020.

‘The Structural Support Programme’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights and obligations — exemptions from essential rules (recast) — rail passenger rights
Railpassengers’ right and obligations (react)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/782 — on rail passengers' rights — recast
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that rail passengers have the same rights as other passengers when travelling by rail in the European Union (EU).
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2021.
KEY POINTS
Rail passengers have a right to:
have the right to purchase rail tickets without undue difficulty;
have their luggage checked by the railway company before boarding the train;
be accompanied by an authorised person to ensure their personal security in railway stations and on trains.
Railways must ensure that:
passengers have access to the information they need to purchase a rail ticket;
the information they receive on their rights is clear and understandable;
railways must provide information to passengers on the conditions of the rail ticket, including:
the number of seats available,
the duration of the journey and the number of hours of the service;
information on the rights of the passenger, including the rights to refuse to board the train and to be checked by a person authorised to do so.
Passengers must also be provided with:
a written statement of their rights;
a copy of the transport contract;
an electronic ticket with information on the rail company’s obligations;
instructions on how to use the ticket and the information to be provided on the ticket.
a list of the railway companies authorised to carry out the checks on passengers.
EU countries may grant exemptions from certain essential rules, such as those relating to non-discriminatory conditions of transport contracts, to rail passenger services offered strictly for historic or touristic use, and to the requirement that railway undertakings be adequately insured.
Exemptions may also be granted for:
urban, suburban and regional passenger services that facilitate the use of rail services by persons with disabilities or persons with reduced mobility;
services operated under the auspices of the European Commission, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
The exemptions may not apply to essential rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
    да  барнашна гракова  
It has applied since 1 February 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 February 2022.

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance (IPA III) for EU countries in the process of joining the European Union (EU)
Pre-Accession assistance for EU governments in the period 2021-2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, 2021/1529 — establishing the Instrument for Pre-Entryion assistance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s instrument for pre-entry assistance for countries which have applied to join the EU.
It aims to provide incentives and guidance to the beneficiaries listed in Annex I to pursue the necessary far-reaching reforms.
KEY POINTS
Annex I covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
Beneficiaries are selected by the European Commission on the basis of the criteria set out in Article 49 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TEU).
The Commission assesses each applicant country on its ability to meet the Copenhagen criteria. The criteria include: the stability of institutions which guarantee democracy, the rule of law, human rights and respect for and protection of minorities, the existence of a functioning market economy as well as the capacity to cope with competitive pressure and market forces within the EU, and the ability to assume not only the rights but also the obligations under the Treaties, including the pursuit of the aims of political, economic and monetary union.
Each applicant country is assessed on its own merits. The assessment of progress achieved and the identification of shortcomings is carried out by the Commission. The Commission then forwards the results of the assessment to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC). The EEC then adopts a recommendation on whether the applicant country should apply for EU membership.
The EEC adopts the EEC recommendation on behalf of the EEU. The EASC adopts an opinion on the application of the Member State to apply for the EU membership of the Union. The decision is binding on the Member States and on the Commission, which must then adopt a decision on whether or not to grant accession to the country.
In the event of a country not meeting the criteria, the Commission may grant the country accession if it is able to:
ensure that the necessary reforms are in place;
promote the principles of democracy and human rights;
improve the functioning of the judicial system; and
establish a free movement of goods, services and capital within the territory of the country concerned.
If a country does not meet the criteria for accession, it may apply for an extension of the period of the instrument for a period of 5 years. The period of extension may be renewed if the country meets the following criteria:
strengthen democracy, respect for human dignity, freedom, democracy, equality and respect of human rights, including protection of persons belonging to minorities, and is committed to promoting those values;
increase the participation of women in the political and economic life of the population;
encourage the creation of a free market economy;
develop the capacity of the private sector to compete in international markets;
establish an effective system of taxation;
implement a system of public procurement;
facilitate the transfer of goods and services between the private and public sectors;
support the establishment of a single market economy.
A country may be granted accession only if it:
has met all the criteria established by the Copenhagen European Council in June 1993 (the ‘Copenhagen criteria’) and provided that the EU has the capability to integrate the new member into its internal market;
has adopted a comprehensive package of economic and social reforms that are compatible with the objectives of the enlargement policy;
is able to assume the obligations laid down in Article 21 of the TEU, including those relating to political, monetary and fiscal union;
ensures the protection and promotion of fundamental rights and principles;
provides the protection of the rights of minorities;
maintains the independence of the judiciary and the independence and impartiality of the police and judicial authorities;
contains the necessary resources to implement its reform process;
recognises the importance of the involvement of civil society in the economic and political life of its country;
cooperates with other Member States in the implementation of its reforms;
fosters the development of good neighbourly relations with the countries listed in the annexes;
supports the efforts of the Commission to implement the common foreign and security policy of the Schengen area;
continues to support the work of the European Observatory for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (EOPCW) and the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) in the field of the fight against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs) and in the area of the eradication of transnational organised crime (WCTs) in order to contribute to the prevention of WMD proliferation and the elimination of the WMDs in the region.

Neighbourhood, development and international cooperation instrument – Global Europe
Neighborhood, Development and International Cooperation Instrument – global Europe
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 — establishing the Neighbourhood and Development and Cooperation Instrument — Global Europe (the ‘Instrument’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s (EU's) (now the EU's) ‘NDFI’ programme for the period 2021-2023.
It aims to support the sustainable economic, social and environmental development of developing countries, with the primary aim of eradicating poverty.

The instrument is funded by the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC).
The programme is designed to:
promote the values, principles and fundamental interests of the EU in order to pursue the objectives and principles of the Union's external action, as laid down in Article 3(5) and Articles 8 and 21 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU);

* Development cooperation: cooperation between EU countries to promote sustainable development, including the reduction and eradication of poverty, and to strengthen international peace and security.
ACT
 
    The European Parliament, the Council and the Commission have adopted the regulation establishing the NDFI on behalf of the European Community.
