Summary 30:
Protecting whistleblowers who report breaches of EU law — EU rules on public procurement and procurement-related fraud
Protecting workers who report abuses of EU rules — EU policies
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/1937 on the protection of persons who report breach of Union law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that persons who work for a public or private organisation or are in contact with such an organisation in the context of their work-related activities are often the first to know about threats or harm to the public interest which arise in that context. By reporting breaches of European Union (EU)* law, such persons act as ‘whistleblowers’ and thereby play a key role in exposing and preventing such breaches and in safeguarding the welfare of society. However, potential whistleblowers are often discouraged from reporting their concerns or suspicions for fear of retaliation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all acts and policy areas where there is a need to strengthen enforcement, such as:
public procurement by national contracting authorities and contracting entities in relation to the execution of works, the supply of products or the provision of services;
contracts involving the procurement of services.
It does not apply to:
contracting authorities in the area of the implementation of the EU budget;
the contracting authorities of the Member States of the European Economic Area (EEA) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) in the field of public procurement.
EU countries may decide to extend the scope of the directive to other policy areas such as financial services and the financial services sector.
.
National authorities must ensure that whistleblowers are protected against:
by:
the protection of the confidentiality of their information
the prohibition of the disclosure of the identity of the person who made the disclosure
the restriction of the communication of confidential information to the authorities of other Member States
the limitation of the right of access to the information of a person who has made a disclosure to the competent authorities of another Member State.
Whistleblower protection
The EU countries must also ensure that the person concerned is protected against the following:
a ban on the publication of the names of the persons who made a public disclosure
a prohibition on the use of the information provided by the person to commit a breach of public law
a limitation on the right to disclose confidential information
a restriction on the person’s right to access the confidential information provided for in the act
the right to be informed of the nature of the breach and the reasons for its disclosure
an obligation to inform the competent authority of the country in which the breach took place.
WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 26 November 2019. EU countries had to incorporate the directive into national law by 26 November 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EU whistleblower protection’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 31:
European border and coast guard (EBCG) — European integrated border management (EIBM) — strengthening the EU’s external border control and border management capabilities
European Border and Coast Guard (ECBG) – strengthening the European border and coastal security and border control capabilities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the European Union Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders — the EBCG
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union agency responsible for the management of the external borders of EU countries, the ECBG, which is responsible for assisting EU countries with implementing the operational aspects of external border management through joint operations and rapid border interventions, risk analysis, information exchange, relations with third countries and the return of returnees.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the EBBG's tasks:
to oversee the effective functioning of border control at the EU's external borders;
to carry out risk analysis and vulnerability assessments;
provide increased technical and operational assistance to EU countries and third countries through joint border interventions; and
to ensure the practical execution of measures in a situation requiring urgent action at the external border.
The EIBG has a standing force of 10,000 staff, which can carry out cross-border operations and carry out rapid border response and return operations. It also has the capacity to carry out return operations and return interventions, as well as to organise, coordinate and conduct search and rescue operations in the event of persons in distress at sea.
.
EU countries have the right to set up a standing border force of up to 10, 000 staff to support the EGBG in the field of border management.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 November 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  EU countries have been responsible for border management since the beginning of the migration crisis in 2015. Since then, the European Commission has taken a series of measures to strengthen the protection of the borders and restore the normal functioning of the Schengen area.
For more information, see:
‘Border management’ on the EBRD's website.

Summary 32:
Ensuring cybersecurity in the EU: the European Union Agency for Network and Information Security (ENISA) and on information and communications technology cybersecurity certification and repealing Regulation (EU) No 526/2013 (Cybersecurity Act)
Eneshing cybersecurity in Europe: ENISA and cybersecurity certification
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) 2019/852 — establishing the EU Agency for network and information security (Enisa) and repealing Regulations (EU, No 525/2013 and (EC, No 605/2013)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
Regulations (EC and EEC) 2017/955 and (EU), which set up the European Commission’s Cybersecurity Directorate-General (CDSG) and (EEC) 2018/853, aim to improve the cybersecurity of EU countries’ networks and information and communication technologies (ICTs) by ensuring that they are better protected against cyberattacks and that they can respond quickly and effectively in the event of a cyberattack.
The regulation sets out the rules for the establishment of ENISA, the EU agency responsible for cybersecurity, as well as for the certification of cybersecurity products and services.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC/953/2013.
KEY POINTS
   
The legislation sets up ENISA to:
ensure that cybersecurity products, services and devices used by EU citizens, organisations and businesses are protected from cyberattacks;
provide information on the cybersecurity features of ICT products, ICT services and ICT processes;
*;
assess the state of the cybersecurity situation in EU countries;
coordinate the response to cybersecurity incidents;
develop a common approach for EU countries to respond to cyberattacks.
ENISA is responsible for:
assessing the cybersecurity and resilience of the EU and the EU countries,
providing a regular assessment of the current state of cybersecurity and assessing the need to improve cybersecurity;
ensuring that EU countries are prepared to respond rapidly and effectively to cybersecurity threats;
preparing for and responding to incidents of large-scale scale.
In addition, the regulation sets up the CDSG, which ensures that the competent national authorities are able to share information on cybersecurity threats, including:
the nature and scale of the threat;
measures to be taken by EU countries in case of a cybersecurity incident;
the types of information that can be shared;
how to respond in the case of an incident.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 7 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to strengthen cybersecurity and to ensure that the EU is more resilient to cyber threats and attacks.
For more information, see:
‘Cyberattacks’ on the ENISA website.

Summary 33:
Harmonisation of gross national income at market prices (GNI)
Harmonomically harmonised gross national incomes (GNIs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/516 — harmonising the harmonisation of Gross National Income
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of GNI (gross national income) data, which are used to calculate the largest share of own resources in the general budget of the European Union (EU).
It repeals Council Directive 89/130/EEC, Euratom.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1), the United States of America (1) and Canada (1).
EU countries must send the European Commission (Eurostat) GNI information visits to other EU countries in order to verify the quality of the GNI aggregates and their components and to verify compliance with ESA 2010, as well as to ensure that GNI data are comparable, reliable and exhaustive.
National statistical authorities are invited to participate in the visits. The participation of representatives of national statistical authorities in GNI verification visits is optional.
The Commission is responsible for carrying out the inspections and for ensuring that the data collected are comparable across EU countries and that they comply with the relevant definitions and accounting rules of ESA 2010.
Inspections are carried out by the Commission on the basis of a request from the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC). The Commission may also carry out inspections on its own initiative. The Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEAS) of the results of the inspections.
Implementation of the regulation is governed by Regulation (EC) No 223/2009, which sets out the rules for the implementation of the EU’s own resources directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 19 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
  *  * 
For more information, see:
‘Gross National Income at Market Prices’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
 * Gross national income: a measure of a country's gross domestic product (GDP) which is divided by its population and divided by the total value of its goods and services. It is used to determine the share of the economy of an EU country in the overall EU budget.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —                     The European Parliament and the Council of 19 March 2018 adopted Regulation (EEC) 2018/852 laying down rules for implementing the European System of Accounts (ESA 2010) (OJ L 91, 29.3.2018, pp. 19-24)

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the EU (Regulation (EU) 2019/26)
EU type approval of vehicles and systems after the UK’s withdrawal from the European Union (TEU)
SUMMARY OF:
Article 50 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU — Article 50 — The United Kingdom's intention to withdraw from the Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the placing on the EU market of vehicles*, systems*, components*, separate technical unit* and other types of vehicles that have been type-approved by the European Commission (EC) or the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) in accordance with Directive 2007/46/EC (see summary).
It also sets out a procedure for manufacturers to obtain new EU-type-approvals from EU countries other than the UK.
KEY POINTS
The regulation allows manufacturers to continue the production of vehicles (cars, trucks, vans, buses and light commercial vehicles) and components (such as axles, wheels, brakes and brakes) that have previously been approved by the EC or the EEC.
It does not allow manufacturers to re-approve types already approved elsewhere in the EU.
Manufacturers can continue to place on the market vehicles and components that had previously been authorised by the UK or EEC or the EC, but which had not been approved in the UK, as long before the date of withdrawal.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers continue to have the freedom to choose the new EU type approval authority or the new UK type authority if they so wish.
They must also ensure that:
the new type authority is independent of the existing UK or EC type authority;
the manufacturer has the choice of the type authority or of an EU country to which it will submit its application for the new type approval;
manufacturers have the right to continue to use the same type of vehicle or component until the end of the transition period;
they have the option to use a different type authority for each new vehicle and component if they wish to do so.
The EU countries must notify the Commission of any changes to the regulation and notify the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the changes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    далибарска бакова  наркограциальног боль  гаралка  
It has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
The United Kingdom has been in a state of withdrawal since 29 March 2017 and had to notify the EU of its intention to leave the EU on 29 March 2018.
For more information, see:
‘The EU and the UK (European Commission)’ on the European External Action Service's website.

European Union agency for law enforcement cooperation
Europol - replacing and repeals Council decisions on the establishment of a European Police Office
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/794 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 May 2016 on Europol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up Europol, the EU’s law enforcement agency, to support and strengthen cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in preventing and combating organised crime, terrorism and other forms of serious crime affecting two or more EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes Europol as a body responsible for:
supporting EU countries in preventing, investigating and prosecuting serious crime;
providing the European Commission with information on criminal offences committed within the EU;
facilitating the exchange of information between national authorities in order to prevent and combat serious crime in the EU.
It lays down procedures for Europol to be subject to parliamentary scrutiny by EU countries, subject to point (c) of Article 12 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) and Article 9 of Protocol No 1 on the role of National Parliaments in the European Union.
The European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) are responsible for the oversight of Europol's activities. The European Commission is responsible for its day-to-day work.
EuroPOL is funded from the general budget of the EU and specific to the EU budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
   
It has applied since 24 May 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 24 May 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  EU countries have to inform Europol of any criminal offences they have committed within their territory.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the Europol website.
* KEY TERMS

ACT
  The regulation has to become law in the Member States by 24 June 2017. It repeals and replaces Council Decision (JHA) No 934/2009.

Summary 36:
European instrument for temporary support to mitigate unemployment risks in an emergency (SURE) following the COVID-19 outbreak
European instrument to mitigate employment risks in the event of an outbreak of the severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus (SARS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/672 on the establishment of a European Instrument for Temporary Support to Mitigate Unemployment Risks in an Emergency (SURES)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European instrument to provide temporary support for EU countries that are in difficulties or are seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control.
It sets up the European Union’s (EU's) temporary support system for the temporary support of EU countries experiencing severe unemployment risks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are exempt from the obligation to apply for the instrument.
The instrument is designed to help EU countries to reduce the impact on employment of the sudden and severe increase in public expenditure on short-time work schemes for employees and similar measures, in particular for the self-employed, as well as expenditure on some health-related measures in particular in the workplace.
Beneficiaries of the instrument are:
employers who have been temporarily placed on a short-term work scheme for the duration of the outbreak;
self-employed who have temporarily worked less than 12 hours a week;
the public sector workers who have not been working for more than 24 hours in the previous 12 weeks;
those who have worked for less than 6 weeks in the preceding 12 weeks.
EU countries may grant the EU countries financial assistance for up to 6 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 20 May 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission submitted a proposal to the Council of the EU on 30 March 2020 to establish a temporary support mechanism for the Member States experiencing severe difficulties in their labour markets. The proposal was accepted by the Council on the basis of a proposal from the Commission.
For more information, see:
‘SURE’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of central counterparties — framework for the recovery and resolution in the event of insolvency of a financial institution or a financial market infrastructure that is at the point of failure (Regulation (EU) 2021/23) (European Commission).
Recovery & resolution of Central counterparties (CCPs): framework for resolving insolvent financial institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation [EU] 2020/23 on a framework on the recovery, resolution and recovery of insolvent counterparties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a framework to deal with the potential for a central counterpartie (CCP) to be insolvent or to be in the process of becoming insolvent if it is exposed to a significant amount of financial risk.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CCPs authorised in the European Union (EU). They must:
facilitate the processing of financial transactions between counterparties;
provide the counterparties with the necessary information to enable them to make decisions on the resolution of their members’ insolvent status;
ensure that CCPs’ members have adequate collateral to cover the costs of the insolvent CCP’s resolution;
assess the extent to which the CCPs have been exposed to the risks of the failure of a counterparties.
The resolution of a CCP is handled by the competent authorities of the EU country where the CCP is based.
Regulators are responsible for the supervision of CCPs. They have the power to:
impose sanctions on CCPs if they fail to fulfil their obligations;
suspend the resolution procedures;
require CCPs to provide information to the authorities of other EU countries and the European Central Bank (ECB) if they have not complied with the requirements of the regulation;
take corrective action if a CCP fails to fulfil its obligations.
Implementation of the legislation
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. It is assisted by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the Committee on Financial Supervision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 16 December 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
The financial crisis of the 2008 financial crisis led to the creation of the European Financial Stability Facility (ESF). The ESF was designed to ensure that if one or more of the financial institutions involved in the crisis were to fail, it would not de-stabilise the entire financial system and damage growth across the wider economy.
For more information, see:
‘Resolving insolvent banks’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 38:
European Year of Rail (2021) — a European year of rail (2020-2023) — European Year of light rail (2022-2024) (European Union (EU) 2020/2228)
The European Union is launching a European Year (Euratom) in 2020 to mark the European Union’s (EU's) transition to a climate-neutral EU by 2050. The Euratom aims to encourage the use of sustainable and smart mobility, including rail, to meet the objectives of the European Green Deal. It also aims to promote the single European railway area (e.g. a single European rail area) which aims to link the EU's rail network with the rest of the EU by 2020.
ACT
Decision (EU, 2020/2328 of the EUROPEAN PARLIAMENT and of the Council of 23 December 2020 on a European Day of Rail [Official Journal L 437 of 28.12.2020].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE DECISION DO?
The EURatom initiative aims to support the development of a European rail network in order to provide rail users with more affordable, accessible, healthier, cleaner and more energy-efficient alternatives to their current mobility habits, while encouraging those who are already using sustainable transport modes, such as walking, cycling and public transport, to use them more.
It aims to ensure that rail users have access to the single railway area by 2020-2022, which will consist of:
a single railway network in the whole of Europe;
a network of intermodal rail links between the EU countries, including the Schengen countries, and
a rail network between the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries.
KEY POINTS
Rail users will be able to use the rail network of the EEFTA countries by 2023, including:
traveling by rail to and from the EEA countries;
using rail for the transport of goods and passengers;
taking advantage of the opportunities provided by the TEN-T (Trans-European Transport Network) and the Trans-European Network of Railways (TEN-TFN);
using the rail infrastructure of the EFTA countries to transport goods, passengers and freight.
Rail operators will be responsible for ensuring that:
rail users have a high level of safety and security;
rail operators are responsible for the safety of passengers and goods;
the safety of goods is guaranteed;
traffic congestion is reduced;
transport infrastructure is improved;
fares are reduced; and
the number of passengers travelling by rail is reduced.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 28 December 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 28 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is on the way to becoming a climate neutral, low-carbon economy by 2050, which means that it will emit no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and that economic growth will be decoupled from resource use.
For more information, see:
European Year 2020 (European Commission).

SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/1515 establishing a European Security and defence college
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE REGULATION?

The regulation sets out the mission and tasks of the ESDC.

The ESDC is responsible for providing training and education in the field of the EU's common defence policy (CSDP) at European level in order to develop and promote a common understanding of CFSP and CSDP among civilian and military personnel and to identify and disseminate best practices in relation to various CFSP, CSDP, issues through its training and educational activities. promote a better understanding of CSDP as an essential part of the CFSP;
to provide EU countries’ administrations and staff with knowledgeable personnel able to work efficiently on all CSDP matters within the wider context of the CSDP;

Its activities include:
facilitating the exchange of young officers;
promoting the European Initiative for the Exchange of Young Officers;
supporting research in domains related to CFSPD;
encouraging cooperation between EU countries and other EU countries with regard to CSDP policies, policies and procedures.

Summary 40:
Minimum requirements for water reuse in the European Union (EU) — minimum requirements for the reuse of treated waste water
Minimum requirements to be met by EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) 2020/741 on minimum requirements on water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the minimum requirements that EU countries must meet to ensure that treated wastewater is reused whenever it is appropriate and cost-efficient, thereby creating an enabling framework for those EU countries who wish to or need to practise water reuse.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out minimum water reuse requirements for EU countries.
The water resources of the EU are increasingly coming under pressure, leading to water scarcity and a deterioration in water quality. In particular, climate change, unpredictable weather patterns and drought are contributing significantly to the strain on the availability of freshwater, arising from urban development and agriculture. Water reuse is a promising option for many EU countries to reduce the impact of water scarcity.
EU countries must ensure that the water they reuse is treated in a way that minimises the impact on the water quality of surface water bodies and groundwater bodies and that it does not harm the environment or human health.
Water reuse is one of a number of measures that can help to reduce water scarcity in the EU, including the use of water-efficient technologies in industry and water-saving irrigation techniques.

- the Regulation lays down a list of water quality objectives for water bodies in the context of the Water Framework Directive (WFD) (see summary). These objectives are to:
ensure that water bodies have a good qualitative and quantitative water status;
limit the extraction of water from surface waters;
improve the quality of water in groundwater bodies;
promote water reuse through multiple uses for urban waste water, while ensuring a high level of environmental protection.

Water Reuse

Summary 41:
EU Blue Card — conditions of entry and residence for highly qualified workers from non-EU countries for the purpose of highly qualified employment (EU Blue card)
European Union (EU) blue card — conditions for the entry into and residence of third-country nationals for the job of highly skilled workers (EU blue card) (European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU)- 2021/1883 on the conditions of admission and residence in the EU for highly skilled foreign workers (Blue Card)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out the rules for the granting of the EU Blue Card to highly qualified foreign workers who are highly qualified in the fields of science, technology, engineering and mathematics (STEM) and information technology (IT) fields.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2009/50/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to EU Blue Cards issued by Member States to highly skilled non-European Union citizens (EU citizens) who have been admitted to the EU on the basis of a highly qualified job offer. It does not apply to the initial purpose of residence of the highly qualified worker, unless that job is another purpose which then changes to the job.
The blue card is valid for a period of 5 years. It can be renewed for a further 5 years if the job is a highly skilled job offer of more than 3 years.
Rights and obligations of the holder
The holder of the blue card has a number of rights and obligations, including:
the right to:
to travel to and stay in the Member State in which issued the card;
to work for the duration of the 5-year period of the card’s validity;
the possibility to work in other Member States for up to 5 years;
access to the benefits of the Blue Card, including the right to live in another Member State for a maximum of 3 years and to work legally in the country in which the card is issued.
Member States must ensure that the holder of a Blue Card has the same rights as non-Blue Card holders and that they enjoy under the national residence permits issued by the Member States. They must also ensure that Blue Card holders have access to the same information as other EU citizens and that the information they provide is used only for the purposes of granting the card and not for other purposes. Member States may refuse to grant the card if the applicant is not a Member State resident or if he or she is not an EU citizen or resident of an EU Member State.
EU countries may also refuse to issue the card to a holder if:
he or she does not have the necessary qualifications to perform the work for which the job offers are being made;
they do not meet the criteria for granting the job;
he/she has not met the minimum qualifications for the position for which he/she is being offered the job,
the job does not involve more than 2 years of work, or
the employer does not provide him/her with the information necessary to determine whether or not the job has been offered to him or her.
In addition, Member States must:
ensure that blue card holders do not have to pay any fees for the processing of the application for the card or for the issuing of the residence permit;
notify the European Commission of any changes in the procedure for issuing the card.
An EU blue card holder may not be refused entry to the country where the job offer is being made or to stay for more than 5 years, provided that the employer has provided the necessary information for the application.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 20 October 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been working on a new EU-wide scheme to attract and retain highly skilled and highly qualified migrants and to promote the mobility of those workers across the EU.
For more information, see:
‘The Blue Card’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Highly skilled foreign worker: a person with a high level of education, training or experience in one or more of the fields listed in the directive's annexes.
ACT
 
    The European Parliament and the Council of the European Union have adopted a series of measures to modernise and streamline the procedures for the admission of highly trained and highly skilled migrants to the Union. These measures include:
introducing a new system for the issuance of EU blue cards;
reducing the administrative burden on employers and simplifying the application procedure;
improving the information exchange between employers and applicants;
ensuring that employers and blue card applicants have the same access to information and the same level of protection.

Summary 42:
Asylum, migration and integration fund
Asylum and migration fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1147 establishing the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Asylum and Migration Fund (EMF) to support Member States’ efforts to ensure a comprehensive approach to the management of migration that is based on mutual trust, solidarity and fair sharing of responsibility among Member States and EU institutions.
It sets up the EMF for the period 2008-2013.
KEY POINTS
The EMF supports Member States in their efforts to:
prevent and respond to situations of pressure in solidarity in solidarity;
ensure that irregular and unsafe arrivals are replaced with legal and safe pathways;
facilitate the transfer of vulnerable asylum seekers to other Member States;
promote the sharing of responsibilities between Member States for the protection of persons in need of protection and for the integration of non-EU nationals in the Member States.
Funding
EMF is funded by the EU’s general budget, the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Investment Bank (EIB). The EMF has a budget of €2.5 billion for the 2018-2020 period.
The EU‘s general budgetary allocation is €1.8 billion for 2018-2021 and €2 billion for 2019-2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 15 July 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 15 July 2022.
BACKGROUND
The EAF is one of a number of EU policies designed to tackle migration, including:
the common migration policy of the Schengen area, which aims to reduce irregular arrivals and the number of deaths at sea;
the European integration policy, which seeks to integrate non-European citizens into the labour market and integrate them into society.
For more information, see:
‘Asylum’ on the EAF's website.

Summary 43:
Work-life balance for parents and carers — EU rules on parental leave and paid parental leave (Directive (EU) 2019/1158)
Work-Life balance for mothers and fathers — new parents and cared for by their partners
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that parents have equal opportunities to work and care for their children.
It lays down rules on the rights of both parents and their carers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland. It repeals Directive 2010/18/EU.
EU countries must ensure that:
pregnant women have the right to paid maternity leave and to parental leave following the birth or adoption of a child, to reconcile family and professional life;
carers are entitled to a paid leave of at least 6 weeks after the birth of their child and at least 12 weeks following the adoption of the child.
The legislation does not apply to the care of children under the age of 18.
Paternity leave is not covered by the directive. It does not affect the right of men to take paternity leave.
National authorities must take all necessary measures to ensure the equal sharing of childcare responsibilities between men and women.
Parental leave must be provided for in the national legislation. It must not be less than 6 weeks and should not be more than 12 weeks.
In addition, EU countries must provide for a minimum of 6 weeks of paid maternity and parental leave. The maximum amount of paid leave may be reduced to 6 weeks if the child is under 18 years old.
Workers must be given the opportunity to work part-time if they are not working full-time.
Employers must also ensure that employers are aware of the responsibilities of parents and take into account the needs of children with special needs.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive 2011/13/EU entered into force on 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive in the EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘Working with children with disabilities’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
  ‘Workers’’ — Working with children’s disabilities on the website of the Council of the European Union (European Commission).
 
Council Directive 2011/113/EU of 1 July 2011 on working with children on the basis of special needs (Official Journal L 188 of 12.7.2011, pp. 1-8)
Directives 2006/54/EC (4) and 2010/41/EU (5) have been repealed by Directives 2011/112/EU, 2011/114/EU and 2011/111/EU respectively.

Summary 44:
Fight against fraud to the EU’s financial interests by means of criminal law — fight against fraud on the common VAT system
Fighting against fraud in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/1371 on the protection of the EU's financial interests through means of civil and criminal law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat fraud against the common system of value added tax (VAT) in the European Union (EU).
It lays down minimum rules relating to the definition of criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU budget.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all measures which negatively affect or threaten to negatively affect the Union's assets and those of the Member States, to the extent that those measures are of relevance to EU policies. It does not apply to the management of EU budget appropriations.
It applies to:
the management of budget appropriations by the European Commission, the European Parliament or the Council of the European Economic Area (EEA) and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) bodies;
direct management of the budget by the Commission or by any public bodies or bodies under the control of the Commission;
contracts or agreements between the Commission and public bodies, such as the European Investment Bank (EIB) or the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA);
public procurement by the EU agencies or bodies established under the auspices of the EEA or the EEFSA.
EU countries must ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities are able to prosecute the perpetrators of the offences.
The EU countries must also cooperate with each other in criminal proceedings.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYORKREACH?
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has a common definition of fraud falling within the scope of this directive, which should cover fraudulent conduct with respect to revenues, expenditure and assets at the expense of the general budget of the Union (the ‘Union budget’), including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
For more information, see:
‘Fraud to the Union’ website.

Summary 45:
Interchange fees for card-based payment transactions — EU-wide rules for electronic payments
INTERFACE FEITS FOR CARD-BARRY TRANSACTIONS — EU countries must ensure that interchange fees are the same as those for cash-based transactions
INTERFERENCES
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the interchange fees charged by payment service providers for the use of payment cards (i.e. cards issued by the European Union (EU) Member States) for the payment of goods and services in the EU are equivalent to those charged by merchants for the same payment within the Member State.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all electronic payment transactions in the Member States. It does not apply to cross-border payments.
It does not cover:
cross-border credit transfers and direct debits in euro in the internal market;
card-based payments in the euro area;
transactions in other currencies other than the euro, such as the US dollar, euro, British pound, Swiss franc or Swiss franc.
The European Commission has published a list of the fees to be charged by card service providers to merchants for each type of electronic payment. These include:
interchange fees, which are charged by the card service provider to the card issuer for the purpose of making a payment on the card’s behalf;
payment service providers’ interchange fees to the merchant for each use of a payment service provided by a card issuer;
cash-based interchange fees, charged by a merchant for the purposes of making an electronic payment on a card.
In addition, the EU countries may charge merchants for certain other fees, including:
.
These fees are calculated by the payment service provider on the basis of the total amount of the payment made by the merchant and the volume of the transaction. They are calculated according to the total number of transactions that the merchant has carried out in the previous 24 hours.
EU countries may also charge merchants directly or indirectly for the services they provide to consumers. These fees must be calculated on a case-by-case basis and must not exceed the cost borne by the trader for using a given means of payment.
Amending the regulation
The Commission has adopted a proposal to amend the regulation in order to:
introduce a ‘Honour All Cards’ rule, which allows card issuers to refuse to accept payment services from a merchant if they do not meet certain conditions;
ensure that the ‘honour all cards’ rules are applied to all card-issued debit and credit cards, irrespective of the country in which the card is issued.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
Regulation (EC) No 924/2009 established the principle that charges paid by users for a cross-banking payment in euro are identical to those paid for the corresponding payment within a Member State, and the same for all other types of payment transactions covered by this regulation.
Directive 2007/64/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council has provided a legal foundation for the creation of a Union-wide internal market for payments as it substantially facilitated the activity of payment service service providers, creating uniform rules with respect to the provision of payment services. However, integration of the Union payment card market is far from complete as many payment solutions cannot develop beyond their national borders and new pan-Union players are prevented from entering the market.
For more information, see:
‘E-payments’ on the European Commission's website.
BACKGROUND
European Union (European Commission) website on interchange fees
KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee that is charged by an issuer of a card to a merchant to make a payment in the form of a credit or debit card. The interchange fee is calculated on behalf of the issuer of the card. It is calculated in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation. It includes the cost of acquiring the card and the costs of processing the payment. It may also include the costs associated with the processing of the transactions.
* Cash-based transaction: a transaction where a user pays for goods or services by cash.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
 
Regulation (EU] 2015/751 of the Member Parliament and the Council of 29 April 2015 on interchange fee for card–based payment activities (OJ L 123, 19.5.2015, pp. 1–8)
last update 14.05.2016

Europass — a common framework for the provision of better services for skills and qualifications (Europass) and repealing Decision No 2241/2004/EC
Europass – a common platform for providing information on skills, qualifications and training
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/646 on a common EU framework for providing better services to help people find jobs and qualifications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets up Europass, an online tool for the documentation of qualifications and skills in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the visibility of skills and competences and to improve skills intelligence and especially information for better career choices.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision sets up a common European Union framework to provide information to people who are looking for a job, studying or working in the EU and who are interested in learning or working abroad. It aims to make it easier for people to access information about their skills, their qualifications and their training.
It repeals and repeals Decision (EC) No 2243/2004 on the European Qualifications Framework (EQF) and its national coordination points (NQF).
The aim of Europass is to provide users with information on how to assess their skills and their qualifications. It also aims to increase the visibility and relevance of the EQF and to make skills more visible and comparable.
National Europass Centres provide support to users and promote the documentation.
The European Commission is responsible national authorities with the necessary to implement Europass.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
 across the EU.
 ?
The legislation entered into force on 2 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
EuroPassport is a portfolio of documents that individuals can use on a voluntary basis. It provides information on their qualifications, skills and training. It is designed to be easily accessible to all potential users.
For more information, see:
‘Europass’ on the website of the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion.

These actions include:
the creation of a European Skills Framework (ESF);
the introduction of the Qualifications Coordination Points (QCPs) to help national authorities in referencing national qualifications frameworks or systems to the European qualifications framework (the EQF); and
the Europass website.

Summary 47:
Statistics of goods transport by inland waterways (codification) (EU) 2018/974 - summary of the rules on inland waterways transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1365/2006 on statistics of inland waterways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals the previous regulation (Regulation No 223/2009).
It aims to improve the comparability of data on the transport of goods across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Inland waterways are an important part of the EU’s transport networks and the promotion of inland water transport is one of the objectives of the common transport policy, both for reasons of economic efficiency and in order to reduce energy consumption and the environmental impact of transport.
The regulation requires EU countries to provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with information on:
the total volume of goods transported by inland water in the EU by road, rail, inland waterways or by other means;
the number of persons and goods carried by these modes of transport in each EU country.
EU countries must also provide the Commission with:
data on the number of people and goods transported on their territory by road or inland waterways;
information on the total tonnage of goods moved across the EU in each year by rail, road, inland water or other means, and the total number of passengers carried by those modes;
details of the types of transport and the modes used.
In addition, EU countries must provide Eurostat with the following information:
a description of the type of transport by water and of the means used to transport the goods;
an estimate of the total amount of the goods transported;
a list of the countries where the inland waterways are located.
Annexes to the regulation provide definitions of the transport modes covered by the regulation, as well as a list of EU countries which are not covered by this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 16 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Inland water transport’ is a key element in the common EU transport policy.
For more information, see:
‘The inland water network’ on Eurostat's website.

  �   барскога   (European Commission).

Summary 48:
Rail transport statistics — European Union (EU) 2018/643 (recast) — rail transport statistics
Rail transportation statistics — EU level (REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Regulation (EC) No 91/2003 of the European Parliament and of the Council
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the collection and transmission of EU-wide data on rail transport by rail in order to enable the European Commission to monitor and develop the common transport policy, as well as the transport elements of policies relating to the regions and to trans-European networks.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 93/2003.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all publicly and privately owned railway undertakings operating in a commercial rail transport market in the EU.
The data are collected by the European Union Agency for Railways (Eurostat) in each Member State under the authority of the bodies and institutions in charge of producing official statistics.
EU countries must transmit the data to Eurostat in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex I to Directive 2012/34/EU on the production of official statistics on all modes of transport (see summary).
The EU countries must ensure that the data collected by Eurostat are:
harmonised across the EU;
accurate and up-to-date;
competent;
inform the Commission of the data they have collected;
how they will be used;
provide Eurostat with a description of the types of data they will transmit to the Commission;
ensure that Eurostat has access to the information it needs to compile the data.
Data collection
The Commission must collect data on the following:
the total number of passengers and goods carried on the rail system;
the number of goods transported by rail;
total number of freight carried by rail.
Eurostat must provide the Commission with:
data on the number and types of goods and passengers carried on a rail system, including the number of trains and the total amount of freight transported;
information on the type of rail infrastructure and the number, type and type of goods carried;
details of the type and number of train types;
data for the types and categories of freight and passengers that are to be carried on rail systems.
Each year, Eurostat must send the Commission a report on the implementation of the regulation. The Commission must also provide the Council with a report every 5 years on the progress made by the EU countries in implementing the regulation and on the results of the consultations conducted by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Егарска баркова грака  глабраша  
It has applied since 2 May 2018 and had to become law in all Member States by 2 May 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring and developing the EU’s transport policy in the field of transport safety.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person travelling by rail to and from a point of entry or exit in another Member State to or from a place other than the place in which he or she is registered in the territory of the Member State in which the train is operated.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Барца балкод  грха хлача

Summary 49:
Third countries whose nationals must be in possession of visas when crossing the EU’s external borders and those whose nationals are exempt from that requirement (codification) (Regulation (EU) 2018/1806) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the free movement of persons between the EU countries and the Schengen area
Decision (EC) No 539/2001 on the exemption from the visa requirement for recognised refugees and stateless persons
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The regulation sets out the criteria for determining whether a third country is subject to a visa for the crossing of EU countries’ external borders (also referred to herein as ‘the visa requirement’) or whether a non-EU country is exempt from such a requirement.
The decision is based on a case-by-case assessment of the third country in which those persons reside and which issued their travel documents.
KEY POINTS
Annex I to the regulation lists the third countries that require a visa when crossing EU borders for stays of no more than 90 days in any 180-day period, with the possibility of having country-specific amendments to those annexes in particular circumstances, for instance as a result of a visa liberalisation process or as the ultimate consequence of a temporary suspension of the visa exemption.
In Annex II, the list of third countries for which a visa is required is divided into two categories:
non-EU countries that have a visa requirement when crossing their external borders for a stay of at least 90 days, with a maximum stay of 180 days, and those countries that do not have such a visa.
A third country may be exempt from the requirement if it meets certain criteria, such as:
recognised refugees;
stateless persons;
nationals of the United Nations Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women (UNCTAD);
the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
The third country that meets these criteria must grant a visa to a recognised refugee or to a stateless person for a period of no less than 30 days. The duration of the period of exemption must not exceed 90 days.
Visa exemptions may also be granted for certain categories of persons, including:
visas issued by the Member State in which the person is resident;
travel documents issued by a Member State issued by another Member State;
visa permits issued by Member States issued by other Member States;
passports issued by non-Member States issued under the UNCTAD.
Exemptions may be granted to recognised refugees or stateless people who are:
traveling under the protection of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) (Article 77(2)(a) of the TFEU);
visiting the territory of an EU country under the auspices of an international agreement signed by the EU and its Member States (Article 81(2) of TFEUs);
travelers of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD) or of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (OCTAD).
A Member State may also grant an exemption to the holder of an ordinary passport for the duration of a 90-day stay, provided that:
the holder of the ordinary passport is not a national of the Member States concerned;
the person is a permanent resident of that Member State and has been resident there for at least 30 days;
there is no reason to believe that the person in question is a refugee or a Stateless person; and
there are no grounds to suspect that he or she is a member of a terrorist organisation.
If an exemption is granted, the exemption is valid for a maximum of 90 days and may be renewed for a further 90 days or for a total of 120 days, depending on the length of the stay. The maximum stay may be reduced to 90 days if the person concerned is a resident of the country for which the exemption has been granted.
Member States may suspend the exemption for up to 30 days if they consider that the exemption does not meet the criteria set out in Annexes I and II of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGUMENTS APPLY?
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2019/1101 and (EU), 2019/1201 have applied since 1 January 2019.
Regulatory (EU/1805 has applied since 14 December 2018 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been gradually liberalising its visa system for the past few years. It has gradually reduced the number of visas required to cross its external borders.
For more information, see:
‘Visa liberalisation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Visas: a visa issued for the purpose of allowing a person to enter the EU without a visa and to leave the EU.



Summary 50:
Eurojust — European Union Agency for Criminal Justice Cooperation (Eurojust) (European Public Prosecutor's Office (EPPO)
Eurojust – European Union agency for criminal justice cooperation (EuroJust)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1727 on the European Union’s Agency for Justice Cooperation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up Eurojust, a European Union body with legal personality, to stimulate and to improve coordination and cooperation between competent judicial authorities of the Member States, particularly in relation to serious organised crime.
It replaces and repeals Council Decision 2002/187/JHA.
KEY POINTS
   
 
The regulation sets out the structure of Eurojust and the role of the European Parliament and the European Council. It also sets out Eurojust's operational structure.
The European Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the national parliaments of the EU countries are responsible for evaluating the effectiveness of the Eurojust. The European Parliament is also responsible for the evaluation of the work of the EPPO.
In addition, the Commission is responsible for drawing up a report on the activities of the agency every 3 years on the basis of an evaluation by the European Judicial Committee (EJC).
The EJC is chaired by the Commission and comprises representatives of the national governments of the participating EU countries. It is assisted by the Council and the Council of Europe. It has a budget of €2.5 billion for the period 2018-2023.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE IN FORCE?
1.1.2019
  The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
2.
BACKGROUND
The Eurojust was set up by the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) in 2002. Its mission is to support and strengthen cooperation between national investigating and prosecuting authorities in the fight against serious crime affecting two or more EU countries or requiring prosecution on common bases.
For more information, see:
‘Eurojust’ on the EU's website.

Summary 51:
Combating money laundering by criminal law — criminalisation of public office holders (Directive (EU) 2018/1673)
Collaborating with other EU countries to combat money laundering — criminalising public office holder (direct and indirect)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2001/500/JHA — Criminalisation of money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to criminalise money laundering offences by means of criminal law, enabling more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between competent authorities.
It complements and strengthens the application of the EU’s 2015/849 directive on combating money laundering.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all offences that are punishable by a term of imprisonment as set out in this directive. It does not apply to:
terrorism or environmental offences;
money laundering in the context of the fight against organised crime;
tax evasion;
laundering in relation to tax evasion.
EU countries must ensure that the offences covered by the directive are punishable in line with the relevant EU legal acts of the European Union (EU). They must also ensure that they do not diverge in their national laws regarding tax evasion and tax evasion offences.
Penalties for the offences listed in the directive must be based on the following:
the amount of the proceeds of the offence;
the nature of the money laundering;
where the proceeds are to be used for the purposes of the criminal activity.
National authorities must also be able to provide each other with information on the persons or bodies involved in the criminal activities covered by this directive, such as:
.
The information must be exchanged in accordance with the national law of the country in which the offence was committed.
In addition to the above, EU countries must also provide the European Commission with a list of individuals or bodies that are subject to criminal sanctions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 November 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU is a signatory to the Council of Europe Convention on Laundering, search, seizure and confiscation of the Proceeds from Crime and on the Financing of Terrorism.
For more information, see:
The fight against money laundering (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Criminalisation: a criminal offence in which a person or body is convicted of committing a criminal activity for the purpose of committing another criminal offence.
* Indirect offence: an offence committed by a public official in order to commit an indirect offence, for example, to commit a tax evasion or tax evasion offence, or to engage in tax evasion, for which the person or entity is responsible for the payment of a tax or other tax obligation.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Directive 
EU  2019/852 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Member States of 23 October 2019 on the criminalisation by criminal laws of public offices of public institutions (OJ L 284, 12.11.2019, pp. 22-24)

Summary 52:
Recovery plan for bluefin tuna in the eastern Atlantic and Mediterranean
Restoration plan for Atlantic tuna
Regulation (EU) 2016/1627 on a multiannual recovery plan for Bluefin tuna
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas’ (ICCAT) recovery plan (the ‘Recovery Plan’).
It repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 302/2009.
KEY POINTS
The recovery plan aims to restore the stocks of Atlantic tuna by reducing the total allowable catch and the fishing capacity in the Atlantic and the Mediterranean in line with the conservation measures set out in ICCAT Recommendation 06-05 (see summary). It also aims to promote coastal fishing activities and the use of fishing gear and techniques which are selective and have a reduced environmental impact, including gear used in traditional and artisanal fisheries, thereby contributing to a fair standard of living for local economies.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the recovery plan. It is assisted by the Standing Committee on Research and Statistics of ICCAT (SCRS) which provides the ICCAT with additional advice on the identification of spawning grounds and on the creation of sanctuaries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   хх  
 
The regulation entered into force on 16 September 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU fisheries policy (CFP) aims to ensure an exploitation of living aquatic resources that provides sustainable economic, environmental and social conditions.
For more information, see:
‘Bluefin tuna’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 53:
Protecting against injurious pricing of vessels
Protecting from the damage caused by selling ships below their normal value
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1035 on protection against the sale of ships below the normal value of vessels (codification)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 385/96 on the protection against shipbuilding injurious pricing.
It aims to ensure that EU countries can take action against any shipbuilder selling a vessel below its normal value which causes injury to the EU industry.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules on the calculation of the ‘normal value’ of a ship.
The normal value is calculated using a representative sale of a like vessel in the ordinary course of trade in the exporting country. It must be based on a comparable sale of similar vessels in the importing country. The normal value must include the amounts for selling, general and administrative costs and the profit margin that should be included in such value.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any shipbuilders from contracting parties to the Shipbuilding Agreement who have sold a ship below normal value to a non-EU country and inform the Commission of the reason for this. The Commission may then launch an investigation into the sale.
If the sale is considered to be made at a loss and may be disregarded, the buyer of the vessel may have recourse to sale of the like vessel to a third country or to an EU buyer, or to the owner of the ship. If the buyer is an EU country, the sale may be subject to a countervailing or anti-dumping duty.
Annex III of the regulation sets out the procedure for the Commission to take all necessary steps to ascertain, in the large conglomerates and holdings of the contracting parties, the validity of accounting and financial statements.
In the event of a sale at less than normal value, the Commission may take all the necessary measures to determine whether the vessel was sold at a lower price than it should have been. If so, it must inform the shipowner of the reasons for the sale and inform him or her of the decision to terminate the sale or to sell the vessel.
Where a ship is sold at below its usual price, the EU country may impose an anti-subsidy or antidumping duty on the vessel if it is found to have been sold below the usual price.
Importers of ships may be liable for a duty of up to 10 % of the normal price of a comparable vessel sold to the importing or exporting country if they have sold the vessel at a price that is below the average price of the comparable vessel. The EU country which sold the ship may also impose a duty on a vessel which is sold below its average price. The duty may also apply to a vessel that has been repaired or rebuilt in another EU country.
A shipowner may also be held liable for any damage caused to the industry caused by the vessel’s normal value if:
the vessel was built in a country which is not a contracting party to the agreement;
the normal value was sold to an importer of a vessel built in another country; or
the ship was built by a shipowner in a non–EU country which was built under the auspices of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD).
The EU may also take other measures to protect the industry if it considers that the vessel is being sold at an unfair price. These measures may include:
suspension of the right to build the vessel;
imposition of duties on the ship;
restrictions on the right of the buyer to repair the vessel or to use the vessel for the purpose of repairing it;
temporary suspension of the rights of the seller or the buyer.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 30 June 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the legislation.
For more information, see:
‘Injurious Pricing of vessels’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 54:
Cableway installations — cableway safety and conformity assessment — EU rules (Regulation (EU) 2016/424 (see summary)
Cableways — cableways — cables and conformity assessments — European Union rules
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2000/9/EC — rules for cableway installations that are designed, constructed and operated with a view to transporting persons
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It lays down rules on the essential requirements for the design, construction and operation of cableways* in the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals Directive 2000/8/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all cableways in the EU designed, built and operated for the purpose of transporting persons. It does not apply to installations designed to transport persons used in particular in high-altitude tourist resorts, in urban transport facilities or in sports facilities.
The cableway installation must comply with the following essential requirements:
it must be designed and constructed for the transport of persons by means of a cableway system;
the installation must be in compliance with the EU’s harmonised standards for the installation of lift systems, such as funicular railways, aerial ropeways (cable cars, gondolas, chairlifts) and drag lifts.
It must bear the CE conformity marking on the end of the cableway and be inspected by a competent authority before it can be placed on the market.
An installation must bear a CE marking on its end. The CE marking must be affixed to the end-of-of the cable and be accompanied by a certificate of conformity. It must also bear the following information:
the type of cableway,
the design and construction of the system and
the purpose of the installation
the number of persons to be transported by the system
the duration of the operation and the number of hours of operation
the total number of people to be carried on the system.
EU countries must ensure that the CE marking is affixed on all cableway components and subsystems that are new to the market or that have been incorporated into the system by a third party.
Manufacturers must carry out a conformity assessment before placing on the EU market any cableway component or subsystem that does not conform with the requirements of the directive. They must also carry out an independent conformity assessment to check that the components are in conformity with the directive and that the installation complies with the necessary safety requirements.
Imports from non-EU countries are subject to the same requirements as those of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
    дантарска баркова грашка  глабракога  
It has applied since 31 March 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 March 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  настанскибаккой  блакца 
For more information, see:
‘Cables’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cableway: a lift system that carries people up and down a cable. It may be used to transport people on a cable car, a gondola, a chairlift, a funicular railway or a cable-car-style lift system. It can also be used for the transfer of passengers between two or more lifts. It is a means of transport by cable or by a combination of the two.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities in the EU — Directive (EU) 2019/882 (accessibility requirements for products and service)
Assistance for people with disabilities is essential for their full and effective participation in society on an equal basis with others. This directive aims to improve the accessibility of goods and services that meet the needs of persons with disability.
ACT
Directive (EU, 2019/884 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 17 April 2019 on the accessibility requirements for items and services in the European Union [Official Journal L 151 of 7.6.2019].
SUMMARY
The accessibility requirements laid down in this directive aim to improve access to mainstream products, services and assistive technologies for persons who have any physical, mental, intellectual or sensory impairments, age related impairments or other human body performance related causes, permanent or temporary, which, in interaction with various barriers, result in their reduced access to products or services, leading to a situation that requires those products to be adapted to their particular needs.
The directive does not apply to:
personal protective equipment (PPAs);
accessibility of lifts and escalators;
access to information and communication technologies (ICTs);
personal mobility devices (MWDs) and mobility devices;
motorised walkers and crutches.
Accessibility requirements are set out in Annexes I and II to the directive. The Annexes III and IV provide a description of the different types of accessibility requirements, as well as an example of a lift or escalator that meets a person with a disability’s needs. Accessibility requirements must be met in accordance with the principles of the United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities, adopted on 13 December 2006 (UN CRPD), to which the EU has been a party since 21 January 2011 and which all Member States have ratified.
In Annexes V and VI, the directive sets out a list of the types of accessible products that are covered by the directive and sets out the minimum requirements for each type of accessible product and service. It also sets out minimum requirements concerning accessibility of the information and communications technologies that can be used to make a product or service more accessible.
National authorities must ensure that:
all accessible products are made available to persons who need them;
the information provided by the manufacturer or supplier is clearly visible and understandable to the consumer;
there is a clear distinction between accessible products and products that do not comply with the accessibility requirement and those that do comply with it.
Annexes VII and VIII set out the requirements for the installation of lifts, escalators and other escalators in public places and for the use of MWDs. The directive also lays down minimum requirements regarding the installation and use of the MWD and MWD in public transport and for access to information on accessibility in the workplace and in the private sector.
References

Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal

Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence obligations for EU importers of tin, tantalum and tungsten from conflict-affected and high-risk areas (OECD Due Diligence Guidance)
Suppliers of minerals from conflict affected and high risk areas must ensure that their supply chain checks are carried out to prevent the financing of armed groups and security forces in resource-rich areas.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/821 — supply chain due-diligence obligations for importers in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down supply-chain due diligence* obligations for the EU’s importers* of tin and tantalum, their ores, and gold originating from areas affected by conflict or high risk.
It aims to ensure that the supply of these minerals does not support armed groups or security forces.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following minerals:
Tin, tin-tantalum-tungsten and gold ores originating from the Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC) and its neighbours in Central Africa (Central Africa, Central Africa, South Sudan and South Sudan (Côte d’Ivoire);
Molybdenum-Tungsten ores from the Republic of Congo (RDC);
Tonga-Tonga ores and gold from the Central African Republic (CAR);
Zambia-Kivu ores;
Zimbabwe-Tibet ores.
EU importers must conduct checks on the following the receipt of the relevant information from the relevant national authorities. The checks must be carried out by the competent authorities of the country concerned.
Importers must inform the competent authority of the information provided by the importer of the minerals they are carrying out. The importer must also inform the authorities in the country in which it is carrying out the checks.
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the compliance of importers with the regulation and for ensuring that the relevant authorities are informed of the results of the checks and that the importers comply with the requirements of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULUMENT APPLYORKS?
 
    б  хъ  �ълърахра  
It has applied since 19 May 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 19 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
  — KEY TERMS
* Conflict-affected areas: areas where armed groups, security forces or other armed groups are involved in armed conflict and where their revenues fuel the outbreak or continuation of violent conflict, undermining endeavours towards development, good governance and the rule of law.
* High risk areas: those where armed conflict is ongoing or is likely to be ongoing. These areas may include child labour, sexual violence, the disappearance of people, forced resettlement and the destruction of ritually or culturally significant sites.

Summary 57:
EU Customs Code — modernisation of the EU’s customs legislation (recast)
EU Customs code — modernising the EU's customs legislation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 952/2013 laying down the EU Customs Code
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the modernisation and harmonisation of customs legislation in the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 450/2008.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to goods originating in the EU.
It sets out rules on:
the registration of economic operators and of other persons;
the technical arrangements for developing, maintaining and employing electronic systems for the exchange of information between EU countries’ customs authorities and economic operators;
common risk criteria and standards, control measures and priority control areas;
customs controls and formalities at ports or airports where customs controls are to be carried out on cabin and hold baggage;
rules on the uniform management of tariff quota and tariff ceilings;
management of tariff surveillance of the management of the export of goods;
procedures to determine the tariff classification of goods and to grant or prohibit the free circulation of goods of a specific origin;
preventing the temporary release of goods from the EU for a limited period of time;
providing a guarantee for the temporary suspension of the importation of goods for a period of up to 30 days;
suspending the import of certain goods for an indefinite period for a maximum of 30 days.
EU countries may also:
suspend the import and export of certain types of goods if they consider that they have not complied with the requirements of the regulation;
ban the import or export of some goods for more than 30 days if they believe that they do not comply with its requirements;
impose a ban on the import, export or release of certain categories of goods, such as motor vehicles;
introduce a temporary suspension for goods of more than 10 days for goods imported for a specified period;
require EU countries to notify the European Commission of any decision relating to the application of the customs legislation.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 TFEU to supplement or amend certain non-essential elements of this regulation. These include:
adopting decisions allowing one or several countries to use means of exchange and storage of information other than electronic data-processing techniques;
designating the customs authority which is responsible for the registration of the economic operator and for the establishment of a customs representative to provide services in an EU country other than the one where he or she is established;
setting out the procedural rules on the conferral and proving of the entitlement for a customs representatives to provide service in a Member State other than that of the Member State where he/she is established and on the submission and acceptance of an application for a favourable decision;
taking and monitoring of favourable decisions;
using the use of a decision which ceases to be valid or is revoked;
requesting Member States to revoke decisions relating to binding information;
drawing up delegated acts to ensure uniform application of customs controls, including the exchange and analysis of risk information and analysis, and the sharing of information which can be made by means other than e-data processing techniques.
Amendments to the regulation
Implementing powers
The European Commission has the power to:
implement delegated acts on behalf of the European Parliament and of the Council;
adopt delegated acts for the purpose of supplementing or amending delegated acts;
set out the modalities for the application by EU countries of the criteria for the granting of the status of authorised economic operator;
define the technical arrangements to develop, maintain and employ electronic systems;
specify the format and code of the common data requirements for the collection of information and the storage of such information.
Implementation of delegated acts
The implementing powers are conferred on the Commission in order:
to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation of the implementing powers;
to set up a system for the transmission of relevant documents to the EU countries and to ensure a simultaneous, timely and appropriate transmission of the relevant documents; and
to adopt decisions allowing the Commission to use a means of exchanging and storing the information it receives from EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The new regulation has applied since 10 October 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU Customs code is a modernised version of the original EU customs legislation, which had to become law in the Member States by 9 October 2008.
For more information, see:
Customs Code (European Commission).

    The European Union — Modernisation of Customs Legislation (European Agency for Safety and Health at European Union borders) (European External Action Service).
