Summary 58:
Customs enforcement of intellectual property rights — customs action against goods suspected of infringing certain rights (Regulation (EU) No 608/2013)
Importers must ensure that goods they place on the EU market are protected by the right-holder’s intellectual property (IP) rights when they are imported into the EU.
Customs authorities must be able to enforce these rights at the border, wherever the goods are, or should have been, under customs supervision or customs control, and to carry out adequate controls on such goods with a view to preventing operations in breach of IP rights.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules on how EU countries’ customs authorities can take action to prevent goods from being placed on the market in the EU if they suspect that they are infringing intellectual property.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1383/2003.
KEY POINTS
The goods covered by the regulation are:
goods that have been manufactured with the consent of the manufacturer or the user of a trade name, but which have been placed on sale without his or her consent without his/her consent or without the authorisation of a legitimate manufacturer or user;
designs and utility models and devices that are primarily designed, produced or adapted for the purpose of enabling or facilitating the circumvention of technological measures;
topographies of semiconductor products and topographies of utility models or devices which are used to enable or facilitate the circumventions of technical measures.
devices that are designed, manufactured or adapted to facilitate the use of a technological measure.
The regulation does not apply to goods that are intended for the manufacture of which the manufacturer has the right to authorise the manufacture.
EU countries must also take the necessary measures to ensure that:
the goods are seized by customs authorities when they suspect they have infringed the rights of the manufacturers or users of the goods;
the import of goods is suspended or the goods seized are detained for at least 3 days;
they must inform the European Commission of the reasons for the suspension or the detention of a goods seized.
If a goods is seized, the goods must be returned to the manufacturer, the user or the legitimate manufacturer. The goods may not be used for commercial purposes and the goods may only be sold for personal use.
Where the release of goods from the customs control is suspended, the right holder may bring a legal action against the importer. The right holder must be informed of the nature of the action and the duration of the legal proceedings. If the goods remain under the control of the customs authorities for more than 3 days, they may be detained for a further 3 days.
In the event that the goods do not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation, the EU country in which the goods were seized must notify the Commission and the other EU countries of the reason for the detention.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
Regulation(EU) 2018/1139 has applied since 1 July 2018 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been working on a comprehensive European anti-counterfeiting and anti-piracy plan for the period 2014-2020.
For more information, see:
‘Counterfeiting in the European Union’ on the Commission's website.

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 59:
Customs programme for cooperation in the field of customs — 2021-2022
Customs program for cooperation at EU level
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/444 — establishing the Customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Customs 2020 programme to support EU countries’ customs authorities in their efforts to enhance cooperation in order to facilitate legitimate and fair cross-border trade.
It aims to support the EU’s external aspects of the internal market, the common commercial policy and the other common EU policies having a bearing on trade, and to overall supply chain security.
KEY POINTS
   
The programme aims to:
facilitate and enhance customs cooperation between EU countries and the customs authorities;
ensure that businesses and consumers benefit from the benefits of this cooperation;
protect the legitimate and economic interests of the EU and its citizens against terrorist, health, environmental and other threats;
provide support for the wider mission of customs authorities, as provided for in Article 3 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
The EU countries are responsible for setting up the programme and the European Commission is responsible for implementing it. The programme is funded by the EU budget and is implemented by the Commission.
The European Commission, assisted by the European Parliament and the Council, manages the programme. The EU countries have the right to opt out of the programme if they do not wish to do so. However, they may decide to participate in the following ways:
contribute to the programme by:
:
providing the EU countries with technical assistance;
contributing to the establishment of a customs information exchange system;
supporting the development of customs information systems and the exchange of best practice;
encouraging the use of information and communication technologies (ICTs) to improve the efficiency of customs procedures;
developing a customs data exchange system for the prevention and detection of fraud;
promoting cooperation between customs authorities and businesses in the fight against counterfeiting, counterfeiting and smuggling of goods;
facilitating cooperation between the EU country concerned and the national customs authorities of the other EU countries.
Implementing the programme
EU countries must ensure that:
customs authorities cooperate with each other in the areas covered by the programme, such as:
information exchange and exchange of information;
the exchange of data;
coordination of information on customs procedures, procedures and systems;
training of customs staff;
cooperation in the fields of information technology (IT) and digitalisation;
sharing of best practices;
exchange of experience and best practice in the area of customs cooperation.
In addition, EU countries must provide the Commission with information on:
the activities of EU countries in relation to the implementation of the customs cooperation programme;
measures to be taken by EU countries to ensure that the programme is implemented in line with the objectives of the Customs policy;
how the programme will be implemented;
information and communication technology (ICT) and data exchange between the Commission and EU countries;
implementation of the common EU customs policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 15 March 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 15 April 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  The customs union has been one of the cornerstones of the Union for over 50 years and is essential for the proper functioning of its internal market for the benefit of both businesses and citizens. It is therefore appropriate, efficient and in the interest of the union to continue financing activities in this field by establishing a new programme.
For more information, see:
‘Customs cooperation’ on the EU's website.

Summary 60:
European Social Fund Plus (ESF+)
European social fund plus
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1057 establishing the European Social Fund plus
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s social investment fund, the ESF+, which supports investments in people and systems in the policy areas of employment, education and social inclusion, thereby supporting economic, territorial and social cohesion in accordance with Article 174 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESF+ supports the implementation of key EU initiatives and activities, in particular the European Pillar of Social Rights (Pillar) and the InvestEU programme. It is funded by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and Cohesion Fund, the specific objectives and scope of support of which are set out in the implementing acts of the ERDF and InvestEU programmes. It also supports the European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund (EMFAF), the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and, where appropriate, the European Rural Development Fund.
It supports the actions of the Member States in the areas of education, training and the fight against social exclusion, poverty and discrimination. It does not provide financial support for specific projects.
EU countries must ensure that their national investment strategies are aligned with the objectives of the Pillar. They must also take into account the relevant national guidelines and relevant country-specific recommendations adopted by the Council of Europe and the European Commission.
The European Semester for economic policy coordination is the framework to identify national reform priorities and monitor their implementation. The European Semesters for economic and social policy coordination are the basis for the annual European investment strategies. They are presented alongside the yearly national reform programmes in order to outline and coordinate priority investment projects to be supported by EU or national funding.
Funding
The EU countries must use EU funding in a coherent manner and to maximise the added value of the financial support to be received, particularly from the programmes supported by the EU under, where relevant, the regional development funds and the investEU programme, and where appropriate from the Regional Development Funds.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘The European Pillar’ on the European Parliament's website.

Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps (ESC) 2017-2021 — volunteering for the EU’s humanitarian aid programme
European solidarity corps (ESCs) 2017–2021 – volunteering for for the European humanitarian aid program
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/888 establishing the European Solidarity Corp Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the ESC, which aims to provide opportunities for young people across the EU to make a meaningful contribution to society, show solidarity and develop their skills, enabling them to obtain not only work experience but also an invaluable human experience.
KEY POINTS
The ESC aims to:
provide young people with opportunities to participate in volunteering activities to support the EU's humanitarian aid programmes;
raise awareness of the need for humanitarian aid in the EU;
promote solidarity and the visibility of humanitarian aid among EU citizens;
support the work of the United Nations (UN) Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs (UNOCHA) in promoting a coherent international response to humanitarian crises;
contribute to an adequate humanitarian response that strengthens the gender perspective in EU humanitarian aid policy and promotes adequate humanitarian responses to the specific needs of women and men of all ages.
ESCs are funded by the European Union (EU), the European Commission, the European Development Fund (EDF) and the European Social Fund (ESF). They are open to all EU citizens, regardless of their age, gender, nationality or political affiliation. The ESCs have a maximum duration of 3 years, renewable once. They are renewable once and can be terminated at the end of their 3-year renewable period.
The European Commission is responsible for the organisation of the ECCs and for the selection of the national coordinators for each ESC. They work closely with the European Parliament and the Council of the EU. They report to the Commission every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation and the results of their work.
Each ESC has a budget of €500,000 for the 2017-2020 period. The budget for the 2018-2022 period is €250,000 and the 2019-2024 period is up to €400,000.
In addition, the EACs receive financial support from the European Investment Bank (EIB). The EICs are responsible for:
setting up the EOCs;
providing financial support for the ELCs’ activities;
coordinating the EIC activities with the EIB and the EIF;
financing the activities of the European Fund for Strategic Cooperation (EFCS) and other EFCS partners;
assisting the EFCs in the execution of their activities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 20 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘The ECC’ is one of the three pillars of the Union's external action. The other two pillars are solidarity and sustainable development.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union and solidarity’ on the EEC's website.

European Globalisation Adjustment Fund for Displaced Workers (EGF) — 2021-2027 (European Pillar of Social Rights) — European Globalisation adjustment fund for displaced workers (EAGF)
European Globalization Adjustment Funds for displaced Workers (EIGF) for 2021-2020 (European Social Fund)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/691 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 April 2021 on the European globalisation adjustmentment fund for displaced workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EIGF, a multiannual financial framework for the European Union (EU), to help displaced workers in the event of a major restructuring of the global economy.
It sets up the European Social Fund (ESF), which is the EU’s main source of financial support for displaced people.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESF is designed to support displaced workers who have been displaced by the globalisation of the world economy or by the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic in the context of the Paris Agreement on Climate Change (see summary). It aims to help these displaced workers to return to their country of origin or to live in another EU country where they can work or study. It is funded by the EU budget.
EU countries are responsible for setting up the ESF, which is managed by the European Commission. The ESF has a budget of €2.5 billion for the period from 2021 to 2027.
Eligibility for EU countries is based on their gross domestic product (GDP) per year.
.
The EGF is open to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are excluded from the scope of the EGF.
Funds are allocated according to the following criteria:
gross domestic product
GDP per capita
EU GDP per capita.
Sustainable development
The EU budget is balanced across the three dimensions of sustainable development:
economic, social and environmental;
environmental;
health and well-being;
human rights.
In addition, the EFG also supports the European Pillar of social rights (EPSR), which aims to promote equal opportunities and access to the labour market and to combat discrimination based on sex, racial or ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation. The EGF supports the EPSR by supporting the implementation of the Pillar and the ESSR.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 April 2021.
BACKGROUND

equal opportunities for men and women;
fair working conditions;
social protection and inclusion.

Summary 63:
A mandatory transparency register for interest representatives in EU policy-making and policy implementation — EU countries’ permanent representations
An agreement between the European Parliament, the Council of the European Union and the European Commission on a voluntary transparency register.
A voluntary transparency registry for EU countries' permanent representations.
SUMMARY OF:
Interinstitutional agreement on a compulsory transparency register
Decision (EU) 2020/852 — conclusion of the Interinstitutional Agreement between the EU countries and the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies on the implementation of the EU’s transparency register (the ‘Transparency Register’)
Interstitutional agreement of 20 May 2021 on a joint framework for their cooperation on a transparency register on a mandatory transparency list
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that interest representatives (representatives of organisations and self-employed individuals who have an interest in EU policies, programmes or activities) are registered in the EU's transparency register and that the information they provide on their activities is made available to the public.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of EU countries.
KEY POINTS
   
The European Parliament (European Parliament) and the Council (European Union (European Council) are committed to maintaining an open, transparent and regular dialogue with representative associations and civil society in accordance with Article 11 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU), and in particular Article 11(1) and (2) of the TEU. This dialogue enables stakeholders to present their views on decisions that may affect them and hence to contribute effectively to the evidence base on which policy proposals are made.
Activities of public authorities, as well as of any association or network of such public authorities that acts on their behalf at EU, national or subnational level, should not be covered by this agreement, although associations and networks of public bodies that engage in interest representation activities should be allowed to register. However, the signatory institutions recognise the importance of coordinating their approach through the adoption of a joint transparency register in order to further promote interest representation that is transparent and ethical.
This agreement sets out the rules and principles to be observed by interest representatives that sign up to the register. It also establishes a code of conduct to be followed by all interest representatives.
In order to encourage interest representatives to register on the transparency register, each EU country must publish on its website a list of all the information it receives from interest representatives on its territory. It must also publish on the website of the register conditionality and complementary transparency measures that they put in place to encourage registration, such as dedicated mailing lists, the recommendation that certain decision-makers meet only registered interest representatives, or the publication of information on meetings between certain EU officials.
Each EU country is responsible for ensuring that the register is up-to-date and that information on the activities of interest representatives is made publicly available. In addition, it must publish a report on the register every 3 years on the progress made by the register and on the actions taken by the signatories to implement the agreement.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS ENTER INTO FORCE?
They entered into force on 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  The EU has adopted a number of transparency agreements with non-EU countries over the past few years, including the 2014 agreement on interest representation (see summary).
For more information, see:
Transparency and accountability (European Commission).

Summary 65:
European Union Recovery Instrument to support the recovery in the aftermath of the COVID-19 crisis
European Union Relief Instrument for the Recovery of the EU’s Gross Domestic Product (EURIM) in the wake of the CoVID19 pandemic
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2094 establishing a European Union recovery instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU recovery instrument to provide financial support for the recovery of the economy of the European Union (EU), which was affected by the Covenantero-Venezuela virus (COVID) in 2020.
It aims to provide support to businesses affected by COVID, in particular small and medium-sized enterprises, as well as support for investment in activities that are essential for strengthening sustainable growth in the EU including direct financial investment in enterprises, measures for research and innovation in response to COVID and measures for capacity building at EU level to enhance future crisis preparedness, for maintaining efforts to ensure a just transition to a climate-neutral economy, and support for agriculture and development in rural areas.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up the EU Recovery Instrument (ERI) for the period 2020-2023.
The ERI is a multiannual financial instrument designed to support EU countries in their efforts to recover from the effects of COVID. It aims to:
recover the economic and social damage caused by the crisis;
reinforce cohesion among EU countries;
support the efforts of EU countries to restore labour markets and social protection;
provide support for social inclusion and repair the immediate damage to businesses;
facilitate the transition towards a green and digital economy;
encourage investment in sustainable growth and jobs.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMEMERCE INITI APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 14 December 2020 and had to become law in the Member States by 14 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
The COVID crisis was declared a pandemic by the World Health Organization (WHO) on 11 March 2020. Since then, EU countries have adopted a set of unprecedented measures to contain the spread of the virus. These measures have already led or will lead to severe deterioration of the financial situation of many businesses in the Union. In addition, the provision of many services has become very difficult or impossible.
For more information, see:
‘Covenanters’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 66:
Budgetary surveillance in the euro area
Budgetarian oversight
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1173/2011 on the effective enforcement of budgetary surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the effectiveness of the EU’s budgetary surveillance system by strengthening the role of the European Commission and the European Parliament and the national parliaments.
It sets out the rules for the enforcement of EU countries’ budgetary surveillance.
KEY POINTS
EU countries whose currency is the euro have a particular interest in and a responsibility to conduct economic policies that promote the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union and to avoid policies that jeopardise that functioning.
To achieve this, the EU has adopted a number of measures to strengthen economic governance. These include:
the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP), which aims to prevent and correct excessive government deficits;
the European Semester for strengthened coordination of economic and budgetary policies;
an effective framework for preventing and correcting excessive government deficit;
minimum requirements for national budgetary frameworks;
enhanced financial market regulation and supervision, including macroprudential supervision by the European Systemic Risk Board.
The European Commission plays a stronger role in the enhanced surveillance procedure as regards assessments that are specific to each EU country, monitoring, on-site missions, recommendations and warnings.
An economic dialogue between the Commission and EU countries may be established, enabling the Commission to make its analyses public and the President of the Council, the European Council and, where appropriate, the president of the Eurogroup to discuss.
. The Commission may also invite the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) to participate in an economic dialogue.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 23 November 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 23 November 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the SGP and the other measures of the ESM. The ESM is the cornerstone of the new EU economic governance framework.
For more information, see:
‘The ESM’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 67:
Population and housing censuses — common European Union (EU) data — common EU rules
Population and Housing censuses – common EU data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 763/2008 on population and housing surveys
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the collection and compilation of comparable and comprehensive EU-wide data on the population and the main family, social, economic and housing characteristics of persons in order to enable the European Commission (Eurostat) to fulfil the tasks assigned to it, in particular by Articles 2 and 3 of the Treaty.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are responsible for their own data collection.
EU countries must collect and compile data on:
the total population of the EU and the total number of people living in the EU;
the number of persons living in each EU country and the number of households in each of those households;
a description of the main characteristics of the population, the main families, social and economic characteristics of each household and the housing characteristics.
The data collected are used for the purposes of:
monitoring the development of social cohesion at regional and local level;
assessing the impact of EU policies on social inclusion and the environment;
developing the EU’s social and environmental policies;
providing the basis for the development and implementation of the European Pillar of Social Rights (see summary);
European Pillar of Human Rights (See summary).
The EU countries must ensure that the data collected by the EU countries are comparable and comparable to those collected by Eurostat at national level.
Each year, the EU country sends data to Eurostat, which then transmits the data to the European Statistical Office (ESO) in Brussels.
Eurostat receives the data subject to statistical confidentiality. The data are transmitted to the ESO by the national and EU statistical authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
It has applied since 13 August 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 August 2011.
BACKGROUND
  The European Commission is responsible for collecting and compiling the data for the European population and its main characteristics. It is assisted by the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESDP).
For more information, see:
‘The European Statistics code of practice’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Community statistics: EU countries’ statistics on the total population and their main characteristics, as defined in the ESDP. They are used to develop the EU's social cohesion policy and the environmental policies that affect the EU. They also provide information on the economic and social development of EU countries and the social cohesion of their regions.
ACT
  EU Parliament and of the Council of 9 July 2008 on a common European statistical framework for the implementation of population and population censuses (OJ L 218, 13.8.2008, pp. 14-18)

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) — rules of procedure for the European Union institutions, bodies, offices and agencies
European data protection supervisors (EDPs) — details of the EU institution, body, office or agency responsible for the protection of personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 — European data protection supervisor
Decision of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 May 2020 adopting the Rules of Procedure of the EDPS
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
   
The regulation sets up an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of their personal data, are respected by the EU institutions and bodies responsible for protecting their data.
The decision sets out the rules and procedures for the appointment of an EDPS.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EDPS is responsible for:
ensuring that:
the protection of the personal data of EU citizens and their families is respected by EU institutions*, bodies and offices, including the European Commission, the European Data Protection Board (EDPB) and the European Agency for Fundamental Rights (EAFR);
protecting the rights of individuals with regard to the free movement of their data, including by ensuring that their rights are respected when they leave the EU and when they move to another EU country;
monitoring the data processing of EU institutions, body or office;
collecting, analysing and analysing personal data;
checking that the processing is in compliance with the relevant EU legislation;
ensure that the data are protected;
providing the competent authorities with the necessary information to make a decision on the processing and storage of personal information;
assisting the EDPB and the EAFR in its work.
Duties and powers
The EU institutions or bodies covered by the regulation are subject to the duties and powers set out in Article 53 of the regulation. The EDPS appoints an independent EDPS, who is appointed by the EEFR and is assisted by a Secretariat. The Secretariat is under the control of the EDFR. The Directorate-General Secretariat:
is responsible for coordinating the work of the Secretariat;
has the power to:
draw up and implement the annual report on the implementation of EU data protection law;
make recommendations to the EFPB on data protection policy;
act as a consultative body for EU institutions in the field of data protection;
publish a report every 2 years on the results of its work;
report to the Council every 3 years on its work and on the progress it has made in implementing the EU’s data protection laws.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Regulations have applied since 23 October 2018 and had to become law in the EU countries by 23 October 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has been governed by a number of legal acts since 1 January 2020.
For more information, see:
EU data protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* European data Protection Supervisor: an independent EU authority that ensures that the right to personal data protection is respected.
* EU institution: an EU institution or body that is subject to EU law, such as the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) or any other EU legal act providing for tasks and powers for the EU's data protection supervisory authority.
ACT
  The European Union established an independent body, referred to as the EuropeanData Protection Supervisor, on 1 January 2019. It is an independent European Union body responsible for enforcing the rights and fundamental freedoms of the natural persons of the Union and for ensuring the right of access to data. It appoints a director-general to carry out the duties of the office.

Summary 70:
System of own resources of the European Union
System of its own resources
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020/2053 on the system of EU’s own resources and repealing Decision 2014/335/EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets up a new system for calculating EU countries’ contributions to the general budget of the EU (‘the EU budget’) based on the quantity of plastic packaging waste that is not recycled in each EU country.
It aims to encourage EU countries to reduce the consumption of single-use plastics, foster recycling and boost the circular economy.
KEY POINTS
The new own resource is based on national contributions that are proportional to the amount of non-recycled plastic packaging that does not get recycled in EU countries. The reduction should correspond to 3,8 kilograms multiplied by the population of the Member States concerned.
The European Commission will put forward a proposal to the European Parliament and the Council for the introduction of the own resource by 1 January 2021. The Council will then decide whether to extend the period of implementation until 31 December 2023.
EU countries will be free to take the most suitable measures to achieve those goals, in line with the principle of subsidiarity. The Commission will also be able to set up a mechanism for the adjustment of the contributions of Member States with a GNI per capita in 2017 below the EU average.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 15 December 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 15 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU budget is the main source of revenue for the EU budget. It is funded by the EU countries and the European Investment Bank.
For more information, see:
‘Own resources’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 71:
Labelling of tyres with respect to fuel efficiency and other parameters — EU rules on the labelling of tyre tyres (EU) 2020/740 (see summary)
Labelling on tyres with regard to fuel consumption, safety and noise (EU rules on tyre labelling)
The European Union is committed to building an Energy Union with a forward-looking climate policy. To reduce the environmental impact of road transport, it is therefore appropriate to update the provisions on the label of tyres to encourage end-users to purchase more fuel‐efficient tyres by providing them with harmonised information on the rolling resistance parameter.
ACT
Regulation (EU] 2020/730 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 25 May 2020 on the EU’s directive on the labels of tyres, amending Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down minimum requirements for the external rolling noise of tyres.
It aims to encourage consumers to buy tyres with a lower external noise level by providing information on their tyres’ fuel efficiency* and their wet grip*.
It also aims to improve the safety of road users by requiring tyre manufacturers to provide information on tyre safety.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all tyres:
C1 tyres, C2 tyres and C3 tyres;
Tyres with a wet grip rating of 0.5 m/s (m/s).
It does not apply to tyres designed for use on the roads of the Schengen area (e.g. motorways).
Road users must have the option to choose tyres that do not exceed 0.2 m/spaces between 0.
The legislation does not affect the requirements for tyres designed to be used on public roads.
National authorities must ensure that tyres are labelled with the following information:
the rolling resistance of the tyre,
the wet grip of the tyres and
the road noise level.
EU countries must also ensure that tyre manufacturers provide the European Commission with a list of tyres that comply with the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 July 2020 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 			
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce the emissions of greenhouse gases (GHG) from the transport sector. These include:
introducing a new directive to reduce GHG emissions from road transport by 20 % by 2020;
introduced a new regulation to reduce greenhouse gas emissions from the road transport sector by 30 % by 2030.
For more information, see:
Fuel efficiency and road noise (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Tyre: a rubber with a rolling resistance rating of at least 1.5 mm/s.
* Wet grip rating: the amount of grip a tyre has on the outside of the outer edge of the rim of the wheel.

Summary 72:
Integrated Border Management Fund — funding for customs control equipment (IBMF)
Integrative Border Management Facility — funding to improve the performance of customs controls across the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1077 — establishing an instrument for financial support for customs controls equipment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide fund to support EU countries’ efforts to ensure the efficient and effective operation of the EU’s external borders. It aims to improve equivalence in the performance by EU countries of their customs control systems and to prevent the diversion of flows of goods towards the weakest points in the customs control system, often referred to as ‘import point shopping’.
KEY POINTS
The Integrated Border Management Framework (IBF) is an instrument designed to provide the EU countries with the necessary technical support for the operation of their national border control systems. It is funded by the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU).
It aims to:
ensure that the competent authorities of EU countries have the necessary resources to carry out the necessary customs control tasks;
facilitate legitimate trade with non-EU countries;
provide the necessary training for customs authorities and other law enforcement authorities;
support the development of new customs control technologies;
improve cooperation between EU countries on border control;
encourage the exchange of information and best practice;
increase cooperation between customs authorities.
The IBFinance for the IBMF is managed by the Commission, the European Investment Bank for Europe (EIBF), the European Economic and Social Fund (EFSF) and the EIBF.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2023.
BACKGROUND
The EU is one of the largest trading blocks in the world. Since the customs union is essential for the proper functioning of the internal market, and for businesses and citizens to benefit from it, continuous steps to strengthen the EU's external borders are needed.
For more information, see:
‘Customs control equipment’ on the IBF's website.

Summary 73:
Common rules for the internal market for electricity — common rules for electricity and amending Directives 2012/27/EU and 2009/72/EC (recast) (Directive (EU) 2019/944 on common rules on the EU’s electricity market
Common rules on electricity markets
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It sets out common rules to ensure that electricity consumers have access to competitive electricity markets across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The directive aims to:
ensure that electricity markets are open to all market participants, including producers of renewable energy, new energy service providers, energy storage and flexible demand.
It also aims to improve the security of supply and sustainability of electricity in the EU by ensuring that consumers have the choice to buy or sell electricity on the open market.
EU countries must ensure that:
the wholesale electricity market is open to renewable energy producers and energy storage providers, as well as energy distributors;
there is a level playing field for all market players in the electricity market;
market participants have the right to participate in the decision-making process of the wholesale market and to participate actively in the retail market, in particular by participating in the setting of prices;
the European Commission is responsible for setting up a European Energy Agency (EEA) to manage the EEA.
The EEA plays a key role in the energy market and ensures that consumers can benefit from the benefits of a competitive electricity market. The EEA also plays an active role in ensuring that is transparent, fair and transparent.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 14 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission launched a public consultation process on a new energy market design in July 2015. The Commission Communication of 15 July 2015, entitled ‘Delivering a New Deal for Energy Consumers’, put forward the Commission's vision for a retail market that better serves energy consumers, including by better linking wholesale and retail markets.

Summary 74:
European High Performance Computing Joint Undertaking (EHCJoint undertaking)
European High-Performance Computing Joint undertaking (EHPCJ)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1173 on establishing the European High-performance computing joint undertaking
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EHCJ Joint Undertakings (EHJCJ) to support the creation of European public common data spaces and federated, secure cloud infrastructures for public, industrial and scientific applications.
It sets up the EHJCJ under the framework of Horizon Europe, the European Union’s framework programme for research and innovation (‘Horizon Europe’).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The HHCJ supports projects of common interest in the following areas:
building and deploying cutting-edge joint digital capacities, including in supercomputing and quantum computing;
expanding the use of supercomputers to develop solutions for medicine, transport and the environment;
improving the availability of high performance computing services and the development of key skills.
Funding
The funding is provided by the European Commission through the European Partnerships for Horizon Europe (EPSI) programme. The EHJ is funded by Horizon Europe and the Connecting Europe Facility (see summary).
EHSJ is a joint undertaking established under the Horizon Europe programme. It is responsible for:
providing financial support for the development and operation of a world-class supercomputer, quantum and data infrastructure;
developing and deploying a network of high-intensive computing resources;
supporting research and development in the field of industrial and digital technologies;
creating a European high-performance data centre for the next generation of communication technologies, such as the Internet of Things (IoT) and the internet of things (i.e. IoT) for the future of information and communication technologies (ICTs);
facilitating the development, deployment and use of a global network of data centres for the processing of data;
promoting the sharing of best practices and best practices in the information society.
EHJoint Undertaking
The European Commission has the exclusive right to appoint the chairperson responsible for administering the HJCJ. The chairperson is appointed by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament and the Council of the EU.
The chairperson of the EJCJ is assisted by a director responsible for the implementation of the work of the joint undertaking. The Chairperson may also be assisted by the Director-General of the Commission.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The EHC Joint undertaking entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND

Summary 75:
Climate neutrality — EU framework for achieving climate neutrality (2020-2030)
Climate neutrality – EU framework to achieve climate neutrality
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1119 establishing the EU’s climate neutrality framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU is a climate-neutral economy by 2020.
It sets out a framework for reducing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from the energy sector and other sectors to achieve this goal.
KEY POINTS
The regulation:
sets out the rules for reducing GHG emissions from energy, transport, agriculture, forestry, fishing and aquaculture, as well as from non-fossil fuels, such as coal, oil and gas, by 2020;
requires EU countries to reduce GHG emission from these sectors by at least 60 % by 2020, and by 80 % by 2050.
EU countries must also:
ensure that GHGs emissions from transport, forestry and fishing are reduced to a level that does not pose a threat to human health, the environment or the economy;
reduce GHGs from other sectors such as energy, agriculture and forestry;
encourage the development of low-carbon energy sources such as biofuels, renewable energy and bio-energy;
promote the use of renewable energy, including bio-fuels and bioenergy, to meet the energy needs of the future;
support the transition to a low carbon economy, including by reducing the CO2 emissions from power generation, transport and agriculture.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 9 July 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 July 2022.
BACKGROUND
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change and to delivering on the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
For more information, see:
‘Climate neutrality’ on the European Commission's website.
For further information about the European Climate Adaptation Platform (EC-ADAPT), see: ‘‘The European Green Deal’’.

Summary 76:
EU4Health programme for the period 2021-2027 — EU action in the field of health (‘EU4health’) for the 2021-2020 period (EU4-2020 programme)
EU4H programme for health
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/522 — establishing a Programme for the EU’s action in health (the EU4Health Programme)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a new and reinforced programme for EU action to combat the spread of severe human infections and diseases across borders in order to combat other serious cross-border threats to health and to safeguard and improve the health and well-being of all people in the European Union (EU).
It repeals Regulation (EU)* No 282/2014.
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to strengthen cooperation between EU countries in the prevention and control of serious cross border threats to human health. It aims to:
ensure a high level of human health protection in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic;
improve preparedness for, and the control of, severe human infection and disease threats across borders;
promote the exchange of information and best practice in the area of public health;
increase the capacity of EU countries to respond to health threats.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the programme.
EU countries are responsible for their health policies and for the organisation, management and delivery of health services and medical care. The programme is funded by the European Commission.
It is implemented by the EU budget.
.
Scope
The EU4 health action covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. It covers:
preventing and controlling severe human infectious diseases;
improving the protection of the health of EU citizens;
providing assistance to EU countries that have been affected by a serious public health threat;
developing and implementing measures to reduce the impact of health threats;
supporting the development of EU-wide information and communication systems;
encouraging the sharing of best practice and best practices in the areas covered by the programme;
raising awareness of the risks associated with public health threats and the need to prepare for and respond to them.
Coordination of actions
The Commission will coordinate the actions of the EU countries under the programme by:
coordinating the activities of the relevant national authorities;
sharing best practice;
exchanging best practices;
facilitating the exchange and sharing of information;
creating a network of national experts to share best practice on the prevention, control and management of severe public health risks.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Еъларска барка  гракова глакса  
It has applied since 26 March 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 March 2023.
BACKGROUND
The COVID outbreak was the first outbreak of the novel coronavirus (COVID) in the history of the World Health Organization (WHO). It was declared a global pandemic on 11 March 2020. It caused severe socio-economic consequences and human suffering.

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine — definition, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of aromatised wines
Aroma-scented wines — definition and labelling
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 251/2014 — definitions, description and presentation of aromats and the protection of the geographical indications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules applicable to aromatisation of wine by means of the labelling, presentation and description of certain aromats, as well as to protecting the geographical indication (GIs) of certain aromatic wine products.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1601/91.
KEY POINTS
 
Scope
The regulation applies to all aromated wine products placed on the market in the EU, whether produced in the Member States or in non-EU countries or in third countries. It does not apply to aromated wines produced for export.
The products covered by the regulation must meet the following criteria:
be produced in accordance with the rules laid down by the International Wine Organisation (IWO);
meet the standards set out in the annex to the regulation;
not be produced in a way that is likely to mislead consumers;
be made in a manner that does not compromise the quality of the wine;
have been produced using traditional production methods.
Labelling
The labelling of the products must contain the following information:
the name of the country of origin of the producer;
the production method used;
a description of the product;
an indication of the GIs;
where the product is produced;
how the product has been produced and marketed.
An indication of a GIs must be attached to the product. The indication must be accompanied by a photograph of the grapes used and a description of how the product was produced.
Directive 2014/13/EU — the European Commission is responsible for ensuring that GIs are applied to all products produced for sale in the European Union (EU).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE?
   
  —
  бараркаканарскахракка  ххлаки   грански (‘aromatisation’) is a term used to describe the production of wine with aromatising properties. It refers to the wine’s colour, aroma, flavour, texture and texture. It is used to indicate that the wine has been made from grapes that have been treated with aromatics, such as botanicals, in order to enhance the aromatics of the wines.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of rules to regulate the production and marketing of aromatic wines. These include:
rules on the definition of the term ‘aroma’,
rules for the description of aromatics and the presentation of the aromatisations;
rules to protect the geographical identification of the gIs of certain products, and
rules concerning the protection for GIs.
For more information, see:
‘Aromatic wines’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 80:
Fishing opportunities for certain fish stocks and groups of fish stocks, applicable in EU waters and, for EU fishing vessels, in certain non-Union waters
Fishing options for certain species and groups thereof, applying in EU and non-EU waters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/123 fixing for 2020 the fishing opportunities for fish stocks* and groups* in the EU and in certain other waters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the total allowable catch (TAC) for each species and group of fish in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The TAC is calculated on the basis of available scientific advice, taking into account biological and socio-economic aspects whilst ensuring fair treatment between fishing sectors, as well as in the light of the opinions expressed during the consultation of stakeholders, in particular at the meetings of the advisory councils.
EU countries must ensure that:
the TACs are set at the maximum yield (MS) of the fish stock or group for which they have an obligation to land all catches, including by-catches from those stocks, in mixed fisheries;
fishing opportunities are fixed in such a way as to ensure relative stability of fishing activities of each EU country for each fish stock and fishery;
the fishing opportunities are based on the advice figure for total catches, not on the wanted catches (i.e. the amount of fish to be caught by a fishing vessel).
The EU countries must:
ensure that the TAC for all species is set at a maximum yield of at least 13 % of the MS;
set the maximum allowable catch for all groups of species at at least 16 % of MS.
Fisheries must avoid discarding fish in order to avoid the phenomenon of ‘choke species’, which occurs when a fish is caught but dies before it can be eaten.
The fishing opportunities must be set in accordance with the objectives of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) set out in Article 2(2) of that regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
The CFP is the EU’s fisheries policy. It aims to protect the marine environment and the economic interests of the fishing industry.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing Opportunities’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Choke species: fish that die before they are eaten by other fish.
* Mixed fishery: a fishery in which fish are caught by more than one fishing vessel in the same area.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) 2018/852 of 26 November 2018 fixing for 2019 the fishing quotas for certain molluscs and crustaceans in the waters of the European Economic Area (EEA) (see summary) and for certain other fish stocks in the exclusive economic zone (EEZ) (See summary).

MEDIAN swordfish— ICCAT 2017–2031
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1154 — Mediterranean swordfish recovery plan
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the ICCAT (International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas) recovery plan.
It aims to restore and maintain populations of swordfish (Xiphias gladius) in the Mediterranean Sea, which has been overfished over the last 30 years.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The plan covers the period from 1 January 2017 to 31 March 2031.
EU countries must ensure that the fishery is managed in accordance with the objectives of the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP) in order to ensure that fishery resources are managed in line with the best available scientific advice so as to ensure sustainable economic, environmental and social conditions.
The management of the Mediterranean fishery must be based on the most up-to-date scientific and technical information available to the Commission.
Fisheries must be managed in a way that minimises the impact of fishing on the marine environment and human health.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework for the years 2014-2020 (MFFs)
Multiannial financial framework (MFs) for the year 2014 - 2020 (MTFs)SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1311/2013 laying down the multiannual budgetary framework for 2014-20
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s budget for the financial year 2014 – 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The MFF covers the period from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020. It does not take account of budget items financed by assigned revenue within the meaning of Regulation (EC) No 332/2002 (2) and in Council Regulation (EU) No 407/2010 (3), while respecting the own-resources ceiling of the MFF.
The annual ceilings on commitments appropriations by category of expenditure and the annual ceiling on payment appropriations established by this regulation must respect the ceilings set for commitments and own resources in Council Decision 2007/436/EC,Euratom (the "Financial Regulation").
The regulation allows the EU to use special instruments to react to specified unforeseen circumstances or to allow the financing of clearly identified expenditure which cannot be financed within the limits of the ceilings available for one or more headings, thereby facilitating the budgetary procedure: the Emergency Aid Reserve (EAR), the European Union Solidarity Fund, the Flexibility Instrument, the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGAF), the Contingency Margin, the specific flexibility to tackle youth unemployment and strengthen research and the global margin for commitments for growth and employment, in particular youth employment.
In case of unforeseen circumstances, the regulation may need to be revised in case of:
the EAR being suspended or reduced;
the balance of payment of the loans provided under the Balance of Payment Facility or the European Financial Stabilisation Mechanism (EFSM) being reduced; or
the implementation of new EU policies or programmes.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 20 January 2014.
BACKGROUND

‘The EU budget’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 84:
European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund (EMFAF) — EU maritime, fisheries and aquaculture fund (EMF) (European Union)
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EUFA) — EMF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1139 — establishing the European Maritime, fisheries, aquacultural and fisheries fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The EMFAF aims to support the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP), its maritime policy and its international commitments in the field of ocean governance.
It sets out the priorities of the EMF, its budget and the specific rules for providing EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the European Parliament and of the Council.
KEY POINTS
The EU is a global ocean actor and one of the world's largest producers of seafood, and therefore has a strong responsibility to protect, conserve and sustainably use the oceans and their resources. The EMFA supports the common EU fisheries policy, the EU's maritime policy, its maritime security policy and international commitments. It is also of socio-economic interest for the EU as a sustainable blue economy boosts investments, jobs and growth, fosters research and innovation and contributes to energy security through ocean energy. It also supports the growth of a sustainable ocean economy.
To achieve these objectives, the EMFAf supports:
fisheries and the conservation of marine biological resources;
food security through the supply of seafood products;
the growth of the blue economy;
healthy, safe, secure, clean and clean and sustainable managed seas and oceans
ably managed oceans
the protection of marine life
marine border control and maritime security
the fight against maritime crime.
EMFAFFs are managed by the European Commission under a partnership agreement with each EU country.
The partnership agreement is concluded with the EU country in which it is signed. It sets out common elements of strategic planning and programming, including provisions on the Partnership Agreement to be concluded with each Member State, and sets out a common approach to the performance orientation of the Funds.

Management, conservation and control measures applicable in the Inter-American Tropical Tuna Convention area
Migration and conservation of fish stocks in the Caribbean and the Pacific
Management and conservation measures applicable to fish caught in the Atlantic and Pacific Ocean
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/56 — management, conservation, control measures for tuna and tuna purse seine fishing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the management and control of fish caught by fishing vessels in the waters of the Caribbean, the Pacific Ocean and the Atlantic Ocean in accordance with the International Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish (ICMST) and the International Dolphin Conservation Programme (IDCP).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels operating in the area covered by the IATTC.
It does not apply to:
fishing vessels operating within the exclusive economic zone (EEZ) of the Atlantic, the Caribbean or the Pacific;
fisheries operating outside the EEZ, the Atlantic or Pacific Ocean;
unloading vessels operating on the Atlantic seabed;
seaworthiness of fishing vessels;
the use of fishing gear;
shark lines;
fish-aggregating devices (FADs);
the design and deployment of FADs;
scientific observer coverage of tuna seabirds seabird mortality records and data collection.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of all fishing activities.
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation by 1 January 2021.
National authorities must inform the Commission of:
the number and type of vessels and the number of vessels participating in the fishery;
a list of the countries participating in fishing activities;
details of the number and types of vessels involved in the fishing activities, including the number, type, type and number of fishing devices used;
information on the species and species of fish that are subject to fishing restrictions;
measures to reduce incidental dolphin mortality in the tuna purse-seine fishery to levels approaching zero.

Summary 86:
Better Law-Making — Interinstitutional Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission on Better Law-making
Better Law Making — InterInstitutional Agreement among the European Union (EU) institutions
SUMMARY OF:
INTERINSTITUTIONAL AGREEMENT BETWEEN THE EU PARLIAMENT, THE COUNCIL OF THE EUROPEAN UNION AND THE EUREAN COMMISSION on BETTER LAW-MAKING
Interinstitutional agreement on better law-making between the EU institutions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE INTERINSTITUTIONS?
The decision marks the conclusion of the Interinstitutional Agreement (IBA) between the three EU institutions — the EU Parliament, Council and European Commission — which aims to improve the quality of EU legislation and reduce the regulatory burden for citizens, administrations and businesses, especially small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
The aim of the IBA is to ensure that EU legislation is as simple and as clear as possible, avoids overregulation and administrative burdens for citizens and administrations, especially SMEs, and is designed with a view to facilitating its transposition and practical application and to strengthening the competitiveness and sustainability of the EU economy.
KEY POINTS
The IBA aims to:
ensure that legislation is based on the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality (i.e. that the legislation is designed to provide the greatest added value for European citizens and businesses);
promote transparency in the legislative process;
encourage the participation of the public in the decision-making process and in the drafting of the legislation;
facilitate the transposition of legislation in the EU;
provide the public with the opportunity to have their views and opinions heard;
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the legislative procedure by ensuring that delegated acts are drawn up in a way that is transparent and accessible to the public.
The three institutions agree to pursue the goals of simplifying EU legislation without prejudice to the achievement of the policy objectives of the Union, as specified in the Treaties, or to safeguarding the integrity of the internal market. In this context, they recall the equality of both co-legislators as enshrined in Article 5 of the Treaty on European Union.
In addition, the three institutions recognise their joint responsibility in delivering high-quality Union legislation and in ensuring that such legislation focuses on areas where it is as efficient and effective as possible in delivering the common policy objectives. They also agree to promote the transparency of the legal process and to encourage the involvement of citizens in drafting EU legislation.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND THE IBA APPLY?
Decision (EU, Euratom and the EU) 2017/852 concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Community (now the EU). The agreement entered into force on 1 May 2018.
Interstitutional agreement between the 3 institutions on better legal making
The agreement is one of a series of agreements and declarations which the three bodies have concluded in recent years. The other agreements are:
Joint Political Declaration of 27 October 2011 on exercising the role of national parliaments in the exercise of their powers and exercising their responsibilities in the implementation of the common foreign and security policy of the Community (Joint Declaration of 13 June 2007 on practical arrangements for the codecision procedure and the use of the recasting technique for legal acts);
Inter-institutional Framework Agreement of 20 December 1994 on the accelerated working method for official codification of legislative texts (Jurisdiction Agreement of 22 December 1998 on common guidelines for the quality drafting of Community legislation (JURDIC Agreement of 28 November 2001 on a more structured use of recasting techniques for legal act (JUIC Agreement) and
JUNIOR FRAMEWORK OF THE INTERinstITUTION ALGUMENTS
The agreements have been amended several times, most recently by the Joint Framework Agreement on Better Legal Making of the Common Foreign and Security Policy (JFSP) on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The 3 institutions are committed to sincere and transparent cooperation throughout the entire legislative cycle.
For more information, see:
Better law making (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Subsidiarity: the ability of a legislative act to be amended only where and to the extent necessary.
* Regulatory burden: the amount of administrative and financial burdens that a legal act imposes on a person or business.
MAIN DOCUMENTS —   
   The decision concludes the interinstitutional agreements between the representatives of the three European Union institutions on the basis of the principles set out in the treaties.
Council Decision (EU), Euratom, the European Economic and Social Committee (EEAS) and the Council of Europe on the conclusion on 1 April 2017 of the agreement to amend the InterInstitutions Agreement on better legislation (OJ L 123, 12.5.2016, pp. 1-2)




Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative — procedures and conditions for supporting a citizens’ proposal
Citizen’s initiative — rules and procedures for supporting an initiative
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 211/2011 — on the citizens' initiative
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for the support of an initiative by EU citizens.
It aims to encourage participation by citizens and to make the EU more accessible.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the rules for the implementation of a citizens initiative.
Citizens have the right to participate in the democratic life of the European Union by way of a European citizens initiative, provided that they do so with full transparency.
The initiative must be submitted to the European Commission by a natural person (organiser) from at least 7 different EU countries. The initiative must have at least 750 signatures from EU citizens from each EU country. The minimum number of signatories must correspond to the number of Members of the EU Parliament elected in each Member State, multiplied by 750. In order to ensure similar conditions for citizens to support an initiative, those minimum numbers should be degressively proportional.
EU countries must notify the Commission of any changes in the composition of their national parliaments. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of these changes.
Organisers must register with the Commission on their initiative's website. They must also provide the Commission with information and informal advice on the initiative's implementation.
If the initiative does not comply with the conditions laid down in the annex to the regulation, the Commission may refuse to support it. If the Commission considers that the initiative has not complied with these conditions, the initiative may be withdrawn from the register.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 11 March 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 11 March 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Citizens' initiative’ is a legal instrument that allows EU citizens to express their views on European-wide issues and to foster reflection on those issues.
For more information, see:
‘European Citizens’ Initiative (European Commission)’ on the European Agency for Fundamental Rights’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —   —
  The European Parliament has adopted two delegated acts on the issue of citizens' initiatives (Regulation(EU) 2015/852 and Regulation (EU, 2016/853) respectively). These have been incorporated into the original text.
  KEY TERMS
* Citizens: a person who is a citizen of an EU country and who wishes to express his or her views on an issue of interest to the EU.
* EU country: any EU country which is a member of the Schengen area and which has a permanent resident population of more than 1 million people.

Summary 88:
Statistics on pesticide sales and use
Statistics on pesticides
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1185/2009 — statistics on pesticides (Official Journal L 324 of 10.12.2009)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection of European Union (EU) statistics on the placing on the market and use of pesticides in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to pesticides which are plant protection products covered by Regulation (EC). It does not apply to biocidal products, such as pesticides used in agriculture.
EU countries are required to collect EU-wide statistics on sales and uses of pesticides from the moment they are placed on the EU market and from the point at which they are withdrawn.
The data are collected by the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Agriculture, Food and Rural Affairs (DAFRA) and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA). The data are used to assess the risks to human health and the environment related to pesticide use. They are also used to develop and monitor EU legislation and policies.
Data collection methods
The EU countries must ensure that the data collected are collected in accordance with the following statistical methods:
by means of an automated system;
by the means of a questionnaire;
inform the Commission of the methods used to collect the data;
at least once a year, the Commission publishes a report on the results of the data collection.
Reporting
The Commission must report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on how the data are being used.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 10 December 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
  ‘The Sustainable Use of Pesticides’ is a communication from the Commission to the EU countries on the implementation of the Thematic Strategy on the sustainable use of pesticide.
For more information, see:
Pesticides (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Pesticide: a substance used to kill plants, animals or other living things.
* Biocidal product: a pesticide that can be used to destroy, damage or kill a plant, animal or plant-like organism.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  *   The regulation applies from 10 November 2009. It repeals and replaces Directive 98/8/EC.

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — placing on the market and repealing Council Directives 79/117/EEC and 91/414/EC — plant protection products (Regulation (EC) No 1107/2009)
Planting on the European Union (EU) market — putting on the EU market and repeals Directives 89/7/EC and 89/8/EC
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 — amending Regulation (EC), which lays down harmonised rules for the approval of active substances and the placing on EU countries’ national authorities for the sale of these substances and their accompanying products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to ensure that substances or products produced or placed on EU markets do not have any harmful effect on human or animal health or any unacceptable effects on the environment.
It repeals Directive 89/763/Eec, which had been repealed by Directive (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances* intended for use in the production of plants and plant products. It does not apply to substances intended to be used in the manufacture, transport or storage of food or animal feed.
The substances covered by the regulation are listed in Annex I of the regulation. They must be approved by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) in accordance with the rules laid down in Article 251 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU). The EFSA has the power to grant or refuse authorisations for the use of substances.
Authorisations are issued by the competent national authorities of EU countries and must be valid for a maximum of 5 years.
EU countries must notify the EFSA of the substances they wish to authorise and the reasons for their refusal.
An application for an authorisation must be submitted by the manufacturer or importer of a plant protection product to the competent authority of an EU country. The application must contain the following information:
the substance’s intended use;
the name of the authorised authorising authority;
a description of the active substance;
an indication of the nature of the product and the intended use of the substance.
If the competent authorities consider that an active substance does not fulfil all of the above criteria, they may grant authorisation to the manufacturer, importer or seller.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    дарна баркова года  грашка  
It has applied since 24 November 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 24 November 2011.
BACKGROUND
Plant production has a very important place in the EU. One of the most important ways of protecting plants and plants products against harmful organisms, including weeds, and of improving agricultural production is to use plant-protection products.
For more information, see:
‘Plant Protection Products’ on the EFA's website.
KEY TERMS
* Active substance: a substance that can be used to protect plants or plant products against a wide range of harmful organisms including weeds.
* Plant protection product: a product that protects plants against a variety of plant pests, such as insects, mites, nematodes, parasitic worms and parasitic mites. It may also protect plants against the harmful effects of certain chemicals in the food chain. It can also protect plant production against certain harmful effects on plant production, including the effects of pesticides.

  The European Parliament and the Council of 21 October 2009 amending Directive (EEC) 2009/722/EC of the European Parliament, of 22 November 2009, concerning the placing of plants protection products on the Market and repealing Directives (Euratom) 1989/742/EC, 89/753/EC or 89/855/EC (Directive 89/762/EC) (OJ L 309, 24.11.2009, pp. 1-12)

Summary 90:
Administrative cooperation through the Internal Market Information System (IMI)
Installing and operating IMI
Implementing the EU’s internal market information system (Imi)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2012 on administrative cooperation through IMI (‘the IMI Regulation’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The regulation establishes a legal framework for the use of IMI, a centralised communication system designed to facilitate cross-border administrative cooperation between EU countries and the European Commission.
The decision repeals Commission Decision 2008/49/EC.
KEY POINTS
Imi is a software application accessible via the internet, developed by the Commission in cooperation with the EU countries in order to assist EU countries with the practical implementation of information exchange requirements laid down in EU acts by providing an effective, user-friendly tool for the implementation of administrative cooperation. It allows EU countries to identify their counterpart in another EU country, to manage the exchange of information, including personal data, on the basis of simple and unified procedures and to overcome language barriers.
IMI is designed to improve the functioning of the internal market by providing a centralisation of information and mutual assistance. It does this by:
providing an effective means of administrative coordination between the competent authorities of EU countries;
ensuring that information exchanged via IMI is collected, processed and used for purposes in line with those for which it was originally collected and for which are subject to all relevant safeguards.
It does not only for the purposes for which would otherwise take place via other means, including regular mail, fax or electronic mail.
Implementation of EU acts requires EU countries’ authorities and bodies to exchange information with one another and with the Commission. It also requires that EU countries:
ensure that personal data are only processed for the purpose for which they were originally collected;
provide each other with the necessary information to carry out the processing of personal data;
notify the other EU countries of any changes to personal data that may be necessary for the application of an EU act.
EU countries must ensure that the information exchanged by IMI complies with all relevant EU data protection rules and that it is protected by all relevant privacy and data security safeguards. They must also ensure that IMI users have access to the following information:
the information they provide to the IMI user;
the data they send to the user, including their name, address and telephone number;
information they receive from the user on the use and processing of their personal data.
In addition, EU countries must provide each other and the Commission with any existing additional translation functionality that meets their needs, is compatible with the security and confidentiality requirements for the exchange in IMI and can be offered at a reasonable cost.
Annexes to the regulation set out the rules for the operation of the system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 14 November 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 November 2013.
BACKGROUND
  EU countries have to cooperate more effectively and exchange information more effectively to implement certain EU acts governing the free movement of goods, persons, services and capital in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Imi’ on the Commission's website.
