Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection in the European Union (EU) — enhanced cooperation in the area of the creation of unitary patent protection (EU 2017/852)
Union-wide authorisation, coordination and supervision of the European Patent Office (EPO) in order to ensure uniform patent protection across the EU.
Unitary European patent protection is the legal basis for the granting of European patents in the EU, which are granted to companies that manufacture or distribute products across national borders.
The European Patent Organisation (EPC) is responsible for granting European patents. The European Patent Cooperation Agreement (EPCA) was signed between the EPC and the participating EU countries in 2011. The EPCA was amended in 2017 by the EU’s new patent directive. It aims to ensure that European patents granted by the EPO have the same protection throughout the EU as those granted by national patent offices.
ACT
Regulation (EU] No 1257/2012 of the EU Parliament and of the Council of 17 December 2012 implementing enhanced cooperation between Belgium, Bulgaria, the Czech Republic, Denmark, Germany, Estonia, Ireland, Greece, France, Cyprus, Latvia, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Hungary, Malta, the Netherlands, Austria, Poland, Portugal, Romania, Slovenia, Slovakia, Finland, Sweden and the United Kingdom (hereinafter ‘participating Member States’).
SUMMARY
The EPO has the power to grant European patents with a unitary effect (i.e. a patent that has the same effect in all EU countries). The EPO grants European patents by means of a special agreement between the participating countries and the European Commission.
A European patent granted by an EU country may benefit from unitary protection by virtue of this regulation in the participating Member States. The proprietor of the patent may transfer the patent to a non-EU country or, if the proprietor is a resident of the participating country, to another EU country or to another European country.
EU countries must ensure that the patent is granted to a company that has a manufacturing or distribution activity in one of their territories and that it is licensed in the territory of the other participating countries.
Each participating country must designate an EPO representative to oversee the implementation of the EPCA and the coordination of the procedures for granting and revoking the patent.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal (Regulation) No 1258/2012
31.12.2012
-
OJ L 361 of 31.12, 2012
RELATED ACTS
Regulatory (EC) No 852/2013 of the EC of 11 December 2013 establishing a European Patent Coordination Centre for the purposes of implementing the European patent directive and amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2013 (see summary) and repealing Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 696/2012 (See summary).
Regulations (EC), (EC and EFTA) No 1096/2014 and (ECE) No 1197/2014 have been amended by the new EU patent directive, which aims to improve the protection of intellectual property rights (IP rights) in the field of patent law. The new directive aims to make it easier for companies to obtain patent protection by simplifying the procedure for granting patents and simplifying procedures for revoking patents. It also aims to increase the level of patent protection for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) by making it possible to obtain a patent in more than one EU country at the same time.

Summary 93:
European Observatory on Infringements of Intellectual Property Rights (EOSI)
European Observatory for Counterfeiting and Piracy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 386/2012 on entrusting the Office for Harmonization in the Internal Market (Trade Marks and Designs) with tasks related to the enforcement of intellectual property rights, including the assembling of public and private-sector representatives as a European Observatory
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the EOSI Observatory, a network of experts from the public and the private sectors to exchange ideas and expertise on best practices and make recommendations to policymakers for joint enforcement strategies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The Observatory is responsible for gathering, monitoring and reporting information and data related to all infringements of copyright, trademarks, trade mark and design rights in the European Union (EU). It is the central resource for gathering information on counterfeiting and piracy in the EU. It is also responsible for publishing a comprehensive annual report on the scope, scale and principal characteristics of counterfeiting, as well as its impact on the EU’s internal market.
It is composed of representatives from EU countries, the European Commission, the private sector and the EU countries. It meets at least once a year to exchange information and to make recommendations on joint action to tackle counterfeiting. It also provides a forum for cooperation between EU countries and the Commission.
The European Commission has the power to appoint a chairperson and a vice-chairperson. The chairperson for the Observatory. The Chairperson is appointed by the Commission and the Vice-Chairperson is nominated by the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The Chairperson must be an EU national or EU country representative and must be a member of the Commission's Directorate-General for Justice, Equality and Human Rights. The Council may also appoint an independent chairperson to serve as the Chairperson of the Observatory's Advisory Board.
In addition, the chairperson is assisted by a Vice Chairperson who is appointed for a term of 5 years. The Vice Chairpersons must be EU nationals or nationals of an EU country who have the right to vote in the Council. They must be members of the public or of the EU country in which they represent.
EU countries are responsible for providing the Observatory with the relevant information on the activities of their national authorities, the Commission, industry and other interested parties. The Observatory must also provide the Commission with the information it receives from the national authorities of the Member States, the EU and private sector on the infringements it receives.
Each year, the Observatory publishes an annual report covering the scope and scale of the infringement of copyright and trademark rights and the impact of these infringements on the internal market and on the health and safety of EU consumers. It must also publish a report on its work.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARITY APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 16 May 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 May 2013.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Copyright: a trademark or trade mark that is used to mark a product or service.
* Trade mark: a mark or design which is used for the sale of goods or services to consumers.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 94:
Exercise of the EU’s rights under international trade rules — exercise of its rights under the World Trade Organization (WTO) and other international trade agreements (Regulation (EU) No 654/2014)
Exemption of the European Union from the obligation to suspend concessions or other obligations when a trade partner fails to respect its commitments under the WTO Agreement on Government Procurement (GPA) or other International Trade Agreements (Trade-Related Agreements)
The European Union has concluded a number of multilateral, regional and bilateral agreements creating rights and obligations for the mutual benefit of the parties. The EU has the right to take rebalancing measures in the event of trade restrictive measures that diminish the benefits accruing to the EU's economic operators under these agreements. Action by the EU in such cases should serve the purpose of inducing the introduction of trade-enhancing measures by third countries in order to restore a situation of reciprocal benefits.
ACT
Regulation [Official Journal L 189 of 27.6.2014].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the exercise of the rights of the Union to suspend or withdraw concessions and other obligations under the International Trade Agreement (ITA) and the WTO agreement on government procurement.
It allows the EU to suspend these concessions or obligations in cases where the third country concerned implements no adequate and proportionate adjustments.
KEY POINTS
The EU can suspend or withdrawal of these concessions and obligations by suspending or withdrawing them if the other party to an international trade agreement fails to fulfil its obligations. The suspension or withdrawal is not to result in the suspension of the agreement or in any other covered agreement of the WTO.
The suspension of these obligations does not result in an end to the agreement. It is only to be suspended if the third party fails to comply with its obligations under that agreement.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of the suspension or withdraw of the concessions, other obligations or the extension of the safeguard measures. The Commission must then inform the other EU countries of the reasons for the suspension.
If the Commission considers that the EU country concerned has not complied with the agreement, the Commission must inform the country concerned of its decision to suspend the concession or other obligation or to withdraw the safeguard measure.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
Regulations have applied since 27 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
The WTO Agreement establishes dispute settlement mechanisms to settle disputes between the EU and non-EU countries over trade in goods and services.
For more information, see:
‘International Trade Dispute Resolution Mechanism’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 95:
Bananas — bilateral safeguard clause and stabilisation mechanism for bananas — Central America
Bananas— bilateral safeguards clause and stabilization mechanism for banana
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 20/2013 — implementing the bilateral safeguard and the stabilisation mechanisms for bananas of the Agreement establishing an Association between the European Union and its Member States, on the one hand, and Central America on the other
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE AGREEMENT?
The agreement establishes a bilateral safeguard for bananas imported into the EU from Central America (known as the ‘Banana Agreement’).
It aims to prevent serious harm to the EU banana growing sector which is of great importance to the agricultural producers of many of the outermost regions of the EU.
It sets out the procedures for the implementation of the agreement.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to imports of bananas from the Central American Republic of Nicaragua, Belize, Guatemala, Honduras, Nicaragua, El Salvador, Panama, Paraguay, Peru, Suriname, Venezuela, Uruguay and the United States of America (USA). It does not apply to bananas imported from the Caribbean or from the Pacific Ocean.
Safeguard measures
The European Commission must notify the Member States of the import of the product in question within 3 months of receiving a proposal from the Commission.
The Member States must inform the Commission of all imports of the banana in question.
If the Commission considers that the import is likely to cause or threaten to cause serious injury to the economic situation of an outermost region of the Union, it may impose safeguard measures.
Importers must provide the Commission with information on the quantities of the imported product and the conditions under which the import would be likely to do so.
In the event of serious injury, the Commission may decide to suspend or reduce the amount of imports.
Stabilisation mechanism
The Commission must monitor the observance by Central America of the social and environmental standards laid down by the International Labour Organisation (ILO) in order to determine whether the import should be suspended or reduced. The Commission must submit an annual report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the implementation and the application of the safeguard measures and the stability mechanism.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 19 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
*Banana: a tropical fruit that can only be grown in the tropics, such as the Andes, the Caribbean, the Pacific and the Caribbean.
*Irregularities: an irregularity in the production or export of a product that is not intended for human consumption or consumption by the intended consumer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  The regulation implementing the agreement establishing an association between the EU and the countries of Central America — known as the Bananas Agreement — is known as Regulation (EU).
Council Decision 2013/852/EC of 23 April 2013 on the conclusion, on behalf of the Council, of the bilateral agreement establishing the Agreement between the Union and certain countries of the Caribbean and the Pacific Islands — the Agreement on Banana (Bananas Agreement) (OJ L 17, 19.1.2013, pp. 13-14)

Summary 96:
European Account Preservation Order procedure
European Account Preserving Order
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 655/2014 establishing a European Account Preservation Order procedure to facilitate cross-border debt recovery in civil and commercial matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes a European account preservation order (EAPO) procedure for preventing the transfer or withdrawal of funds held by a debtor in a bank account maintained in a Member State if there is a risk that, without such a measure, the subsequent enforcement of his claim against the debtor will be impeded or made substantially more difficult.
KEY POINTS
The EAPO allows a creditor to obtain a protective measure (‘Preservation Order’ or ‘Order’) preventing a debtor from transferring or withdrawing funds held in an account in another Member State. The creditor may also exclude the debtor himself or persons authorised by him to make payments through that account, for example by way of a standing order or through direct debit or the use of a credit card, from using the funds.
It does not apply to:
the debtor’s own bank accounts;
the accounts of other parties to insolvency proceedings, such as banks, credit institutions or other financial institutions which have been opened by the debtor;
investment projects which have not been financed by him or by his own money;
other financial institutions established by him and which are not involved in the recovery of his or her own money.
the creditor may not use the funds of a third party.
A creditor may apply for an EOP if he or she has been declared insolvent by the Court of Justice of the Member State in which the account is located.
An EOP is valid for up to 30 days from the date of entry into force of the EOP. The EOP may be renewed for a further 30 days if the debtor has not complied with the conditions laid down in the EAP.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APO APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  The EAPOs were first introduced in the framework of the ‘Green Paper on improving the efficiency of the enforcement of judgments in the European Union: the attachment of bank accounts’ (2006).
For more information, see:
‘EAPOs’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Decision 2014/852/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 May 2014 on the establishment of a European EAPo procedure for the preservation of funds in bank accounts held in other Member States (OJ L 189, 27.6.2014, pp. 59-65)
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 652/2013 and (EU), Euratom No 653/2013 of the Euratom Council of 16 June 2013 establishing a common framework for the implementation of EAPs and repealing Regulation (EC) No 852/2012 and Regulation (Euratom No 853/2012, respectively, and repealing Regulations (EC), (EC, EFTA and Euratom (EC).

Summary 97:
EU border surveillance — rules for the surveillance of the external sea borders in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the Member States of the European Union (EASA)
EU Border Surveillance: rules for border surveillance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 656/2014 — rules governing border surveillance at the EU’s external borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for EU countries’ border surveillance operations at the external borders of other EU countries.
It aims to ensure the efficient monitoring of the crossing of external borders including through border surveillance, while contributing to ensuring the protection and saving of lives.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to border surveillance activities carried out by EU countries at their external sea border with non-EU countries at the sea border of other countries or on the high seas.
EU countries must cooperate with the European Border Surveillance Agency (EBSA) to detect and prevent illegal border crossings and to take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
The EBSA is responsible for coordinating operational cooperation between EU countries in the field of border management, asylum and immigration, including as regards border surveillance.
Cooperation with neighbouring countries is essential to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to avoid loss of life at sea. It also includes steps such as intercepting vessels suspected of trying to gain entry to the EU without submitting to border checks, as well as arrangements intended to address situations such as search and rescue that may arise during a border surveillance operation at sea and arrangements to bring such an operation to a successful conclusion.
Risk analysis and training are provided by the EASA and by the EU countries and the European Maritime and Coast Guard Agency (EMCA).
The European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts to implement the regulation. These delegated acts are adopted by the Council of the EU and the EU Member States.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
    б  х  
BACKGROUND
The EU is governed by the principle of solidarity and fair sharing of responsibility in border management.
For more information, see:
‘Border management’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 98:
Asylum, migration and integration fund — general provisions on the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund and the instrument for financial support for police cooperation, preventing and combating crime, and crisis management
Asylum and migration policy — specific rules on the EU’s external action and foreign policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 on the asylum, migration, integration and police cooperation fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out general rules on how the European Union (EU), the EU countries and the European Commission (the European Commission) should work together to support the development of the area of freedom, security and justice.
It aims to create an area without internal borders where people may enter, move, live and work freely, confident that their rights are fully respected and their security assured, bearing in mind common challenges such as:
the development of a comprehensive EU immigration policy to enhance the competitiveness and social cohesion of the EU, the creation of a common European asylum system, the prevention of threats of serious and organised crime and the fight against illegal immigration, human trafficking, cybercrime and terrorism.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the categories of actions eligible for EU funding under the specific rules of the fund. These include:
emergency assistance for the protection of asylum seekers and their families;
police cooperation and prevention of crime;
crisis management.
The specific rules for each category of action are set out in the annexes to the regulation.
Each EU country must submit a national action plan for each year to the Commission, which must be approved by the European Parliament and the Council. The Commission then selects the actions to be funded by the EU country of destination.
EU countries must ensure that the actions are consistent and coherent with the principles and general objectives of the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the EU's foreign policy. They must also ensure that they respect international and human rights law, including as regards actions implemented in third countries, showing solidarity amongst all Member States and demonstrating an awareness of the need to respect national responsibilities in the process of ensuring a clear definition of tasks.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that actions are coordinated with other actions outside the EU supported through EU external assistance instruments, both geographic and thematic. They should complement, when appropriate, the financial assistance provided through external aid instruments.
They should also ensure the implementation of emergency assistance is consistent with, and, where relevant, complementary to, the EU humanitarian policy and respects humanitarian principles as laid down in the European Consensus on Humanitarian Aid.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU‘s home affairs policy is to create a single European area without external borders. It aims to ensure that people are free to enter and stay in the EU without fear of being separated from their families.
For more information, see:
‘Asylum’ on the European Agency for Fundamental Rights’’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Emergency assistance: EU funding is used to support actions that are directly or indirectly related to development. It is not intended to support humanitarian aid.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  The European Union Asylum and Migration Fund (AIM) is a multiannual financial instrument designed to support EU countries’ efforts to respond to the pressure of migration and asylum applications and to manage the management of the external borders of the Union, ensuring full respect for international, human rights and humanitarian law.
  It sets up a common framework for EU countries to co-financing the actions of the Commission and the Member States in dialogue with the Commission in order to ensure the proper implementation of that funding across the policy areas concerned.
Successive amendments to the Regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Annex I to Regulation (EU) No 516/2014 establishing the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund — rules on the allocation by Member States of the EU’s Asylum and Integration Funds
Asylum and migration funds — rules governing the application of the principles of solidarity and responsibility-sharing between the Member States and cooperation with non-EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets up the Asylum and Migration Integration Fund (AMIF) to support Member States in their national programmes to support the development of the common EU policy on asylum and immigration and to the strengthening of the area of freedom, security and justice in the light of the principle of solidarity.
KEY POINTS
The AMIF supports Member States to:
ensure a uniform and high-quality asylum policy and apply higher standards of international protection;
promote the effective functioning of the Common European System, which encompasses measures relating to policy, legislation and legislation, while acting in the interests of the protection of displaced persons and third countries;
improve the efforts of international agencies to fully and properly deal with displaced persons;
facilitate the effective integration of asylum seekers and their families into the European Union (EU);
ensuring that Member States are able to share the burden of managing migration flows.
The EMIF supports the following actions:
supporting Member States' efforts to share responsibility for the management of migration flows by:
facilitating the return of asylum-seekers to their country of origin;
sharing responsibility for their return to their countries of origin
promoting cooperation between Member States on the return and return of refugees to their home country
support for the integration of unaccompanied children and young people in the EU
support in the field of education and training for asylum seekers,
providing financial support for the implementation of the European Asylum System for the Protection of Children and Young People (EASPY)
provide financial support to the European Commission to implement the common European Union policy on immigration and asylum, which is based on solidarity and is fair towards third countries and their nationals.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISL 150-page regulation APPLY?
   
It has applied since 20 May 2014 and had to become law in the Member State concerned by 20 May 2015.
BACKGROUND

establishing a common European system for the reception of refugees and asylum seekers;
strengthening cooperation between the EU countries on the protection and repatriation of persons who have been displaced by natural disasters or who are in danger of becoming a danger to themselves or to others;
increasing the capacity of international organisations and bodies to deal with asylum seekers.

‘Asylum’ on the EMIF's website.

Summary 100:
Internal Security Fund — police cooperation, preventing and combating crime, and crisis management and repealing Council Decision 2007/125/JHA
External Security Fund – police cooperation and crime prevention
External security fund — policing cooperation and criminal justice
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 513/2014 establishing the EU’s internal security fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Internal Security Fund (ISF) to support EU countries’ police cooperation to prevent and combat crime and to respond to serious threats to the internal security of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The ISF aims to provide EU countries with financial support for police cooperation in the fight against organised crime, crime and terrorism, as well as for crisis management. It also aims to support the work carried out by Member States’ competent authorities.
It sets up and manages the ISF, which is managed by the European Commission.
The main objectives are to:
promote cooperation between EU countries in order to prevent, combat and respond to crime;
raise levels of security for citizens and businesses in cyberspace;
strengthen border management and to increase Europe’�s resilience in the face of crises and disasters;
increase the level of protection of people and goods from increasingly transnational threats.
EU countries are responsible for:
providing the necessary financial support to EU countries to implement these objectives;
supporting the work of EU institutions, agencies and non-EU countries in the field of police cooperation;
cooperating with other relevant EU bodies, such as Europolices, Europol, Eurojust and Eurojust;
contributing to the prevention and combatting of terrorism;
promoting cooperation between the relevant national authorities in the area of criminal justice, including with Eurojust or other relevant international organisations;
raising levels of protection for the security of EU citizens and business in the areas of cybercrime, terrorism, drug trafficking, corruption, cybercrime and trafficking in human beings and arms.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISL 150/93 APPLY?
   
It has applied since 20 May 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 May 2015.
BACKGROUND
The Internal Security Strategy for the EU (‘Internal Security Strategy’), adopted by the Council in February 2010, constitutes a shared agenda for tackling these common security challenges. It sets out five strategic objectives:
to disrupt international crime networks, to prevent terrorism and address radicalisation and recruitment, to raise security levels for citizens, businesses and civil society, to strengthen border management, to increase security through border control, to improve the protection of EU countries against terrorism and to strengthen security through cross-border border management.
For more information, see:
‘The EU's internal security strategy’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Police cooperation: cooperation between national authorities and other national authorities of Member States, including Europol and other relevant Union bodies, and with relevant third countries and international organisations.
* Criminal justice: the cooperation between law enforcement authorities of the Member States and the relevant non-governmental organisations (NGOs) of the EU to combat crime.

Summary 101:
Conservation of fish stocks in relation to countries allowing non-sustainable fishing — measures for the conservation of fish species (Regulation (EU) No 1026/2012 on certain measures to be taken by the European Union (EU).
Conservation measures to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing in the EU — measures to encourage countries to reduce their fishing activities in order to conserve fish stocks.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the type of measures that the EU may impose on non-EU countries that allow fishing activities that jeopardise the sustainable management of certain shared, straddling and highly migratory fish stocks*.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to countries that:
have an interest in a fishery involving a fish stock of common interest to that country and to the EU;
have the right to participate in the management of that fishery;
are not cooperating with other countries and the EU in its management;
do not cooperate with other EU countries in the conservation and management of those stocks;
fishery activities that threaten the sustainability of that stock are prohibited.
It does not apply to:
non-EU fishing vessels or EU fishing equipment;
unregistered fishing vessels;
ships that are not registered in the territory of an EU country.
The measures may be imposed by the EU or by EU countries.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any measures they take to prevent or reduce fishing activities.
they must inform the Commission of the reasons for these measures.
They must also inform the countries concerned.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The Commission adopts the regulation on behalf of the EU.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a system of fisheries management organisations (RFMOs) to manage fish stocks for the benefit of the fishing countries involved. These RFMOs are set up by the UNFSA, the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the EU’s website.

Summary 102:
Transitional arrangements for bilateral investment agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries
Transitional provisions for bilateral investments
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1219/2012 establishing transitional arrangements for EU countries to conclude and/or maintain in force agreements between Member States and third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes transitional arrangements to ensure that EU countries are able to conclude or conclude bilateral investments agreements with non-European Economic Area (EEA) countries before the entry into force of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) on 1 January 2013.
It sets out the conditions under which EU countries may amend or conclude such agreements.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to agreements signed between 1 December 2009 and 9 January 2013 between the Member States of the EU and the countries of the EEA. It does not apply to agreements concluded before 1 December 2011.
Bilateral investment agreements are binding on the EU countries under public international law and will be progressively replaced by agreements of the Union relating to the same subject matter.
The EU has exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy. It may legislate and adopt legally binding acts within that area only if so empowered by the EU Member States.
EU countries are required to take the necessary measures to eliminate incompatibilities, where they exist, with EU law, contained in bilateral agreements concluded between them and other countries. They must also inform the European Commission of any incompatibility between their own law and those of the other Member States, in accordance with Article 2(1) of the TFEU (see summary).
.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that their investment agreements comply with the common rules on capital movements laid down in Chapter 4 of Title IV of Part Three of the Treaties on the movement of capital between the EU, including in respect of capital movements involving investments.
Investment agreements must be amended or concluded with the consent of the competent authority of the Member State in which the investment is to be made. The agreement must contain the following information:
the terms and conditions of the investment agreement;
the nature and scope of the investments;
a description of the activities to be carried out by the company;
an indication of the type of investment agreement and the conditions for its conclusion.
If the agreement does not contain the necessary information, the Commission may decide to suspend or terminate the agreement if it considers that it does not meet the conditions set out in the agreement or that the agreement is incompatible with the EU’s investment policy.
Where the Commission considers that an investment agreement with an EEA country does not comply with its investment policy, it may issue an authorisation to open negotiations or conclude the agreement with the country concerned. The Commission must inform the other EU countries of its decision to do so and inform them of the reasons for the suspension or termination of the agreement.
An EU country may suspend or cancel the agreement on its own initiative if:
it does not fulfil the conditions laid down by the agreement; or
it fails to fulfil its obligations under the agreement, or if the agreement has been terminated by the other party.
Implementation of the regulation is subject to the approval of the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
 
    дантарска барка  граков  блакса на городна  
It has applied since 20 December 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 December 2013. It had to become law in the EU country in which it was signed by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EEA is governed by Article 3(1)(e) and Article 207(2) TFEUs.
For more information, see:
‘Foreign direct investment’ on the European External Action Service's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: a bilateral investment agreement between an EU country and a non-member country on the basis of which the parties agree to:
provide for the conditions of investment;
guarantee the protection of the interests of the parties;
ensure that the conditions are met by the parties in the event of a conflict of interest;
set out in Annex II to the agreement that the parties do not fulfil their obligations under Article 2 of the Agreement; and
set up a procedure for the termination or suspension of an agreement if the parties fail to respect the conditions specified in Article 2.

Summary 103:
Generalised tariff preferences for developing countries (GSPs) (until 2015)
Generalised rate preferences for development countries (GDSPs), until 2015
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 978/2012 — applying a scheme of generalised tariffs and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 732/2008
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application of the EU’s Generalised System of Preferential Trade Arrangements, which aims to help developing countries in their efforts to reduce poverty and promote good governance and sustainable development.
It repeals and repeals the previous regulation (Council Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2008.
KEY POINTS
The general arrangement is granted to all those developing countries which share a common developing need and are in a similar stage of economic development. It does not apply to countries which have completed their transition to the European Union (EU).
The tariff preferences are based on the following criteria:
developing countries have greater development, trade and financial needs;
they do not impose an unjustified trade burden on developing countries;
the development needs of developing countries are not different from those of the most developed countries.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the GSPs. It is assisted by the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the European Development Fund (EDF).

Summary 104:
UN Firearms Protocol — export authorisation and import and transit measures for firearms, their parts and components and ammunition (Article 10 of the UN Firearms Protocol)
Unilateral measures to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms (Article 9 of the United Nations Convention against Transnational Organised Crime)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 258/2012 — implementing the UN’s Firearms Protocol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to facilitate the tracing of firearms and efficiently combat illicit trafficking by establishing export authorisations for firearms.
It complements the Protocol on the illicit manufacture of and export of firearms, its parts and essential components, and ammunition, which entered into force on 3 July 2005.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that the authorisation is issued by the competent authority of the country in which the firearms are imported or exported;
provide the competent authorities of the EU country in question with a copy of the export and import authorisation form;
implement the export control system for firearms and their parts;
take all necessary measures to ensure that the relevant national authorities have the necessary information to identify and trace the firearms involved.
The regulation does not apply to firearms intended specifically for military purposes or to firearms for civilian use.
Imports and transit of firearms are subject to export control measures. EU countries must ensure that:
the authorisation for a single shipment of a firearm is valid for a period of at least 6 months from the date on which it is received;
;
the export controls are in place to prevent the transfer of firearms from one EU country to another EU country; and
the import controls for the export of a firearms to a non-EU country must be in place for at least 12 months from when the transfer takes place.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?

BACKGROUND

Summary 105:
European border surveillance system (Eurosur)
Eurosurvey of illegal immigration and cross-border crime — Eurosur (EuroSur)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 establishing the European Border Surveillance System (EUROSUR)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The regulation establishes the European border surveillance (EurosUR) system, which aims to improve the situational awareness and reaction capability of EU countries’ border control authorities at the external borders of the EU (‘external borders’) for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration.
KEY POINTS
EurOSUR is the EU’s border control system for the EU countries. It aims to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration by providing the national authorities with the information they need to detect and react to illegal immigrants. It is designed to reduce the number of migrants drowning at the southern maritime external borders.
The European Union Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders (EASO) is responsible for the development and operation of EUROSUR and for its development. It also provides the necessary financial and human resources to fulfil the additional tasks assigned to it under this regulation.
EuroosUR was set up by Regulation (EC) No 2007/2004 (see summary). It is the successor to the European External Action Service (EES), which was established in 2007.
It is the main instrument of the European model of integrated border management of the external border of the internal security strategy of the Internal Security Strategy of the Union. It provides the EU with the infrastructure and the tools needed to improve their situational awareness, reaction capability at the EU's external borders and to provide the necessary assistance to the other EU bodies, offices and agencies.

Summary 106:
Eurodac for the comparison of fingerprints for the effective application of the Dublin Convention (recast) (EurodAC) (REcast)
EuroDac is a European Union (EU) system that allows EU countries to check whether a non-EU national or a stateless person has applied for international protection in another EU country. It is designed to establish the identity of applicants for asylum and of persons apprehended in connection with the unlawful crossing of the external borders of the EU. It also allows for the exchange of information between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities and Europol for law enforcement purposes.
ACT
Regulation (EEC) No 603/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2013 on the establishment of ‧Eurodatac’ for the purpose of the effective assessment of the application for International Protection [Official Journal L 180 of 29.6.2013].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE REGULATION DO?
It sets up Eurodac, a central database of fingerprints, to check if an applicant for asylum in an EU country has been granted international protection.
KEY POINTS
Each EU country is responsible for determining the EU country responsible for examining an application for asylum lodged in one of its territory.
The applicant’s fingerprints are sent to Eurodatac by Europol, which then sends them to the national authorities of the other EU countries. The national authorities then compare the fingerprints of the applicant with those of the person who has been apprehended in the EU and to determine whether the applicant is an EU citizen or not.
When an application is rejected, the applicant must be informed of the reasons for the rejection.
EurodatAC is a computerised central database which is operated by the European Agency for the operational management of large-scale IT systems in the area of freedom, security and justice (EASJ).
The European Commission has the responsibility for the operation of EurodatAC.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 29 June 2013.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* EU country: the country in which the applicant lives or is resident.
* Non-EU country: a country which does not have an official border with the EU, but which is a candidate country for membership of the Schengen area.

Summary 107:
Quality schemes for agricultural products and foodstuffs — geographical indications and designations of origin for certain specific agricultural products (Regulation (EU) No 1151/2012 on quality schemes for food and agri-foods)
Quality schemes — geographical indication and designation of origin (RIS) for certain agricultural products
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the operation of geographical indications (GIs), designations* and labelling of agricultural products* and certain specific products for sale in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
agricultural products, such as honey, honeycomb, honey-based drinks and aromatized wine-product cocktails;
foodstuffs such as meat, poultry, fish, eggs and meat products;
organic products such as fruit, vegetables, meat, fish and eggs;
alcoholic beverages such as beer, cider, wine, spirits and spirits;
wine and wine-based products, including sparkling wines, sparkling wines and sparkling wines.
It does not apply to the production of wine, sparkling wine or sparkling wines in the EU.
GIs, designations or labelling
Each GIs, labelling and presentation must be accompanied by the following information:
the geographical indication of the country of origin of the product;
the name of the producer or the country where the product is produced;
a description of the production method used to produce the product.
The European labelling system must be in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 1234/2007 (see summary).
Labelling
The labelling must include the geographical indication (GIS) of the place of origin and the labelling or presentation of the products. The labelling may also include:
a geographical indication for the place where the products are produced and a description of how the product was obtained;
an indication for a specific place of production for a particular type of agricultural product or a specific type of foodstuff;
An indication for an agricultural product which is produced in the territory of an EU country and which is intended for sale on its territory.
Labels
The GIs must include a description and a labelling number for each GIs and a label for each of the following categories of products:
wine, beer and cider;
whiskey;
fruit, vegetables and meat;
eggs and poultry;
meat, poultry and egg products; and
wholly or partially processed meat products.
Aromatized wines
Wines, cider and sparkling wine must be labelled with the GIs of the producers of the wine, cider or cider products and the label of the manufacturer of the sparkling wine. The label must also include the name and the geographical indications for the wine or cider.
Spirits
The label of a spirit must contain a geographical indication indicating the place or place of manufacture of the spirit. It must also contain a description for the spirit, the spirit’s name or the name of its manufacturer.
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 amends Regulation (EEC) 2018/853 on the definition, description and presentation of aromatised wines, aromaticated wines, aromatized wine-by-products, and aromaticated wine-products cocktails, as well as its labelling.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?

Summary 108:
The accelerated phasing-in of double-hull or equivalent design requirements for single-Hull oil tankers in the EU (recast)
The acceleration of the phased phasing in of the design requirements of single-hollers in order to reduce the risk of accidental oil pollution in the event of collision or stranding in the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 530/2012 on the accelerated phase-out of the single hull requirements for oil tanker design
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to speed up the phasing out of single hull designs for existing oil tanker designs and to ensure that they comply with the requirements of the International Maritime Organisation’s (IMO) International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships, 1973 and the Protocol of 1978 related thereto (MARPOL 73/78).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
single hull designs* designed for the carrying crude oil (e.g. crude oil, refined petroleum products, refined natural gas, liquefied natural gas (LNG) and refined petroleum product (LPG) and/or refined petroleum derivatives) and to vessels flying the flag of an EU Member State;
double hull designs designed to carry crude oil and LNG and LPG;
floating oil terminals* designed to transport LNG, LPG, LNG or LPG.
It does not apply to warships, naval auxiliary or other ships owned or operated by a Member State and used only for government non-commercial services.
 commercial services. It does not covered by MARPOL 73/1978.
Implementation of the regulation
EU countries must ensure that:
the design of new ships flying their flag is based on a design that complies with MARPOL 1973/78;
new ships flying the EU flag are built with a double hull design;
all existing ships flying an EU flag must be designed to be able to withstand the impact of a collision or a stranding of a single hull design.
The EU countries must also ensure that the design of existing ships is compatible with the new design and that is being built for the same purpose.
National authorities must also:
ensure that new ships are designed to withstand an accidental oil spill of up to 1 million tonnes of crude oil or LNG;
ban the carriage of new oil tanker types into EU ports;
introduce a ban on the carriage and anchoring of new heavy oil terminals;
implement measures to prevent the loading of heavy crude oil into ports of Member States under the jurisdiction of the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of this regulation. The report must include information on the number of ships flying EU flag and the number and type of oil terminals that have been built under the flag. The Commission must also provide the EMSA with an estimate of the total number of oil tanks in service in the Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    дальна барка грака  глакова варша  
It has applied since 30 June 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 June 2014.

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement for workers within the EU
Freedom of mobility for workers inside the EU 
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 492/2011 on freedom of movement within the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to guarantee the right of all workers in the EU to move freely within the Union in order to pursue activities as employed persons subject to any limitations justified on grounds of public policy, public security or public health.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1612/68.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to permanent, seasonal and frontier workers, as well as those who pursue their activities for the purpose of providing services.
Eligibility for employment
Employers must ensure that all workers have the same priority as regards employment, remuneration and other conditions of work and employment, and also that obstacles to the mobility of workers are eliminated, in particular as regards the conditions for the integration of the worker’s family into the host country.
Workers must be informed of living and working conditions. Employers must also inform workers of the conditions under which they can exercise their right to live and work.
The employer must inform the worker of the terms and conditions of his or her employment and of the possibility to take up employment in another EU country if he or she wishes to do so.
Access to housing
The worker must have access to a residence permit issued by the competent authority of the EU country in which he/she lives or works. The permit must be valid for at least 6 months and may be renewed for a further 6 months if the employer does not fulfil the conditions of the permit.
Housing
The employee must be provided with a housing permit issued in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation. The housing permit must include the following information:
the duration of the residence permit;
the number of days the worker is entitled to live or work in the country in question;
a description of the premises where the accommodation is to be provided; and
the type of accommodation provided for in the housing permit. The employer must provide the worker with information on the type of housing that is available to him/her in the host EU country, including the number of rooms available for rent.
Rights and obligations of the employer
An employer may refuse to employ a worker if:
he/she is a national of an EU country;
he or she is a resident of a non-EU country; or
the employer has the right to refuse employment to a worker who is not an EU national.
If an employer refuses to hire a worker because of his/her nationality, the worker may lodge a complaint with the competent authorities of the country where the employee is employed. The employee may also take legal action against the employer in the territory in which the employer is located.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 27 May 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 27 May 2012.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Non-discrimination: any discrimination based on nationality between workers of EU countries.
* Mobility: the ability to move between different EU countries to work, live or study.
ACT
  The regulation has applied in the Member States since 26 May 2011 and had to become law in the countries of the European Economic Area (EEA) by 27 June 2011.

Summary 111:
Ozone-depleting substances
Ozone depleting substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1005/2009 on substances that deplete the ozone layer
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on the use of substances that reduce the visibility of ultraviolet-B (UV-B) radiation in the stratosphere (the ozone layer) to protect human health and the environment against adverse effects resulting from ozone depletion.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation bans the production and use of hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HFCs) used for the maintenance or servicing of refrigeration or air conditioning equipment. These substances are greenhouse gases and are not controlled under the United Nations Framework Convention for Climate Change and its Kyoto Protocol on the assumption that the Montreal Protocol will phase out ODS by the end of the 21st century.
It does not apply to the production or use of virgin HFCs recovered from recycled recycled material or to the re-use of recycled hydrochloroarbons recovered from chloropropyls (COPs) recovered from the disposal of waste materials.
The ban applies from 1 January 2020. It will continue to apply until 1 January 2023.
EU countries must ensure that:
the use of these substances does not exceed the limits set out in Regulation (EU) No 2037/2000;
they do not exceed those set out by Regulation (EEC) 2017/852/EU (see summary);
they comply with the Annex I of the regulation;
there is no exemption from the ban on the production, use or disposal of virgin HCFCs;
the EU countries are required to ensure that the chemicals used in their production and disposal are traceable and traceable to the source of the HCFC;
in the event of non-compliance with the ban, they must inform the European Commission and the other signatory countries of the reasons for their refusal to comply.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer (Ozone Layer) was signed in Montreal in December 2005. It aims to reduce the levels of ozone in the upper stratosphere by reducing the amount of ozone depletion in the atmosphere.
For more information, see:
The ozone layer (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* HFC: a substance that depletes the protective ozone layer by reducing its visibility in the air. It is a greenhouse gas and is one of the greenhouse gases covered by the Kyoto Protocol.
* HCFC: an organic substance with a high global warming potential.

European Environment Agency and the European Environment Information and Observation Network (EEAINN)
European Union (EU) Agency and EEAINNN (European Union)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 401/2009 — European Environment Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Environmental Agency (EEA) to provide objective, reliable and comparable information at European level enabling the EU and its Member States to take the requisite measures to protect the environment, to assess the results of such measures and to ensure that the public is properly informed about the state of the environment.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 1210/90 on the establishment of the EEA.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EEA is responsible for:
collecting, processing and analysing environmental data at EU level;
coordinating the EU’s policies on the environment;
preparing and implementing environmental legislation;
assessing the environmental needs of Member States;
providing information to the European Commission on the implementation of EU environmental legislation.
Function
The agency has a number of tasks, including:
assisting the Commission in developing and implementing EU environmental policies;
monitoring and evaluating the environmental situation in the field of environmental policy;
contributing to the development and implementation of European environmental information and observation networks (EEOINN).
The work of the agency is carried out in close cooperation with the existing national and international facilities providing environmental data and services in the EU.
The Agency has the right of access to documents of the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission, as well as to the documents of other Member States. It may also grant access to the information it receives from the EEOIN Network.

Summary 113:
Voluntary participation by organisations in a Community eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS)
Voluntary membership of an EU environmental management and audit system
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1221/2009 on the voluntary participation by non-governmental organisations in an EU eco-managing and auditing scheme
Decision No 1600/2002/EC on the Sixth Community Environment Action Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to encourage non-government organisations (NGOs) to participate in the European Union’s (EU) eco-managed and audit (EMAs) scheme.
It sets out the rules for EMAS.
KEY POINTS
Participation in EMAS
EMAS is open to all organisations, in and outside the EU, whose activities have an environmental impact and that are able to demonstrate an improvement of their environmental performance.
Organisations are encouraged to participate on a voluntary basis and may gain added value in terms of regulatory control, cost savings and public image provided that they can demonstrate that they have an improved environmental performance and that their environmental management systems (EMMSs) are up-to-date.
The European Commission is to review EMAS in the light of the experience gained during its operation and propose appropriate amendments to the regulation.
Member States must ensure that EMAS is implemented in a way that is transparent, non-discriminatory and non-intimidating. They must also, in order to raise the appeal of EMAS for organisations, take account of EMMSs in their procurement policies and, where appropriate, refer to EMAS or equivalent environmental management system as contract performance conditions for works and service.
National authorities must encourage organisations to register with EMAS and to provide the Commission with information on their EMAS activities. The Commission may also use EMAS as a tool in the enforcement of EU environmental legislation.
Registers
The Commission must publish a register of all organisations that have been registered under EMAS, as well as a list of those that have not been registered. The register must be updated every 5 years. It must contain information on:
organisations’ environmental performance;
the number of sites covered by EMAS;
how EMAS has been implemented;
environmental impact assessments carried out;
information on the environmental impact assessment carried out by the Commission and by the Member States.
An annual report must be published on the EMAS website. The report must contain the following information:
the total number of environmental impacts of an EMAS project;
total number of activities carried out under the scheme; and
the results of the environmental assessment.
A report must also be published by the European Commission on the implementation of the regulation and on the measures taken by the EU countries to ensure that the scheme is implemented correctly.
In addition, the Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on EMAS’ implementation and the measures it has taken to implement it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 22 December 2009 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 22 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the environmental performance of organisations. These include:
establishing a single register of environmental management sites;
introducing a single system for monitoring environmental performance at sites located on the territory of the EU.
For more information, see:
Environmental Management and Audit Scheme (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-governmental organisation (NGO): an organisation that is not a member of a national, regional or local authority.
* EMAS: an EU scheme that aims to improve environmental performance by encouraging organisations to implement an environmental management or audit system.

Summary 114:
Definition, description, presentation, labelling and the protection of geographical indications of spirit drinks (Regulation (EC) No 110/2008 - rules on the definition, description and presentation of spirits and their names
Definition, descriptions, presentation and labelling of spirits, their names and geographical indications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 110 /2008 on spirit drinks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to ensure that spirit drinks produced in the European Union (EU) are defined, described, presented and labelled in a way that respects the traditional production methods used in the production of alcoholic drinks.
It also sets out rules to prevent the misuse of the term ‘spirit drink’ to mislead consumers and to protect the geographical indication (GIs) of certain spirits.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks placed on the market in the EU, whether they are made in the Community or in non-EU countries. It does not apply to spirits produced for export to the EU for export.
Manufacturers must ensure that their products meet the following criteria:
they meet the requirements laid down in Annex I of the regulation;
they do not use the terms ‘vodka’, ‘bourbons’ or ‘pomegranates’ in their names or in their labelling;
their products do not contain the words ‘alcohol’ and ‘wine’.
They do not mislead consumers or mislead them by:
using the term’s definition of ‘spirits’ as a generic term for all other than ‘drinks of alcoholic origin’;
using words such as ‘vegetative’ for the definition of a spirit drink;
misleading them by using the terms’ ‘avocados’ (vodka), ‘beverages’*, ‘liquor’, ‘ciders’
using a name other than that of an agricultural product other than cereals and/or potatoes for the description of an alcoholic drink other than a beverage of agricultural origin.
Labelling
Labels must contain the following information:
the name of the spirit drink and its geographical indication;
the geographical indication of the country of origin;
a description of the production method used to make the spirit drinks;
details of the raw materials used for making the spirits;
any other information on the raw material used where the vodka is made from raw material other than cereal or potato.
Annexes II and III to the regulation set out the rules for the labelling, packaging and advertising of the spirits and the use of the names of the drinks in the presentation of foodstuffs.
The European Commission may, in exceptional cases, grant a derogation from the requirements of Annexes I and II to this regulation in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny. This derogation is valid for a maximum period of 5 years from the date of entry into force of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?

Summary 115:
Civil aviation security — common rules for safeguarding civil aviation
civil aviation security— common rules to safeguard civil aviation			
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 300/2008 on common rules in the field of civil aviation security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the basic principles of what has to be done in order to protect civil aviation against acts of unlawful interference (i.e. acts of interference with civil aircraft) and lays down common rules and common basic standards on aviation security as well as mechanisms for monitoring compliance.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EU) No 2320/2002.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to airports serving civil aviation located in the territory of an EU country, to operators providing services at such airports and to entities providing goods and/or services to or through such airports. It does not cover security measures that apply on board an aircraft, or during a flight, of EU air carriers.
EU countries may decide whether to deploy in-flight security officers on aircraft registered in that EU country and on flights of air carriers licensed by it and to ensure, in accordance with paragraph 4.7 of Annex 17 to the Chicago Convention on International Civil Aviation and under the terms of that Convention, that such officers are government personnel who are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board the aircraft.
Each EU country must designate a single aviation security authority responsible for the implementation of the security measures laid down in the regulation. This authority is responsible for ensuring that:
airport security measures are in place at the airport where the airport is located;
airports in the airport are secure;
security measures are implemented by the airport authority of the EU country in which the air carrier is registered;
the airport authority in question has the authority to monitor the security of the airport;
there is a risk assessment of the level of threat to civil aviation.
The European Commission may, on the basis of this risk assessment, examine whether to grant derogations from the basic security standards laid down by the EU countries. If the Commission considers that a derogation is not justified, it must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for its decision.
If the Commission concludes that the derogations are justified, the derogation may be granted by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   		 	  
It has applied since 9 April 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 April 2011.

Summary 116:
Restrictive measures against serious human rights violations and abuses — targeted restrictive measures (RTRs)
Restricted funds — targeted restrictions on the movement of persons, entities and bodies — targeted restraints on the movements of persons and entities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/1998 — Restrictive measures to address serious human right violations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The regulation establishes a legal framework for targeted measures designed to freeze the funds and economic resources of individuals, entities or bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in human rights abuses.
The decision sets out the list of individuals and entities covered by the RTRs.
KEY POINTS
The measures are based on the basis of a joint proposal from the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy and the European Commission, the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the United Nations Office for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (UNODC).
The EU countries must inform each other of the measures taken pursuant to this regulation and of other relevant information at their disposal in connection with this regulation.
They must also inform the other EU countries of the names and other relevant data of the natural and legal persons or entities listed in the annex to the regulation. They must inform the Commission of the reasons for the freezing of these persons and bodies and of the penalties applicable to infringements of these measures.
In addition, the Commission must notify the European Parliament and the Council of any information it receives from the other than those listed in Annex I to the Regulation.
. The Commission must also notify the EU countries if it receives information from non-EU countries.
EU countries may freeze the assets of the individuals or entities on the list provided for in Annex II.

Regulatory (EU), Euratom and Euratom Regulation (EC) 2017 /854 (see combined version) — EU-wide sanctions (See consolidated version).
Regulating (EU).EU.EU.2016/679 — EU countries may impose targeted sanctions on individuals, companies or entities that:
have committed or are suspected of committing or are likely to commit serious violations or abuses in the territory of an EU country;
have provided financial support to, or are providing support for, persons or bodies involved in, serious human trafficking; or
are suspected of supporting or aiding or abetting the activities of those who commit or are accused of committing such violations and/or abuses;
are involved in the financing of, or attempting to finance, the activities or activities of, persons, companies, entities, bodies or bodies that are suspected or suspected of engaging in, or have been accused of, committing or aiding, or aiding in, any of the following:
the financing of the activities and activities of individuals or bodies suspected or accused of serious violations;
the funding or support of the actions of persons or organisations suspected of, accused of or implicated in, committing, aiding or assisting in, facilitating, facilitating or aiding the activities, activities or actions of any of those individuals or organisations;
participating in or providing material support to persons, organisations or bodies implicated in serious violations and activities;
supporting or aiding persons, organizations or bodies engaged in, attempting to commit or aiding such violations or activities.

is not bound by the arms embargo established by the United Nations Security Council Resolution (UNSCR) 1970 (2011) and subsequent Resolutions, in particular UNSCRs 2292 (2016) and 2473 (2019);

Summary 119:
Regulation of the professions — proportionality test before adoption of new regulation of professions (Directive (EU) 2018/958) (European Commission)
Regulation on the access to and pursuit of regulated professions (Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2005/36/EC — a proportionality assessment before adoption by EU countries of new regulations of professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure the proper functioning of the EU’s single market by setting out rules for the assessment of the proportionality of new rules on access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all regulated professions falling within the scope of Directive 2005/35/EC. It does not apply to new professions or to existing regulated professions or new professions that EU countries are considering to regulate.
It does not affect the freedom to choose an occupation or to conduct a business.
The regulation does not restrict access to the activities of professional organisations or to the freedom of establishment of a professional organisation.
National authorities must ensure that the regulation is applied in a non-discriminatory manner and that it is justified by public interest objectives; be suitable for securing the attainment of the objective which they pursue; and not go beyond what is necessary in order to attain that objective.
EU countries must also ensure that:
the regulation is based on the principles of non-discrimination and proportionality;
the legislation does not prejudice the rights of workers, employers or self-employed persons;
it does not prevent the free movement of workers;
or
the freedom to provide services.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE IN?

BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been working towards a European single market for the past 3 years.

Summary 120:
Fisheries in the North Sea — multiannual management plan for demersal stocks in the north sea and the fisheries exploiting those stocks (2018-2021)
Fishers in the northern sea — multi-annual management plans for demerals
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/973 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 4 July 2018 establishing a multiannually planned management plan to restore fish stocks in North Sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the management of fish stocks and fisheries exploiting them by the European Commission (the EU’s fisheries authority).
It lays down the rules to be followed by EU countries to ensure that fishing and aquaculture are environmentally sustainable.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fisheries exploiting stocks of demersals in the Norwegian North Sea, including:
mixed-fish fisheries;
cartilage fisheries in the Atlantic Ocean;
prawns in the Baltic Sea;
fishing in the Black Sea and the adjacent waters of the North Atlantic Ocean.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 676/2007 and Regulations (EC, EC) No 1342/2008.
EU countries must notify the Commission of the stocks falling within the scope of the management plan within 3 months of receiving it.
The plan must contain objectives, quantifiable targets with clear timeframes, conservation reference points, safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches. It must also include the best available scientific advice for the stocks within its scope.
National authorities must inform the Commission every 3 years of the results of the assessment of the stock and, where appropriate, of the measures to be taken to restore the stocks to levels that can produce the maximum sustainable yield (MSY)*.
The Commission must also inform the public of any changes to the management plans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?

Summary 121:
Management, conservation and control measures applicable in the South Pacific Regional Fisheries Management Organisation (SPRFMO) Convention Area
Migration and fisheries management measures applicable to the south Pacific Regional fisheries management organisation (SPFMO) convention area
Migrating fish in the southern Pacific Ocean
Fishing and fishing gear
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/975 — management, conservation & control measures for fisheries in the south pacific region
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the management and control of fishing gear and techniques in the waters of the southern pacific area (known as the SPRFMO Convention Area).
It aims to ensure that the exploitation of marine biological resources contributes to long-term environmental, economic and social sustainability.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels operating in the area.
It does not cover the fishing opportunities decided by the Commission, as those fishing opportunities are allocated in the framework of the annual Regulation on fishing opportunities adopted pursuant to Article 43(3) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the conservation and management measures adopted by the SPRFMO. These are binding on the contracting parties to the convention.
EU countries must ensure that:
fisheries are managed in accordance with the principles laid down in the common fisheries policy (CFP), including the precautionary approach to fisheries management and an ecosystem-based approach;
*;
fishing gear and fishing techniques are selective and non-targeted;
.
Fisheries must be used only in areas where there is a high risk of overfishing;
all fishing gear is designed to minimise the impact of fishing on the environment;
the use of nontargeted fishing gear must be minimised to a minimum of 10% of the total weight of the fishing gear used;
sustainable fishing methods are used to reduce the impact on the marine environment.
Inspections
The EU countries must carry out regular inspections of fishing vessels to check that they are complying with the rules of the convention and that they have not been involved in any illegal fishing activities.
If a vessel is found to be fishing illegally, the EU country concerned must inform the Commission of the vessel’s registration number and the number of persons on board. The Commission must then inform the other contracting parties and the other countries of the results of the inspections.
The European Commission may also carry out its own inspections of vessels that are not in compliance with the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
 
    дарска барка  гракова гарша  
It has applied since 16 July 2018.
BACKGROUND

Summary 122:
Controls on cash entering or leaving the EU
Controlling cash entering and leaving the Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1672 — controls on cash on entering or entering the EU and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and detect money laundering and terrorist financing by laying down a system of controls applicable to natural persons who enter or leave the EU carrying amounts of cash or bearer-negotiable instruments equal to or greater than EUR 10 000 or its equivalent in other currencies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any natural person who:
enters or leaves the EU with cash or cash-related instruments greater than €10 000;
has a bank account in an EU country;
is a legal resident of the EU.
It does not apply to cash couriers.
National authorities must inform the European Financial Intelligence Unit (EFIU) of any cash movements that are suspected of being the proceeds of criminal or terrorist activities. The EFU must report the information to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and the European Central Bank (ECB).
The EFU is responsible for monitoring the cash movements of natural persons in and out of the European Union (EU). It must also alert the European Commission of any suspicious cash movements.
The directive repeals Regulation (EEC No 89/2005, which had been repealed by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/2023.
