Summary 123:
The EU’s energy union — governance of the Energy Union and climate action (EUC)
The EU's energy union – governance of its energy union and carbon action (EC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1999 — Governance of the energy union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the necessary legal framework for reliable, inclusive, cost-efficient, transparent and predictable governance of Europe's energy market and the energy sector.
It aims to ensure that the energy market operates in a transparent, efficient and sustainable way, with a view to achieving the EU's 2030 and long-term objectives and targets.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation lays down the structure for the EU energy union, which aims to give EU consumers, including households and businesses, secure, sustainable, competitive and affordable energy, and to foster research and innovation by means of attracting investment. It also aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by reducing energy consumption by at least 40 % by 2030.
The energy union is governed by a governance mechanism, the EUC Governance Mechanism, which is responsible for the implementation of the objectives and the long-range targets of the EU Energy Union. It is chaired by the European Commission. It consists of the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU), the European Energy Agency (EEA) and the European Atomic Energy Community (EEC).
The EUC governs the energy system and the EU countries’ national energy markets. It sets out rules on energy efficiency, energy security, energy efficiency and decarbonisation. The EUC also ensures that EU countries have the right to set their own national targets for energy efficiency.
In addition, the regulation sets out a framework for the development of new and renewable forms of energy, including energy performance of buildings, and market design.
EUC governance
The Commission appoints a chairperson and a vice-chairperson to ensure the functioning of EUC governance. The chairperson is appointed by the Commission and has the power to:
make recommendations to EUC chairpersons and vice-chairs;
make proposals to the Council and the EEA Chairpersons;
draw up a report on the implementation and functioning of the regulation;
set up a committee to review the regulation every 5 years;
ensure that EUC decisions are based on the best available scientific, technical and financial information;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to implement the regulation.
Each year, the Commission adopts a report to the EEC Chairperson on the functioning and implementation of this regulation. The report is published in the Official Journal. The Commission publishes the report on its website.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Energy Union is one of a series of initiatives in sectoral energy policy designed to transform the EU into a leading region for innovation, investment, growth and social and economic development, in turn providing a good example of how pursuing high ambitions in terms of climate change mitigation is intertwined with measures to foster innovation and investment and growth.
For more information, see:
Energy Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: an energy system that aims to provide consumers with secure, affordable, reliable, sustainable and competitive energy.
* Decarbonisation: the reduction of energy consumption and greenhouse gas emissions.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EU] 2018/ 1999
1.1.2019
-
OJ L 328 of 21.12.2018
Last updated: 20.01.2019



Summary 124:
Persistent organic pollutants (POPs) — European Union (EU) rules on their use and disposal
Persisting organic pollutants
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) 2019/1021 — EU rules on POPs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ban the manufacture, placing on the market and use of certain chemicals and pesticides that can pollute the environment by releasing them into the air.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out a common legal framework within which to take measures designed, in particular, to eliminate the manufacturing and placing on market and the use of intentionally manufactured POPs.
It does not apply to substances listed in the Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants or the 1979 Rotterdam Protocol on Long-Range Transboundary Air Pollution (LRTAP) or the Minamata Convention on Mercury.
EU countries must restrict the manufacture of POPs and their use in their territory, as well as to the import and export of these substances to the EU, and to non-EU countries. They must also restrict the import of these POPs into the EU and the export of those POPs to other EU countries.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that POPs listed in Annex I of the regulation are not used in the manufacture and import of other POPs that are not covered by Annex II of Regulation (EU/EU) No 110/2009 (see summary) or Annex III of the EU/EU Directive on the Safe Chemicals Directive.
The European Commission must consult the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic Area (EEA) and the Committee of the Regions (CEAR) before it adopts a decision on whether to approve or reject a POPs import or export permit.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    дародноданска барка  гранка (‘краканъка’) is the EU’s official language. It has to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is seriously concerned by the continuous release of persistent organic pollutants into the environment. These chemicals are transported across international boundaries, far from their sources, and they persist in the environment, bioaccumulate through the food web, and pose a risk to human health and the environment and to the food chain.
For more information, see:
‘The EU and POPs’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 125:
Reducing the impact of plastic waste on the environment — reducing the quantity of plastic in the EU’s waters
Reducing plastic waste — reducing plastic in EU waters
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/904 on reducing plastic waste in the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the amount of plastic that ends up in the sea and in the air by reducing the plastic waste generated by the manufacture, import and export of plastic products in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
plastic waste from the production, import, export and disposal of products, such as bottles, bags, containers, bottles and other plastic packaging, that are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling.
It does not apply to the disposal of waste from land-bound plastic waste (e.g. plastic bottles and bags) or waste from ships.
EU countries must ensure that plastic waste is disposed of in a way that minimises its impact on the marine environment and minimises the risk to human health and the environment. They must also ensure that the waste is treated in a manner that reduces the risk of microplastics entering the environment and that it does not result in marine litter.
The EU countries must also:
ensure that plastic wastes are disposed of only in the most environmentally sound and environmentally sound ways;
ban the import of plastic wastes from landbound waste into the EU;
limit the volume of waste that can be exported to non-EU countries;
introduce a ban on the export of plastics from the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLANTS APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission concluded in its European Strategy for Plastics laid down in its Communication of 16 January 2018 entitled ‘A European strategy for plastics in a Circular Economy’ that the steady increase in plastic waste generation and the leakage of plastic into the environment, in particular into the marine environments, must be tackled in order to achieve a circular life cycle for plastics.
For more information, see:
‘Plastics in a circular economy’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Plastics: a plastic material that is widely used in everyday life.
* Plastic waste: plastic waste that is not designed to be reused or recycled.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
   
DirectIVE (EU] 2019/ 904 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 5 June 2019 on the reduction of the impact from the manufacture and import of certain plastic products and on the transport of plastic materials to the environment (OJ L 155, 12.6.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 126:
EU trade mark — EU-wide protection for goods and services — codification of Regulation (EC) No 207/2009 (EU) 2017/1001 on the European Union trade mark (codification) (OJ L 154, 16.6.2017, pp. 1-11)
EU trade sign — codified Regulation (EU-2017/1001 — EU trade mark
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules for the protection of trade marks in the EU (known as EU trade marks) which are governed by a uniform EU law directly applicable in all EU countries.
It codifies and repeals the previous Regulation (Regulation (EC, No 40/94) on trade marks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products and services manufactured and distributed within the EU, whether they are manufactured or distributed by an EU-registered company or by a non-EU company. It does not apply to the manufacture or distribution of goods or services of companies from outside the EU.
The rights conferred on the proprietors of trade mark rights are limited to the use of the trade mark in one or more EU countries and the right to use it only as a distinctive trade mark if it is obtained by means of a trade mark obtained only as an EU or national trade mark, or if it does not comply with the rules of the EU countries in which it is registered.
National trade marks may not be used as distinctive trade marks if:
the trade mark does not conform with the EU rules;
it does not meet the essential requirements of the law of an EU country;
or
it is not clearly visible or intelligible to the public;
the use of it is not consistent with the laws of the other EU countries
it has not been authorised by the national law of the country where it is used.
EU-wide trade marks
The trade mark may be used only in EU countries where it has been authorised or authorised by a national law.
A trade mark can only be used if it conforms with the requirements of EU law and if it:
has a clear and intelligible form of representation,
is clearly visible to the general public,
does not conflict with national law,
has the same function as national trade marks;
is of a distinctive character and does not interfere with the functioning of the internal market;
has not been used by a company which is not a member of the European Economic Area (EEA);
is not used by an organisation which is a national of an EEA country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 16 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a distinctive sign or indication of the identity of a product or service.
* National trade mark: an indication of a company’s place of manufacture, place of distribution or place of business, or place where it does business, that is, the place where the goods or service of the company are manufactured, distributed or manufactured, or where the company does business.

Summary 127:
Accessible format copies for the benefit of persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled (Marrakesh Treaty) (Regulation (EU) 2017/1563) (European Commission).
Accessible formats for the purpose of the exchange of accessible format copies of certain works and other subject matter protected by copyright and related rights for the beneficiary of persons with disabilities (Marrakeesh Treaty).
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2001/29/EC on the cross-border exchange between the EU and non-EU countries of accessible formats of protected works (the ‘Marrakh Treaty’) (see summary) (See summary)
Regulation [EU] 2017/564 on the export and import arrangements for accessible formats for non-commercial purposes between EU countries that are parties to the Marrakesh treaty (see consolidated version)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE AND THE REGULATION?
The directive aims to improve the accessibility of printed works for beneficiary persons in all EU countries and the circulation of such copies within the internal market.
The regulation aims to implement the obligations under the treaty with respect to the export, import and distribution of accessible-format copies of protected materials for the use of beneficiary persons.
KEY POINTS
The legislation applies to the following works:
books and other printed material in an accessible format (e.g. e-books, audiobooks, newspapers, magazines, etc.).
It does not apply to:
printed works in a language other than that of the author or the author’s own;
printed materials in an electronic format (i.e. electronic books, audio books, magazines and newspapers);
printed material in a format other than those of the authors or authors’ own.
EU countries must ensure that:
the accessible format of the works is made available to beneficiary persons by the competent authorities of the EU country in which the work is published;
the distribution of the accessible format is carried out by a third party without the consent of the rightholders of the work.
Exceptions to the regulation are provided for in the annexes to the directive.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Decision 2001/30/EC has applied since 1 January 2002. Directive 2001/23/EC had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2003.
Decisions 2001/33/EC and 2001/34/EC have applied since 31 December 2001.
Regulations (EU, EU, EFTA, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Turkey and the United Kingdom have to apply from 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
Accessibility of printed material for persons with visual impairments or disabilities is one of the main aims of the Marrakeesh treaty. It aims to ensure that beneficiaries of the treaty are able to read printed works to substantially the same degree as persons without such disabilities.
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility for Persons with Visual Impaired or Print-Disabled’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 128:
Cooperation between national authorities responsible for the enforcement of consumer protection laws
Cooperation among EU countries’ consumer protection authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2394 — cooperation between EU countries responsible for enforcing consumer protection law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between the national authorities that are responsible for enforcement of cross-border consumer protection legislation in order to tackle the challenges of e-commerce and the digital environment and to prevent non-compliant traders from exploiting gaps in the enforcement system by relocating to Member States whose competent authorities are not equipped to tackle unlawful practices.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are encouraged to designate one of the competent authorities as the single liaison office responsible for implementing the regulation. That authority must have sufficient powers and necessary resources to undertake that key role. It must cooperate with each other more quickly and more efficiently to detect, to investigate and to order the cessation or prohibition of infringements covered by this regulation.
Each EU country must designate a national authority to carry out the tasks delegated to it by the European Commission. This authority is responsible for:
coordinating with other national authorities;
ensuring that necessary information and evidence can be validly exchanged;
providing the Commission with all the necessary information on the activities of the designated authority;
carrying out the duties of the delegated authority.
The delegated authority must inform the Commission of all the information it receives from other EU countries.
In the event of a trader’s refusal to comply with the regulation, the designated by the EU country in question, the Commission may decide that the trader should cease to do so. The Commission may, however, decide to grant the trader the right to continue to operate in the EU if he or she is not a member of a trade association.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    дарска барканълар гракародна глакода  гъранарна  
It has applied since 27 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
EU consumer protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cross-border trader: a person who trades in goods and services across borders for profit or loss.
* Trade association: a trade body that represents traders in a trade or business.

In vitro diagnostic medical devices — EU rules for their manufacture and sale

SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the placing on the market and putting into service of in vitro medical devices and their accessories on the EU market.
It repeals Directive 98/79/EC and repeals Decision 2010/227/EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products, such as medical devices, general laboratory products and products for research only. It does not apply to biologics, biodegradable medical devices or products for use in the manufacture of human tissues.
Scope of the regulation
It applies to:
products in the category of ‘biologics’, i.e. products involving human cells or other biological substances, and
products involving human tissues or other living things.
The scope of application of this regulation is clearly delimited from other legislation concerning products that fall within the scope of the European Medicines Agency’s (EMA) ‘borders’ and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
It does not cover:
medical devices for the use of human organs or tissues;
products for the manufacture, manufacture or sale of medical devices for research purposes; or
products intended for the production, manufacture, sale or use of medicinal products.
Products that are not intended for human organs, tissues or biological substances.
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the EFSA of any products that do not fall within its scope.

KEY TERMS

Summary 130:
Electrical appliances burning gaseous fuels — rules for their placing on the market and putting into service in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2016/426 on appliances burning gas (Article 114 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union)
Electromagnetic appliances burning gases (Annex I)
Annex II)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the placing on sale and putting in service of electrical appliances and fittings designed to be incorporated into such appliances.
It repeals Directive 2009/142/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all appliances intended for a number of specified applications and to fittings which are new to the market when they are placed on the EU market. It does not apply to:
new appliances or fittings made by a manufacturer established in the Union or imported from a non-EU country and whether new or second-hand;
appliances from a third country;
new or secondhand appliances and fittingings possessing a historic value or serving within the artistic value of an antique or historic value within the meaning of Article 112 of the TFEU (the Treaty on European Union).
It does not affect:
the safety of the appliances;
the energy efficiency of the fittings;
safety of the gas supply;
compliance with the essential requirements of the directive.
EU countries must ensure that the appliances comply with all essential requirements and that they bear the CE conformity assessment marking.
The European Commission must carry out a conformity assessment before putting on the appliances on sale or putting them into service. It must also carry out an EU-wide market surveillance programme to check that the products comply with the directive’s essential requirements.
Importers of appliances are responsible for placing them on the European market must inform the European Commission of the conformity assessment results within 3 months of putting them on sale. The Commission must then inform the EU countries of the results of the surveillance programme within 3 weeks of putting the appliances into service and of the EU country in which the appliance was placed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLYORK INTO FORCE?
Directive 2009/143/EC had to become law in the Member States by 31 March 2016. EU countries had to transpose it into national law by 31 December 2016.
Regulation 2016/425 amended Directive (EC) No 765/2008 to lay down rules on the accreditation of conformity assessment bodies, provides a framework for the market surveillance of products and for controls on products from third countries, and lays down the general principles of the CE marking. It repeals and replaces Directive (142/141/EC with effect from 31 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
The directive sets out the rules for putting on sale, placing into service or putting into the market appliances that burn gas. The regulation lays down rules to ensure that these rules are applied uniformly throughout the EU.
For more information, see:
Electro-electrical appliances (European Commission).

Summary 131:
Professional cross-border transport of euro cash by road between euro-area countries (CITs)
Professional cross - border transport of euros by road
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1214/2011 on the professional cross -border transport in the EU of cash-in-transit services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to make it easier for professional cash handlers to transport euro banknotes and coins by road across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the professional transport by road of euro-denominated cash in the euro area (Euro area) between participating EU countries. It does not apply to the transport of non-euro notes and coins.
It sets out the conditions under which cash handlers can transport euro cash between participating countries.
Cross-border CITs must hold a licence to transport the cash in a vehicle designed for the purpose of transporting euro cash. The licence must be held by a CIT company established in an EU country with a minimum of 24 months of regularly transporting cash in another EU country without infringing national law before they are granted a licence by that country’s national central bank or CIT cash centre.
The licence is valid for a period of 5 years. It may be renewed for a further 5 years if the holder of the licence has not fulfilled the conditions set out in the regulation.
CIT companies must:
provide the CIT security staff involved with the cash transport;
ensure that the cash is transported under the same conditions as the cash handlers in the other EU countries covered by the regulation;
have the same security staff in each participating country;
notify the competent authorities in the participating countries of any changes to their national rules for the security or transport sectors;
carry out the transport in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 on common rules for access to common international road haul (see summary).
The transport of the cash must be accompanied by the following:
a vehicle designed and built to carry the cash;
a security staff member;
the driver of the vehicle;
cash handlers who have been authorised by the host country to handle the cash.
Transport arrangements
The CIT transport company must ensure that:
the transport of cash is free of charge;
it is accompanied by a security guard;
there is no risk to the safety or health of the security staff or the general public involved; and
the CIT driver has the right to refuse to transport cash if he or she is not authorised to do so.
In addition, CIT companies are required to provide the security guard with the following information:
an annual report on the activities of the company;
information on the number of CIT staff involved in the operation of the business;
details of the total amount of cash transported by road and the total value of cash handled by the company in the previous 5 years;
an estimate of the cost of the transport and the amount of money that is to be transported by CIT in the preceding 5 years, including the amount to be moved by road in the last 5 years and the cost to be paid for the transport.
A CIT must also provide information on:
its annual turnover;
its turnover in the past 5 years by the end of the previous financial year;
how much cash it has been transported in the current financial year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
  The regulation has applied since 29 November 2011 and had to become law in the Member States by 29 November 2012.
BACKGROUND
The introduction of the euro has considerably increased the need for the cross-Border transport of money by road. The very principle of a single currency implies the freedom to move cash between the participating Member States.
For more information, see:
‘Cross-Border Transport of cash’ on the European Commission's website.

  —

Successive amendments to the directive have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 132:
Nutrition labelling for foodstuffs
Nutritionlabelling for foods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011 on the provision of food information to consumers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the labelling of foods to help consumers make informed choices about the foods they eat.
It aims to ensure that consumers are appropriately informed as regards the food they consume and to prevent any practices that may mislead the consumer.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all foods on sale in the EU. It does not apply to pre-packaged foods or to certain categories of foods, such as ready-to-eat cereal, cereal bars, cereal-based foods, cereal syrup, cereal powder, cereal cereal bars and cereals.
EU countries must ensure that the information on the nutrition content of the food is clearly visible on the packaging and on the label of the product.
The information must be accompanied by a statement that:
the food is safe and wholesome for human consumption;
there is no risk of adverse reactions to the food;
the nutrition content is not likely to be harmful to the health of the consumer or to the environment;
consumers have the right to know whether the food meets their nutritional needs;
it is safe for them to eat the food on their own initiative.
Labelling must include the following information:
a description of the foods’ composition;
an indication of the amount of protein, carbohydrate, fat, carbohydrate and sugar in the food contains;
a statement of the total carbohydrate content;
information on the carbohydrate content and the total protein and carbohydrate content of each carbohydrate and carbohydrate in the product
the amount of fat in the final product
a summary of the carbohydrate and protein content by weight, carbohydrate content, protein content and total protein content,
the total carbohydrate, protein and fat content by volume,
a list of the carbohydrates, protein, fat and carbohydrate contents by weight and a list of carbohydrate content by carbohydrate by weight
the number of grams of carbohydrate per 100 g of food,
total carbohydrate and total carbohydrate in each serving
the quantity of protein in the finished product,
how much fat is in the total product and
the carbohydrate content per 100 grams of food
the content of protein and the carbohydrate contents of each serving.
Directive 2000/13/EC lays down the general principles and requirements of food law, establishing the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and laying down procedures in matters of food safety. It also lays down procedures for the establishment of the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Food Safety and Health.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    даранска барка  гракова  харна глаки  
It has applied since 22 November 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 22 November 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  настанка is a system of national laws in the European Union (EU), the European Economic Area (EEA) and the United Kingdom (1) that aims to protect the health and well-being of EU citizens.
For more information, see:
Food labelling (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Food: a food that is intended to be eaten by a person who is under the influence of a diet.
* Prepacked food: a prepackaged product that is placed on a plate or in a bag and placed in front of a consumer without the knowledge of the contents of the prepacked food.

Summary 133:
Issuing euro coins
Issuing euros
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 651/2012 on the issuance of euro coins (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuing of euro notes and coins in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
Issues
EU countries may issue 1-euro and 2-euro coins to commemorate specific subjects, subject to limits set per year and per issuing Member State for the number of issues of such coins.
They may also issue euro collector coins, which are not intended for circulation and which should be readily distinguishable from circulation coins. They may not be issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
The Commission must conduct an impact assessment on the continued issuance of 1- and 2 euro coins. The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council on the results of the impact assessment within 3 months of the Commission’s conclusion of the report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 27 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
  —  
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Commemorative euro coin: a euro coin designed to commemorate a specific subject. It may be issued in denominations of 1 euro, 2 euro, 3 euro, 5 euro, 10 euro, 50 euro, 100 euro, 200 euro, 500 euro, 1000 euro, 2000 euro, 1,000 euro and 1,500 euro.
1-euro coin: coins denominated in euro and cent and complying with the denominations and technical specifications laid down by the Council.
2-euro commemorative coins: coins designed to celebrate specific subjects. They must comply with the technical specifications set out in Council Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro and Council Recommendation 2009/23/EC on common guidelines for the national sides and the issuance by EU countries of euro coin designs.
Commemorative coins may only be issued for the purpose of collecting. They do not have the status of legal tender in the EU country of issuance and may be used only in the country of destruction of fit euro circulation coins and the use of euro collect coins. EU countries must consult each other prior to the destruction of these coins and ensure that they inform the Commission of any plans to issue commemorative euro coins in another EU country.
3-euro collector coins: these coins are designed to be used for the purposes of collecting euro coins intended for collection. They are issued by the issuing EU country with the highest volume of coins in circulation per year.
4-euro collectorative coins: collectible euro coins designed for the collection of euro banknotes and coins issued by euro countries with the lowest volume in circulation. These coins are not issued with the aim of entering into circulation and must be authorised by the European Central Bank (ECB).
5-cent commemorative coin: collectorative euro coins which are intended to be entered into circulation by the holder of the coin.
6-cent collector coins are intended for the collecting of coins issued in other EU countries.
7-cent collectors coins are issued in the form of coins with the same design as the 1-cent coin. They have the same form as the 2-cent coins, but may be deposited in the same place as the 3-cent collectorative coin. The design of the coins is determined by the Commission.
8-cent and 10-cent collection coins are authorised only by the country issuing the coins in question.
9-cent collections coins may be minted only in a country with a high volume of circulation coins in order to ensure that the coins can circulate effectively.
10-cent collected coins may not enter into circulation until the Commission has issued a report on the impact of their production on the cost and public acceptability criteria.

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — the social fund for the EU’s social partners (2013-2020)
European social fund (EsF) - the social network for the European Union (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1304/2013 on the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the framework for action by the European social fund, the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the Cohesion Fund for Europe, the EU's social partners, to support the European 2020 strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth.
KEY POINTS
The ESF is the social partner for the social partners of the EU, which are the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Fund for Strategic Investments (EFSI), the EU countries’ social partners and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD). It is responsible for supporting the implementation of the European Agenda for New Skills and Jobs, the "Youth on the Move" and the "European Platform against Poverty and Social Exclusion". It also supports relevant activities in the initiatives on the "Digital Agenda" and "Innovation Union".
The EU is confronted with structural challenges arising from economic globalisation, technological change and an increasingly ageing workforce and growing skills and labour shortages in some sectors and regions. They have been compounded by the recent economic and financial crisis, which has resulted in increased levels of unemployment, hitting in particular young people and other disadvantaged people, such as migrants and minorities. The ESFocusing on social inclusion, the ESF aims to improve employment opportunities, fight poverty, promote education, skills and life-long learning and develop active, comprehensive and sustainable inclusion policies in accordance with Article 162 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU), and thereby contribute to economic, social and territorial cohesion.
It is funded by the EIB, the EFSI and the EAFRD.
The EIFI is the main source of financial support for social partners in the EU. The EEFI supports the implementation by the Member States of the Europe 2020 strategy, which aims to create a Europe that works for all citizens, regardless of their age, gender, ethnic origin, nationality or place of birth. It also aims to reduce social exclusion, poverty and discrimination in the context of the "Europe 2020 strategy" and to promote the better functioning of labour markets by enhancing the better mobility of workers, the better integration of migrants and refugees and the fight against social exclusion and discrimination.
In order to achieve its objectives, ESF supports Member States’ efforts to:
promote employment, improve access to the labour market and support voluntary labour mobility;
fight against poverty and social exclusion;
ensure the guarantee of adequate social protection;
support active and healthy ageing, including through innovative forms of work organisation, promoting health and safety at work and increasing employability at work;
encourage cross-border geographical mobility by enhancing transnational geographical mobility, in particular through the creation of a European mobility network.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?

It has applied since 20 December 2013 and had to become law in Member States by 20 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
 The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to set up the ESI, including Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006, which lays down the rules for the funding of ESF activities.
For more information, see:
‘The ESI’ website.

Summary 135:
European demographic statistics (2013-2020)
European demographic data (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2013 on European demographic statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and dissemination of EU countries’ annual demographic data to the European Commission (Eurostat).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are responsible for their own national statistics.
EU countries must provide Eurostat with data on:
the total population of each EU country;
the number of people living in the country; and
the age of the population of the country.
The data are collected by Eurostat using the NUTS classification, which is based on the population breakdown of the EU countries according to their territorial units (NUTS 2 regions).
National authorities must ensure that the data they provide to Eurostat are comparable to those provided by the other EU countries. They must also inform Eurostat of the sources of the data, such as the sources and methods used.
Data collection and dissemination
Eurostat is responsible for collecting, transmitting and disseminating the data on the EU’s population by means of the European System of Accounts (ESO) and the European Neighbourhood Statistical System (ENSS).
The European Commission has the responsibility for collecting the data and for the purposes of drawing up the annual EU population projections.
. The Commission publishes these on a regular basis.
Each year, Eurostat publishes a report on the progress made towards achieving the objectives of Article 151 TFEU, including the demographic situation in the EU. It also publishes a monitoring report on progress towards achieving economic, social and territorial cohesion. The report provides an objective statistical picture of progress, based on a Union set of sustainable development indicators.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
It has applied since 10 December 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Territorial unit: a geographical subdivision of an EU country which is broken down by a NUTC (National Urban Statistical System) classification.
* Regional unit: an administrative subdivision of a country which has more than 1 million inhabitants and is divided into more than 2 million administrative subdivisions.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Textile labelling and marking of non-textile parts of animal origin — textile fibre names and related labelling of fibre composition of textile products (Regulation (EU) No 1007/2011)
Textile labels and markings of certain textile products which contain non-animal origin — textiles fibres — textile fibres
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules for the labelling or marking of certain products which are not made exclusively of textile materials but have a textile content which constitutes an essential part of the product or to which attention is specifically drawn by the economic operator.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down harmonised rules with regard to certain aspects of textile labelling, in particular textile fibre names. It sets out the requirement for manufacturers to indicate the presence of non animal fibres on the labels, markings and documents which accompany textile products at the various stages of their production, processing and distribution.
Labelling must indicate:
the fibre name of the fibre;
the quantity of the fibres in the product; and
the quality of the finished product.
The labelling must be clearly visible on the label or on the product’s accompanying documents. It must also indicate the fibre composition of the textile.
Manufacturers must indicate the fibre name and the fibre composition in the form of a numerical value in the format indicated in the Annexes to the regulation. This must be done in a language easily understood by the end consumer.
(s) and in the end user (e.g. the consumer).
).
Labels and markings must indicate that the product has been manufactured in a way that is clearly visible to the consumer and that is not misleading. The labelling should be accompanied by the textured fibre name ‘Fibre Name’ or ‘Textile Fibre Fibre Composition’. The textured fibres must be labelled ‘Ternary Fibre’, ‘Binary Fibre Mixture’ and ‘Mixtures’ in accordance with Directive 96/73/EC on the quantitative analysis of ternary fibre mixtures (see summary).
The texturing of the label and the accompanying document must indicate, in a clear and legible way, the fibre content of each fibre and the quantity of each fibre in each textile. The label and accompanying document should also indicate that:
a fibre name is used to indicate that a fibre has been made from a fibre of a non-human animal origin;
a fibre name indicates that the fibre is made from fibres of an animal origin other than that of the animal;
an indication of the quality of a fibre is indicated on the texturing document indicating that a fibre is made of a fibrous material other than the animal origin.
EU countries may not prohibit the use of a new textile fibre name if it is compatible with Article 114 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and is not incompatible with the Treaties. However, they must ensure that it is not used for the purpose of misleading the consumer or that it does not refer to a fibre which is not of the same fibre composition as the one specified in the regulation or which is of a different fibre composition.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?

KEY TERMS
* Ternary fibres: fibres which are made from ternaries, which are fibres that are derived from the fibrous materials of animals.
* Mixtures: mixtures of two or more different fibres, such as cotton, wool, linen, rayon, cotton twill, woolen twine, cotton thread, cotton cordage, cotton wool, cotton rope and cotton thread.

support for the development, implementation and coordination of coordinated maritime operations and decisions;

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at EU airports within a balanced approach (Balanced Approach)
Noise - related operating reductions at EU airport within a Balanced Approach (BAA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 598/2014 on the establishment of rules and procedures with regard to the introduction of noise-related operational restrictions at Union airports within the Balanced Approach
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out rules for the introduction by EU countries of operating restrictions (noise restrictions) at airports in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the procedure for introducing operating restrictions.
It does not apply to civil aircraft, military aircraft, police aircraft, customs aircraft or any other aircraft engaged in emergency rescue operations.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of the decision to introduce an operating restriction at an airport within the BAA.
The Commission must inform the other EU countries within 3 months of the notification.
A noise assessment is carried out by the Commission before the decision is made.
If the Commission considers that an airport does not meet the noise abatement objectives of the regulation, the EU country may impose an operational restriction.
An operational restriction may be imposed on an airport if:
the noise level of the airport is greater than 0.5 decibels per hour;
the airport is located in a densely populated area;
there are more than 50,000 flights per year in the airspace around the airport; or
there is a significant increase in the number of passengers flying between the airport and the nearby residential areas.
In addition, the Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC) every 3 years on the results of the noise assessment. The report must contain information on the measures taken by the EU countries to reduce the noise level at the airport. The Commission must also provide the EEC with a report on the impact of the operating restrictions on the environment and on the rights of interested parties. The EEC must publish its report in the Official Journal of the EU by 31 December each year.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of the ‘balanced approach’ to noise management, which aims to improve the noise environment around EU airports.
For more information, see:
‘Balanced approach to air traffic management’ on the European Aviation Safety Agency's website.
KEY TERMS
* Noise: the sound of an aircraft’s engine.
* Ambient noise: the noise of the air in which an aircraft is flying.
ACT
 			 	  	
Commission Directive 2002/30/EC of 15 February 2008 entitled ‘Noise Operation Restrictions at EU Airports’ and repealing Directive 2006/93/EC (OJ L 173, 12.6.2008, pp. 65-72)

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings — rules for the coordination of insolvencies in EU countries (Regulation (EU) 2015/848) (REcast)
Transnational insolvent companies have more and more cross-border activities and are therefore increasingly being regulated by EU law. This Regulation aims to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of insolvent proceedings across the EU. It lays down rules on jurisdiction, recognition and applicable law in this area.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules on the coordination between EU countries when an insolvent company or other legal person seeks to liquidate its assets in another EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
bankruptcy proceedings which meet the conditions set out in it, irrespective of whether the debtor is a natural person or a legal person, a trader or an individual.
Direct winding-up proceedings, which are directly derived from insolvent cases, and analogous proceedings and actions related to such proceedings are excluded from the regulation. However, the mere fact that a national procedure is not listed in Annex A to this regulation does not imply that it is covered by Regulation (EU] No 1215/2012.
National procedures for winding-ups are laid down in the annex to the regulation, which is binding and directly applicable in all EU countries. These procedures must be carried out by the competent national courts of the EU country in which the insolvent debtor is resident.
The insolvent creditor must have the right to appoint a lawyer to represent him or her in the proceedings. The lawyer must be appointed by the court of the debtor in the country where the proceedings are taking place. The debtor may also appoint a representative of the general body of creditors to represent the creditor in the course of the proceedings, provided that the representative is not a member of the creditor’s general body. The representative must be independent from the debtor and must not have a financial interest in the case.
Annexes A and B of the regulation set out the national procedures to be followed by insolvent creditors in the event that the debtor wishes to transfer assets or judicial proceedings from one EU country to another, seeking to obtain a more favourable legal position.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 5 June 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 5 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Commission adopted a report on the application of Council Regulation (EC) No 1346/2000 in the context of the restructuring of companies. The report concluded that the regulation is functioning well in general but that it would be desirable to improve its application in order to enhance the effective administration of cross–border insolvent procedures.
For more information, see:
‘Involving insolvent entities’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 142:
Liability of carriers of passengers by sea in the event of an accident in the European Union (EU) — liability for damage caused to passengers in the case of an international transport accident
Liability for carriers of passenger ships in the EU — insurance for damage inflicted on them by an international passenger ship
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 392/2009 on the liability of carriers carrying out international passenger transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down liability rules for passengers travelling by sea to and from a foreign port.
It aims to ensure a proper level of compensation for passengers involved in maritime accidents.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all international passenger ships registered in an EU country. It does not apply to vessels registered in a non-EU country.
The liability of the carrier must be recognised by the national authorities of the country in which the passenger is travelling.
Carriers must make an advance payment to the insurance company to cover the costs of the damage caused by the accident. The advance payment must be made at least 30 days in advance of the date on which the accident occurred.
Insurance premiums must be paid by the carrier. The insurance company must provide the carrier with a statement of the total amount of the insurance premiums to be paid. Insurance premiums are calculated on the basis of the number of passengers on board the ship and the total number of persons on board. The carrier’s liability is calculated on a case-by-case basis.
 basis. The amount of insurance premiums paid must not exceed the total value of the passengers on the day of the accident and must be calculated on an annual basis. If a passenger dies or is seriously injured on board, the carrier is liable for the death or personal injury.
An insurance policy must be in place for the duration of the journey. The policy must include the following information:
the name of the insurer;
the number of people covered by the insurance policy;
a description of the type of insurance policy and the amount of damage caused;
details of the policy and of the person covered by it;
how the policy is to be drawn up and drawn up;
where an insurance policy is not in place, the insurer must draw up an estimate of the cost of insurance for the period from the date of the incident to which the policy was drawn up. The insurer must also draw up a list of the persons covered by that insurance policy. Insurance companies must also provide the passenger with information on the rights to be conferred on passengers prior to their journey or, where that is not possible, at the latest on departure. The information must be provided in a language easily understood by the passenger.
A passenger may refuse to pay the advance payment if he or she:
does not wish to be compensated for the loss of his or her life; or
is not entitled to compensation for death, personal injury or damage to the property of another person.
If a passenger does not pay, the passenger must be informed of the rights conferred on him or her by means of a written statement.
In the event that a passenger is killed or injured, the person who is responsible for the damage must be notified of the cause of death or injury by the person responsible. The person responsible must also inform the passenger of the reason for the injury or death. The passenger must also be provided with a copy of the death certificate and the identity of the body responsible for his/her identity. The body responsible must inform the other passengers of the identity and identity of that body.
Passengers are entitled to the right to compensation of up to €10,000 for each of the following:
for the death of a passenger;
for personal injury to a passenger who is a member of the family of the passenger; and
for death or bodily injury caused by a passenger on board a passenger ship.
Claims must be filed with the competent authority of the EU country where the accident took place. The claimant must be able to prove that the damage was caused by negligence on the part of the ship-owner or the passenger himself or herself. The claim must be submitted to the competent authorities of that EU country within 30 days of receipt of the claim. If the claim is refused, the claimant must provide proof of the negligence of the vessel-owner and the passenger shall be entitled to a compensation of at least €5,000. The liability must be established by the competent national authority of that country and must not be less than the sum of the claims of the claimant and the insured passenger. In addition, the claim must include proof that the passenger was accompanied by a valid travel document issued by the owner or the holder of the ticket or the ticket.
EU countries must ensure that passengers are informed of their rights to compensation and that they have the opportunity to make a claim against the carrier of their own free will. They must also ensure that the carrier has the right of appeal against the decision of the court of an EU



Summary 143:
European rail network for competitive freight
European rail system for freight transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 913/2010 — establishing international rail corridors for a competitive freight network
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the establishment of international rail freight corridors for the purpose of providing rail freight services across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the rules for the setting up of international freight corridors (i.e. rail freight networks) which meet specific needs in one or more clearly identified segments of the freight market.
The objectives of the regulation are to:
ensure that freight trains can run under good conditions and easily pass from one national network to another;
provide a good quality and sufficiently financed railway infrastructure that allows freight transport services to be provided under good terms in terms of commercial speed and journey times and to be reliable, namely, that the service it provides actually corresponds to the contractual agreements entered into with the railway undertakings.
It also aims to improve the conditions of use of existing railway infrastructure by enabling the required interconnections between existing rail infrastructure.
Design of freight corridors
The design of the corridors should take into account, where appropriate, the need for better interconnection with the rail infrastructure of European non-EU countries and with those of European third countries. Projects aimed at establishing such freight corridors should be supported by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF. The EIB is responsible for the development of rail infrastructure in the EU.
.
Funding
The EIB and the EIB are funded by the EU’s Cohesion Fund, which is the main source of EU funding for the creation of the European rail network. It is managed by the Commission and is managed under the responsibility of the Commission. The Commission may also provide financial support from the European Economic and Social Fund (EESF) and from other sources, such as the European Research and Innovation Facility (ERIF) or the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF).
The European Commission may grant EIB or EEF financial support for projects which meet the following criteria:
the establishment of an international freight corridor;
the development of an intermodal link between the existing TEN-T and/or the ERTMS rail networks; and
the creation of a European railway traffic management system (ERTMS) for the transport of freight.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?

BACKGROUND

Summary 144:
Rules on admission to the occupation of road transport operator (OJ L 300, 14.11.2009, pp. 51-58)
Rules on entry into the occupation and the conditions to be complied with by road transport operators (Oj L 300)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1071/2009 — common rules concerning the conditions concerning the requirements to be met to pursue the occupational qualification of road haulage operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common rules for the admission of companies to the EU’s internal market in the transport of goods and certain cabotage operations.
It repeals Council Directive 96/26/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to undertakings operating in the outermost regions of the European Union (EU) which have the right of establishment in national and international transport operations. The only condition imposed on such undertakings is that they have a Community licence, which can be obtained provided they satisfy the following conditions:
they have a minimum level of professional qualification (i.e. an EU licence);
they fulfil the conditions laid down in Regulation (EC, No 1072/2009 on common rules on access to the international road transport market;
they meet the conditions set out in Article 299(2) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (see summary).
EU countries may decide to adapt the conditions with which they wish to allow undertakings established in those regions to pursue this occupation. However, they must ensure that they do so only as a result of adapting to the requirements laid down by the regulation.
Amendments to the regulation
An EU countries may apply for a derogation from the requirements of the regulation if they do not fulfil the requirements set out by the EU country in which they are established.
The derogation may be granted if the derogation is justified by the following reasons:
the derogation does not affect the operation of the road transport business;
the operating conditions of the business are not incompatible with the rules on road safety and social welfare, which may harm the image of the sector.
An amendment to the regulations may also be granted by the European Commission if it is necessary to ensure that:
there is no conflict of interest;
there are no conflicts of interest between the interests of the undertakings concerned;
it does not infringe on the rights of the public or the public policy of an EU country; or
the regulation does not apply to the activities of an undertakings which have been admitted to the job by the competent authorities of the country where the operation is carried out.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?

* Occupational qualification: a professional qualification is a document that indicates that an individual or organisation has a high level of competence in the field of transport.

Summary 145:
Coordination of social security systems — procedure for implementing Regulation (EC) No 987/2009
Coordinating social security system — procedure to implement Regulation (EU) No 883/2004
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) 2018/858 — rules on the coordination of EU countries’ social security services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on how EU countries must coordinate their social security and pension systems and the procedures for implementing them.
It aims to improve the efficiency of these systems by simplifying and streamlining the procedures between national social security institutions and the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all persons covered by the social security legislation of the EU country in which they live or work. It does not apply to:
those who are not covered by this regulation;
those whose social security benefits are covered by other EU legislation;
persons who do not have the right to benefit from the benefits of the system or who have not been members of it for more than 6 months;
individuals who have been members for less than 2 years;
members of the family of an insured person who are members of the institution responsible for the processing of their benefits, but who are no longer members of that institution;
the family members of a person who is not a member of the institutions responsible for processing his/her benefits.
EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities of the country where the insured person lives or works have access to the relevant information, such as the name, address and telephone number of the person concerned;
information on the person’s place of residence and the identity of the persons concerned; and
the person concerned receives a timely response to their request for information on their rights.
The European Commission is empowered to establish and manage a database and ensure that it is operational at least from the date of entry into force of this regulation. The Commission must, in particular, take the necessary steps to integrate into that database the information listed in Annex 4 of the regulation. It must also ensure that the information in the database is updated in real time.
Access to information
The competent authorities and social security institution must provide the person covered by Regulation (i.e. the person in question) with all the necessary information to make a decision on whether or not to accept or reject a claim for social security. The person concerned must receive a response within the time limit set out in the legislation of that country within which the claim is examined. The information must be provided by the competent institution within 6 months of receipt of the application.
In addition, the competent authorities must provide information on the persons who are entitled to benefits under the regulation, including:
their name and contact details;
their place of birth;
where the person is a resident of an EU country other than the one in question;
any other information that may be necessary for making a decision;
a description of the conditions under which the person may refuse to accept a claim;
details of the rights and obligations of the insured persons concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
  И   дантарска барка  гракова  храна гарна
Regulations (EC, EEC, Euratom) 2018 and 2019 have applied since 30 October 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
The social security regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and streamline the coordination between EU countries and the Commission in order to reduce administrative and administrative burdens on citizens.
For more information, see:
‘Social security coordination’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work website.

Summary 146:
Statistics on public health and health and safety at work — European statistics on public and occupational health indicators (ECHI)
Statistics on national and EU-wide health and occupational safety indicators (ECHI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1338/2008 on Community statistics on Public health and Health and Safety at work
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU) statistical system for the collection of comparable data and information on health and workplace safety indicators.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Commission (Eurostat). It sets out the minimum statistical data set needed for the calculation of the European Community Health Indicators (ECI), a list of indicators that provide an overview of health status, determinants of health and systems.
The European Commission is responsible for developing the ECHI and Eurostat, the European Union’s statistical office, is in charge of its implementation.
EU countries are responsible for collecting the data and providing the data to Eurostat for the purposes of calculating the ECI. The data are collected by national authorities, Eurostat and Eurojust, Eurojust and the Commission.
It is the responsibility of Eurostat to ensure that the data collected by EU countries are comparable and that the information provided by the national authorities is comparable.
National authorities must ensure that:
the data collected are collected in accordance with the European schedule of occupational diseases (the European schedule for occupational diseases), which is set out in Annex II to the regulation on occupational diseases;
 (Annex III to the Regulation on occupational disease statistics, which sets out Annex IV to the regulations on occupational illnesses;
the information collected by the EU countries is comparable to those collected by Eurostat.
Each year, EU countries must send Eurostat a report on the results of the national surveys they have carried out and on the basis of the information they have provided.
Annex V sets out how the data are to be used to calculate the ECHI and how they should be used in the development of a sustainable health monitoring system.
In addition, the Commission must provide the European Parliament and the Council with an annual report on its implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
  The European Commission has been working towards the creation of a European information system for public health indicators since 2002.
For more information, see:
‘Health and safety indicators’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Occupational health indicators: indicators that measure the health status of people working or living in the same place at the same time, such as occupational accidents, occupational illnesses and occupational accidents at work. They are used to assess the impact and effectiveness of the measures taken under the new Community strategy on occupational safety and health, as well as to take into account the increase in the proportion of women on the labour market and to respond to their specific needs in relation to policies on health protection at work, and in particular to the need to develop a European statistical system in the area of occupational safety. These indicators are used by the Commission to identify possible indicators for joint objectives for developing care systems and to provide information on the progress made in the context of the Community action programme in the field of public health (2003-2008).
* European Community health indicator: an indicator that measures the health of people living or working in a particular place at a particular time in order to assess their health status and their health and well-being. It is based on a set of measures that are designed to measure the impact of the actions taken by the Member States to improve the quality of life of people in that place and to assess how the measures are being implemented.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Summary 148:
Hydrogen-powered motor vehicles — type-approval of hydrogen-powered vehicles (Regulation (EC) No 79/2009)
Hydrogen powered motor vehicles – type approval
The European Union (EU) has adopted a new regulation to allow hydrogen vehicles to be registered in the EU. It sets out the technical requirements for the type approval of hydrogen vehicles and their trailers.
ACT
   
Commission Regulation (EC’s) No 78/2009 of 14 January 2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council amending Directive 2007/46/EC.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the technical specifications for hydrogen systems and components that can be used to power motor vehicles.
It sets out rules for the EU type approval procedure for these systems.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the vehicle meets the requirements laid down in Annexes IV, VI and VII of the regulation;
the hydrogen system and its components meet the requirements set out in Annex IV;
there are no exemptions from the requirements for hydrogen storage or usage, and the vehicle has to be equipped with a hydrogen storage system capable of storing up to 10 kg of hydrogen per kilogrammes of fuel.
The vehicle manufacturer must submit an application to the European Commission for type approval within 3 years of the date on which the vehicle is placed on the market.
If the Commission considers that a hydrogen system or component meets all the requirements, it grants the hydrogen type approval. If it does not, it must inform the manufacturer of the reasons for its refusal.
.
Where the manufacturer does not comply with the requirements of Annex IV, the manufacturer must notify the Commission of the reason for the refusal. The Commission must then issue a technical opinion on the hydrogen vehicle. If the manufacturer fails to provide the Commission with an opinion within 3 months of receiving the technical opinion, the Commission may refuse to grant the type of hydrogen vehicle it considers to be a hydrogen vehicle to the manufacturer. The manufacturer must then inform the Commission within 2 months of the refusal of the vehicle.
In the event of a refusal, the company must submit a technical proposal to the Commission, which must then grant the hydrogen manufacturer type approval in accordance with Annex VII. The hydrogen manufacturer must also submit the technical proposal for the hydrogen propulsion system and the hydrogen system components to the competent authorities of the EU country in which the hydrogen is to be placed.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2017/852 amends and repeals Regulations (EC, EU) No 77/2009, (EC), (EU, EU, EFTA) No 852 and (EU), as well as Regulation (EU).
BACKGROUND
The introduction of alternative fuels can significantly improve the quality of urban air and consequently also the state of public health.
For more information, see:
‘Alternative fuels’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at European Commission's website.

Summary 149:
Access to the natural gas market — conditions for access to the gas transmission networks
Access to access the natural energy market — requirements for third parties
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 715/2009 on conditions for accessing the gas market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for third party access to natural gas networks in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all natural gas transmission and distribution systems in the EU. It does not apply to high-pressure pipelines linking up local distributors to the network which are not primarily used in the context of local distribution.
Tarriffs for access are set out in the regulation’s Annex I.
The tariff for access is calculated according to the actual costs of an efficient and structurally comparable network operator (i.e. a natural gas distribution system operator) and the actual cost of operating the network. The tariff is calculated using the benchmarking of tariffs by the regulatory authorities of the EU countries.
It does not affect the tariffs for access provided for in Directive 2009/73/EC on the internal market in natural gas (see summary).
The tariffs are calculated on the basis of:
the actual costs incurred by the network operator;
the return on investments made by the operator and the return on investment (ROI);
the level of investment by the third party in the network; and
the amount of gas supplied to the consumer.
Access conditions
the tariff is based on the same criteria as those set out of the national market for natural gas.
Third party access services
The third party must provide the consumer with:
a guarantee of equal market access for all gas consumers in the country in which it is located;
access to the market for the purposes of the sale of natural gas in the territory of the country where it is supplied;
information on the prices charged for the services provided;
a description of the costs of the services offered;
an indication of how the tariff is to be calculated.
A third party may not provide the same services as the national network operator if:
it does not have a dominant position in the market;
it is not a member of the regulated gas market; or
it has not been involved in the operation of the network for more than 5 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 August 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 August 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Natural gas: natural gas supplied by pipelines or other means of transport.
* Transmission and distribution system: a system where natural gas is transported by pipelines and/or other means to and from a distribution point to a consumer’re home or business.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    The regulation is a delegated act of the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 July 2009 laying down the rules for the access of third parties to the national natural gas markets in the Member States. It repeals and replaces Directive 2003/55/EC.
last update 14.08.2016



Summary 150:
Negotiating and concluding agreements with non-EU countries on civil justice matters
Negotiations and conclusion of agreements on civil Justice matters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 662/2009 establishing a procedure for the negotiation and conclusion agreements between Member States and third countries on particular matters concerning the law applicable to contractual and non-contractual obligations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure to authorise a Member State to amend an existing agreement or to negotiate and conclude a new agreement with a non-European Union (EU) country.
It does not affect the exclusive competence of the EU and the provisions of Articles 300 and 307 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the procedure for authorising an EU country to amend or conclude an agreement with another EU country on specific civil justice issues falling within the scope of Article 300 of the TFEU. It does not apply if the EU has already concluded an agreement on the same subject matter.
An EU country must notify the European Commission of its intention to conclude a bilateral agreement with the country concerned by way of an already existing mandate of negotiation or an envisaged mandate.
The Commission must then notify the EU country of its intentions to conclude the agreement by means of an electronic notification. The agreement must contain all the information necessary for the conclusion of the agreement, including:
the terms and conditions for its conclusion;
the reasons for the agreement’s entry into force;
a description of the parties’ rights and obligations under the agreement;
details of the procedures for concluding the agreement.
If the Commission considers that the agreement is not compatible with the EU acquis, it may decide to terminate the agreement without prejudice to its exclusive competence. If the agreement does not contain the necessary information, the agreement may be terminated.
In the event that the Commission decides to terminate an agreement, it must inform the other parties of the reasons for its decision and inform them of the reason for its termination. It may also decide to suspend the agreement until the other party has provided the necessary documentation to conclude it.
Where the Commission concludes that an agreement is incompatible with the acquis or with the principles of EU law, it has the right to withdraw from the agreement and to enter into negotiations on a new one. The Commission may also withdraw from negotiations if it considers the agreement to be incompatible with EU law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARITY APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 July 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Non-EU country: a country which is not a member of the Community.
* EU country: any EU country which has its own laws in force and which does not have its own legal system in place.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    The regulation is known as the ‘Judicial Cooperation Regulation’. It has been amended several times, most recently in 2013 by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852.
last update 14.07.2020



Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and international protection — EU countries’ data on foreign workers and asylum seekers (Regulation (EC) No 862/2007)
Statistics on migrants and asylumseekers (regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the collection and analysis of EU statistics on migration, international protection and asylum.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It does not cover estimates of the number of persons illegally resident in the EU. Member States must not provide such estimates or data on such persons to the European Commission (Eurostat), although they may be included in population stocks due to surveys.
EU countries must provide Eurostat with information on:
the total number of foreign workers in their territory;
the number of non-EU nationals who have entered the EU illegally;
total number of EU citizens who have left the EU;
number of EU countries that have refused to grant asylum to non-European Union citizens.
The data must be collected and analysed by Eurostat in accordance with the rules laid down in the annex to the regulation.
National authorities must ensure that the data are collected in a uniform manner and that they provide the data to Eurostat within 3 working days of receiving them.
Eurostat has the right to use the data provided by the national authorities for the purposes of its work programme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULTRY ENTER INTO FORCE?
  ?
Regulation(EC) 2018/862/2008 has applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
The Thessaloniki European Council of 19 and 20 June 2003 concluded that more effective mechanisms were needed to collect and analyse data on immigration and asylum in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum statistics’ on Eurostat’s website.

Summary 153:
Common Fisheries Policy — rules for the conservation and sustainable exploitation of fisheries resources under the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) (European Commission).
Common fisheries policy — rules governing the conservation, management and exploitation of fishery resources in the common fisheries policy (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the Common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to ensure that fishing and aquaculture activities contribute to long-term environmental, economic, and social sustainability.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the EU’s fisheries policy. It aims to ensure the conservation of marine biological resources and the management of fisheries targeting them.
EU countries are responsible for:
conserving and managing marine resources at levels which can produce the maximum sustainable yield within national jurisdiction and on the high seas;
cooperating with other countries to that end, and to apply the precautionary approach widely to conservation and management measures;
ensuring the availability of food supplies and that they reach consumers at reasonable prices.
It also lays down rules on market measures and financial measures in support of its objectives.
The CFP applies to:
fresh water biological resources;
aquaculture;
fishery activities;
the processing and marketing of products marketed in the EU.
Fisheries
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the CFP. It is assisted by the European Fisheries Agency (EFTA).
The Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts.

Summary 154:
