SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1379/2013 on the common organisation of the markets in fisheries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), as a whole, has a single market for fishery products in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the rules governing the common fisheries markets (CMOs) for the fishery sector and the fish sector in general. It aims to improve the placing on the market of products and to encourage the participation of small-scale fishery producers in the sector.
It sets out rules on the allocation of fishing quotas and the management of fishing effort. It also lays down the rules on fishery producer organisations (PIOs) and on the sharing of fishery resources.
The CMO is managed by the European Commission, which is responsible for the day-to-day management of the fisheries sector. The Commission is assisted by the Fisheries and Aquaculture Directorate-General (FADD).
The Commission has the responsibility for the implementation of the CMO. It is assisted in this task by the FADD and the European Fisheries Management Board (EFMB.

Summary 155:
Improving securities settlement in the EU and on central securities depositories (CSDs) — Regulation (EU) No 909/2014 (SUMMARY)
Improving financial markets in the European Union and on Central securities depositors (CSDs)
This regulation aims to improve the safety, efficiency and competition of the EU’s securities settlement markets by harmonising rules on the activities of CSDs. It also aims to ensure that CSDs are subject to the same prudential requirements when importing the risks encountered by CSDs from other EU countries.
ACT
Regulation (EEC) 2018/852 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2018 on the harmonisation of rules on CSDs and amending Regulation (EEU) 2018 /852 on the supervision of securities settlement systems (SPSs) (OJ L 347, 16.12.2018, pp. 1-2)
SUMMIT
This Regulation aims to enhance the safety and efficiency of the securities settlement market in the Union by harmonizing rules on:
the activities of central securities depositors (CDSs);
the supervision of the CSDs by national authorities;
the requirements for CSDs when importing securities from other countries;
rules on the operation of the SPSs when CSDs import securities from another EU country.
The regulation lays down the following rules:
The CSDs must:
ensure that their activities are governed by the same rules as those applicable to other CSDs;
have the same level of risk management and risk control procedures;
be subject to a risk-based prudential framework for their activities;
provide information to the market on the risks they face;
notify the authorities of any CSD that has been involved in an insolvent situation;
report to the European Commission every 3 years on the state of their systems;
keep the Commission informed of any changes to the rules.
Each CSD must have a risk management system in place to deal with the risk of insolvency or disruption of its systems. The system must be able to cope with a number of different types of risks, including:
bankruptcy;
lack of liquidity;
loss of market share;
failure of a CSD to comply with the requirements of the regulation;
non-compliance with the regulation.
In addition, the regulation sets out the rules for the establishment of a central information system (CIS) and for the management of the CIS. The CIS must be set up by the CSD in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 852. It must be up-to-date with the latest developments in the securities market and be available to the public within 3 years of its establishment.
National authorities must ensure that:
CSDs comply with all the requirements set out in the regulation, including those relating to their risk management systems and their risk control systems; and
the CIS is set up in such a way that it can be used by all CSDs, irrespective of the country in which it is located.
A CSD may be subject to different rules depending on whether it is based in an EU country or in a non-EU country. In addition, national authorities may impose different rules on certain aspects of its activities, such as:
its role in the execution of cross-border securities transactions;
its liability for the loss of market participants’ money in the event of a failure of its securities settlement system; or
the amount of money it is liable to lose if it fails to fulfil its obligations.
Regulators must also:
set out a procedure for the identification of the persons responsible for the failure of a CIS;
set up a procedure to identify the person responsible for failing to fulfil their obligations;
draw up a list of the individuals responsible for failure to meet their obligations, including the number of persons responsible and the reasons for their failure;
establish procedures for the notification of the competent authorities of the failure to fulfil the obligations of CDSs;
impose penalties on the CDS if it is found that a CDS has failed to meet its obligations or that it has not complied with the obligations laid down by the regulation or by the national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 28 August 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* CDS: a central depository is a financial institution that holds securities on behalf of investors.
* CSD: a depositary is a bank that holds the securities of an individual or group of individuals or groups of individuals.

Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund — environmental and transport infrastructure projects (TEN-T) — energy efficiency and renewable energy projects (EU-wide) (EU)
Cohesion fund — environmental, transport and mobility projects (U-TEN)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 — setting up the European Structural and Investment Funds (ESIFs) and repealing Council Regulation (EC-No 1084)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE RECOMMENDATION?
The regulation sets out the tasks of the European Commission’s ESMF, the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Social Fund, the Cohesion Fund and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD).
It repeals and replaces Council Regulations (EC, No 1083/2006 and (EC), which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2013.
The ESMIFs are the main sources of funding for the EU's social partners and the EAFRD. The EIFs support projects in the fields of the environment, transport infrastructure and rural development.
KEY POINTS
The European Commission is responsible for setting out the priorities for the ESMFF, the EDFs and the EU-wide cohesion funds. The objectives are to:
promote energy efficiency, renewable energy and smart energy distribution, storage and transmission systems and measures aimed at reducing air pollution, even if one of the indirect effects of such activities is the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions, or if they are listed in Annex I to Directive 2003/87/EC (see summary);
increase investment in the transport sector outside the trans-European networks, rail, river and sea transport, intermodal transport systems and their interoperability, management of road, sea and air traffic, clean urban transport and public transport.
In order to achieve these objectives, projects must comply with the TFEU (the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU) and the TEN (the Trans-European Transport Network) guidelines. They must also meet the following criteria:
contribute to the creation of a network of high-quality, high-speed rail and high-capacity road networks across the EU;
encourage the use of renewable energy;
support the development of energy efficiency in the energy sector, including the co-generation of heat and power and in district heating networks;
provide technical assistance to the public authorities of EU countries;
ensure that projects are implemented by the same economic operators and include activities such as energy efficiency investment, smart energy transmission and distribution systems, and smart urban transport projects.
EU countries must ensure that the projects covered by this regulation comply with all the requirements of the ESIFF.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?

It has applied since 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* ESMTF: the European structural and investment funds (EIFs).
* TEN: the transport network for the European Union (TFEU) which aims to improve the quality of life of its citizens and reduce the environmental impact of its activities.
* COVID-19: the fight against the spread of disease, poverty and other forms of social exclusion in Europe. It aims to tackle the root causes of these problems by improving the health and well-being of people, the environment and the economy of the countries involved. It is the main source of EU funding for social cohesion and social cohesion.

Summary 157:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — Investment for growth and jobs goal — specific provisions concerning the investment for jobs goal and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1080/2006 (Regulation (EU) No 1301/2013)
European regional development fund (ERFD) — investment for growth, jobs and climate change goal — special provisions concerning investment for job creation
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s regional development funds, the European Regional Development Funds and the European Territorial Cooperation Fund (ETSF).
KEY POINTS
The ERDF is the main instrument of the European Union (EU), which aims to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
It is funded by the European Social Fund (ESF), the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and the Cohesion Fund. The ERDF also contributes to the EU strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth, thus ensuring greater concentration of ERDF support on the priorities of the EU.
Scope
The European Regional development fund supports the investment priorities relating to the shift towards a low-carbon economy. It also supports the European territorial cooperation goal, which encourages regional cooperation between regions in order to reduce regional imbalances in the EU, particularly in the north, the south and the east.
The EU countries’ efforts to reduce their geographical distance from the rest of Europe.
EU countries must ensure that the ERDF supports activities that are not listed in Annex I to Directive 2003/87/EC (see summary). These include activities such as energy efficiency investments in district heating networks, smart energy distribution, storage and transmission systems and measures aimed at reducing air pollution, even if one of the indirect effects of such activities is the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions, or if they are listed in the national plan referred to in Directive 2002/83/EC.
In addition, the EU countries must provide the European Commission with information on the types of projects that are eligible for ERDF funding under the EAFRD and the ETFRD.
Programmes
The EDF supports the following types of activities:
research and innovation, information and communication technologies (ICT), small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and promoting a low carbon economy.
These activities must be supported in line with the EU's investment priorities, which are set out in Article 4 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) and in particular Articles 178 and 349 of the Lisbon Treaty.
Cohesion Fund funding
The funding is allocated by the Commission to:
support projects that contribute to the development of jobs and the creation of jobs in the regions of the Member States;
provide financial support for projects that support the development and growth of small businesses;
support research and innovation (R&I) in the fields of information technology (ICT) and information society (IoS) and promote the growth of SMEs;
contribute to the establishment of a European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF), which supports projects that promote the sustainable management of maritime resources and the sustainable use of marine resources.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation 2013/12/2013 has applied since 20 December 2013.

Summary 158:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — specific provisions for the support from the EU's cohesion policy to the European Territorial Cooperation goal
European regional development fund (ERTDF) - specific provisions on the support for the European territorial cooperation goal
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1299/2013 on specific provisions to be made available to the EU’s cohesion policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out specific rules for the funding of cross-border, transnational and interregional cooperation under the European regional development funds (ERCDFs).
It aims to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, crossborder and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF supports projects that aim to tackle common challenges identified jointly in the border regions, such as: poor accessibility, especially in relation to information and communication technologies (ICT) connectivity and transport infrastructure, declining local industries, an inappropriate business environment, lack of networks among local and regional administrations, low levels of research and innovation and take-up of ICT, environmental pollution, risk prevention and negative attitudes towards neighbouring country citizens.
The funding is provided by the European Commission, the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD).
. The ERDF is one of the four main pillars of the EU cohesion policy: the European cohesion policy, the Cohesion Fund, the Structural and Investment Funds (SIFs), the Regional Cooperation Fund (RDF), the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFFD) and EAFRD.
Programmes
The European Regional Development Funds (ERFDs) are funded by the EU countries’ cohesion policy priorities. They aim to support the harmonious development of their territories at different levels. They are divided into 4 priority areas:
transnational cooperation
cross-border cooperation
research and innovation
interregional research and development.
Cross-border research
Interregional collaboration
Research and innovation (R&I)
Research-intensive R&I programmes
The Regional Research and Innovation Framework (RIFs)
The RIFs are designed to support R&Is in the areas of research, innovation and entrepreneurship in the regions of the participating EU countries. They support the development of research facilities and research clusters, as well as the creation of research centres and R&D centres in the participating countries.
Cooperation in the fields of education and health
The EIFs aim to promote the integration of the labour market in border regions by encouraging the exchange of knowledge and experience between EU countries and other countries in order to improve the quality of education, health and social services in border areas.
Transnational cooperation is designed to strengthen cooperation by means of actions conducive to integrated territorial development linked to the Union's cohesion priority priorities, and also to exploit the untapped growth potential of border areas (development of R & I facilities and clusters), while enhancing the cooperation process for the purpose of the overall harmonious growth of the Union.
Regional cooperation
The regional cooperation programmes under the EIFS are organised by the Regional Coordination Centre (RCC). The RCC is responsible for coordinating the activities of the RDFs under the RIFFs and for monitoring and evaluating the implementation of the programmes. It is assisted by the RCC and the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    даральна на барска гракова  гаркога  
It has applied since 20 December 2013 and had to become law in the Member States by 20 December 2014.

Summary 159:
Trans-European energy infrastructure — guidelines for trans-European networks
Trans - European energy networks — guidelines to support the completion of the EU internal energy market while encouraging the rational production, transportation, distribution and use of energy resources
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 347/2013 — guidelines on guidelines for cross-border energy infrastructure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down guidelines for the development of cross-European (TEN-E) energy networks.
It aims to help the EU to meet its energy and climate policy objectives of competitiveness, sustainability and security of supply.
KEY POINTS
The guidelines are designed to:
ensure that the EU’s gas and electricity networks are interconnected and that all EU countries have access to the same energy supply and distribution networks;
reduce the isolation of less-favoured and island regions;
secure and diversify energy supplies, sources and routes, including through cooperation with non-EU countries;
provide for alternative supply or transit routes and sources of energy and to develop renewable energy sources in competition with traditional sources.
The regulation repeals Decision No 1364/2006/EC. It repeals Regulation (EC) No 713/2009, which sets out the rules concerning energy infrastructures for the European electricity market and the common market for gas. It also repeals Regulations (EC), No 714/2009 and No 715/2009.
Scope
The legislation applies to energy infrastructure for the period from 1 January 2015 to 31 December 2020. It does not apply to the transmission and distribution of electricity between the EU countries or to the interconnections of natural gas and liquefied natural gas (LNG and LNG).
It does not affect existing energy infrastructure, such as pipelines, power stations, distribution grids, transmission lines, interconnectors, substations, etc.
Projects must comply with the following conditions:
they must meet the following criteria:
have a minimum installed production capacity of at least 10 % of their installed production capacities;
have the capacity to supply at least 20 % of the total energy consumed in the EU by the end of the period covered by the guidelines;
be designed to meet the objectives set out in the guidelines.
They must be designed and built in a way that minimises the impact of the energy network on the environment, human health, the environment and the economy. They must also be designed in such a way as to minimise the environmental impact of energy infrastructure.
In addition, they must meet certain other criteria, including:
availability of the infrastructure;
availability and reliability of the network;
cost efficiency;
environmental impact;
energy security;
transparency and accountability.
Trans-EU energy networks
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up a list of selected projects for the implementation of the guidelines and for monitoring the progress of these projects. The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the progress made by the selected projects.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULAR ACT APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 25 April 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 April 2015.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Trans-European: an interconnector is an energy network that links different energy resources in different EU countries.
* Transmission and distribution network: a network that provides electricity, gas or LNG (natural gas, LNG) with other energy resources across the EU. It is a key element of the European energy system and is essential for the functioning of the internal market and for the supply of energy to consumers and businesses. It links the different energy markets in the European Union (EU).
* Interconnector: an energy infrastructure network that connects different energy sources, including gas, electricity and natural gas, in order to provide a reliable supply of electricity and gas to consumers, businesses and the public.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �   The regulation is known by its acronym, TEN-2013. It replaces and repeals Decisions (EC, 2006, 2007, 2008, 2009, 2010, 2011, 2012, 2013, 2014, 2015, 2016, 2017, 2018, 2019, 2020, 2021, 2025, 2030, 2030 and 2040.

Summary 160:
Online dispute resolution (ODR) for online sales and services
Obligations for online sellers and traders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 524/2013 on online dispute resolution for consumer disputes and amending Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 and Directive 2009/22/EC (Regulation on consumer ODR)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DIRECTIVE?
The regulation aims to make it easier for consumers and traders to resolve disputes arising from the sale of goods or the supply of services online.
The directive amends the 2006 and 2009 directives on alternative dispute resolution* (ADR) to ensure that consumers can use ADR when they have a dispute with a seller or a trader.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This regulation applies to all online transactions in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to the sale or supply of goods, services or other goods by a trader in another EU country.
Disputes arising from online transactions must be resolved by an ADR platform. ADR platforms may be set up by traders or by consumers themselves.
EU countries must ensure that:
ADR platforms are open to all traders and consumers in their EU country of residence, irrespective of whether they are resident in the EU or not;
AdR platforms must be open to traders in all EU countries, regardless of where they are registered in the country in which they are located;
adR platforms can only be used by traders who have registered in an EU country other than the EU country where the ADR is set up.
AdDR platforms must:
provide consumers with an online complaint procedure;
allow traders to submit their own complaints against ADR providers;
set up a dispute resolution procedure for traders;
ensure that disputes can be settled by ADR within 3 working days of the complaint being submitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 June 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 June 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  *   * * 
For more information, see:
Online dispute resolutions (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
 *  * *  * Online dispute resolution: an online dispute solution that allows consumers to resolve their disputes with a trader or seller by means of an ADDR platform.
* ADR: an ADRA platform that allows users to resolve online disputes by means, for example, of an online mediation service.
ACT
  —
  The regulation has applied from 18 June 2014.

Summary 161:
Rules for direct payments to farmers under support schemes within the framework of the common agricultural policy (CAP) (2013-2020)
Rules for directly paying farmers in the European Union (EU) under the Common Agricultural Policy (2007-2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1307/2013 — rules for direct payment to farmers in support schemes under the common agriculture policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the payment of direct payments by the European Commission (the EU’s executive arm) to farmers covered by support schemes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out a list of the direct payment support schemes covered by it.
The Commission has the power to adopt acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) to supplement or amend certain non-essential elements of the regulation.
Direct payments are made to farmers on the basis of the following criteria:
permanent grazing areas must be kept at a minimum of 16 hectares per year, irrespective of whether the land is cultivated or grazed;
the land must be managed according to the rules laid down in the regulation;
a good agricultural and environmental condition must be in place;
farming activities must be carried out in a way that minimises the risk to the environment and human health;
farmers must have the necessary financial resources to carry out the activities covered by the support scheme.
EU countries must ensure that:
the annual grazing area is at least 16 hectares, regardless of whether it is cultivated, grazed or not;
there is a limit on the amount of annual grazing land that can be paid to farmers;
all grazing activities must comply with the rules set out in the Regulation;
in the event of non-compliance, the Commission may impose administrative measures and administrative penalties in the case of noncompliance.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Direct payment rules for support schemes came into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and streamline the rules of the CAP. These include:
introducing a new system for calculating the total amount of support to be paid by the Commission to farmers for the period 2013-2020;
reducing the administrative burden for EU countries by simplifying the procedures for applying the rules.

‘Direct payments’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 163:
Statistical returns in respect of the carriage of goods by road (recast) (react) (REFERENCES
Act
Regulation (EU) No 70/2012 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 18 January 2012 on statistical returns on the carriage by road
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by Member States of the Commission’s exercise of implementing powers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
Each Member State must compile comprehensive regional statistics with regard to:
carriage of goods and vehicle journeys by means of vehicles registered in the Union;
the degree of utilisation of vehicles carrying out this transport.
The data must be collected in each Member State under the authority of the bodies and institutions in charge of compiling official statistics.
EU countries may exclude certain vehicles from the scope of the regulation, such as:
vehicles belonging to public undertakings or public transport undertakings with a maximum load limit of less than 1 tonne;
vehicle belonging to private undertakings whose maximum weight limit is less than 2 tonnes;
other vehicles belonging to a private undertaking with a load limit less than 3 tonnes.
Access to the sources of administrative data
Each EU country must provide the Commission with access to its administrative data. The Commission must ensure that the data are provided in a timely manner.
Communication of individual data, once rendered anonymous, is necessary in order to estimate the overall accuracy of the results.
Commission implementing powers
The Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU. It must ensure a simultaneous, timely and appropriate transmission of relevant documents to the EU Parliament and to the Council.
Implementation of this regulation is subject to the following conditions:
the Commission must transmit the results of the collection of the data to the national authorities of the Member State in which the data were collected.
It must communicate the results to the European Commission within 3 working days of receiving the data.
If the Commission considers that a Member State has not complied with this regulation, it must inform the other Member States and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) of its decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
Regulations (EC) No 1172/98 and (EU), No 182/2011 have applied since 1 February 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the common transport policy, which aims to improve the efficiency of transport in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Carriage of Goods by Road’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 165:
Advanced therapy medicinal products — rules for their production, distribution and use in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 1394/2007 on advanced therapy medicines
Advanced therapy medicines — rules to ensure the free movement of those products within the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2001/83/EC on medicinal products for human use — the EU’s rules on the manufacture, import, export and import of advanced therapies for the treatment of diseases and disorders in human beings (see summary)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the production, import and export of advanced therapy (ART) medicines for the purpose of treating or preventing diseases in humans.
It aims to ensure that these medicines are safe, effective and not to interfere with human health.
KEY POINTS
   
 
The regulation applies to all medicinal products intended for use in or administered to human beings with a view to restoring, correcting or modifying physiological functions by exerting principally a pharmacological, immunological or metabolic action. It does not apply to products that are intended to be placed on the market in Member States and either prepared industrially or manufactured by a method involving an industrial process, in accordance with the general scope of the Community pharmaceutical legislation laid down in Title II of Directive 2001/1983/EC.
The legislation does not affect the rights of human beings or the fundamental rights of animals.
Scope of the regulation
It applies to:
advanced therapy products containing viable cells or tissues, such as gene therapy, somatic cell therapy, stem cell therapy and tissue engineering*;
tissue engineered products (e.g. stem cells derived from the stem cells of the human body, stem cells from the embryonic stem cell line or stem cells obtained by means of stem cell transplantation;
sensory products derived from stem cells or stem tissues derived from human embryonic stem cells.
Manufacturers of ARTs must ensure that:
they comply with the quality and safety requirements set out in Annex I to the regulation;
they ensure that their products are manufactured according to specific quality standards and that they meet the requirements of the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Medicines Agency (EMA);
they have the necessary documentation to prove that they have been authorised by the competent authority of the Member State in which they are being manufactured;
the competent authority has the power to authorise or prohibit the import, import or export of products that do not comply with this regulation.
EU countries may restrict the import of products which do not meet the definition of a medicinal product. They may also restrict the use of these products if they do not fulfil the following conditions:
the product is not intended for the intended use of a patient;
it is not authorised for the use by a medical practitioner for a medical prescription for a custom-made product for an individual, in order to comply with an exclusive professional responsibility of a medical doctor;
there is no reason to believe that the product does not fulfil these conditions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
  The regulation has applied since 10 December 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Advanced therapy: a medical device or combination of medical products intended to treat or prevent a disease or disorder in humans, or to restore, correct or modify physiological functions.
* Tissue engineered product: a product derived from a human cell or a stem cell.

Summary 166:
Emissions from light passenger and commercial vehicles (Euro 5 and Euro 6) and access to vehicle repair and maintenance information (OJ L 171, 29.6.2007, pp. 1-8)
Emissionsfrom light passenger or commercial vehicle (EEC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 715/2007 on type approval of motor vehicles with respect to emissions from light-duty vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s type-approval procedure for light passenger vehicles (e.g. vans) and their trailers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all new vehicles entering the European Union (EU) from 1 January 2008. It does not apply to existing vehicles, such as buses and coaches, that have already been approved for use in the EU.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of the type of vehicle they intend to approve by 1 January 2009.
The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of its decision within 30 days of receiving the notification.
It must inform EU countries of the date on which it intends to approve the vehicle and of the technical specifications for the vehicle.
Emission limit values
The regulations set out the emission limit values for each of the three types of vehicle:
light-duty passenger vehicles;
large-duty commercial vehicles; and
dwarf passenger vehicles.
Each EU country must set its own emission limit value for each category of vehicle. These are set out in Annexes I and II to the regulation.
Access to vehicle technical information
The European Commission must provide EU countries with a standardised format for the transmission of vehicle technical specifications. This format should be used to retrieve the technical information from manufacturers’ websites.
Manufacturers must provide the Commission with technical specifications and technical information on their websites within 3 months of receiving a notification from the Commission. The Commission must then publish the specifications on its website.
National authorities must ensure that the Commission has the information it needs to make a decision on whether to approve a vehicle. If the Commission considers that a vehicle does not comply with the regulation, it must inform it of the reasons for its decision and inform the other EU countries.
If a manufacturer does not provide the information requested by the Commission, the Commission may decide that the vehicle is not a candidate for type approval.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    дарнальна барка гародна is the official name of the EU country in which the regulation has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a comprehensive type approval system for motor vehicles, established by Council Directive 70/156/EEC (see summary).
For more information, see:
EU type approval (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —
 
  накалка  гранна   is the abbreviation of the acronym for the European Chemical Safety Agency (ChemSA). It is the body responsible for administering the harmonised system for the control of the emissions of organic solvents and other volatile organic compounds (VOCs) from motor vehicles. It is also responsible for the monitoring of VOCs and other VOC-related substances in the environment and for the harmonisation of national legislation on the VOC and VOC control system.
Successive amendments to the original regulation have been incorporated in the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 167:
Food additives, enzymes and flavourings — common authorisation procedure
Food additives and flavouring agents — common authorised procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1331/2008 establishing a common authorisations procedure for food additives, food enzymes and food flavourings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU-wide procedure for the authorisation of food additives*, enzymes*, flavourings* and food flavours* for use in foodstuffs for human consumption.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following substances:
food additives,
food enzymes* and
food flavourings*.
Authorisation
The European Commission must assess the safety of these substances before authorising them to be placed on the market or used in food.
An application for authorisation must be submitted to the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) by the manufacturer or importer of the food or food ingredient. The application must contain the following information:
the name of the competent authority responsible for assessing the substances’ safety;
the date of entry into force of the EU list of authorised substances;
a description of the substances concerned;
details of the steps to be followed by the Commission to grant authorisation.
If the Commission considers that the substances pose a risk to human health or the environment, it must issue a decision to authorise them.
The Commission must consult the EFSA and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic Area (EEA) before granting authorisation to the substances. The Commission must also consult the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Food Safety and Consumers’ Affairs (DGFSA) and the Committee of the Regions (COSA).
Authorisations are granted on the basis of a risk assessment carried out by the competent authorities of the Member States. The EFSA is responsible for monitoring the authorisations granted to substances and for ensuring that they comply with the rules of the regulation.
Authorised substances may not be used on the EU market or placed on it unless they fulfil the conditions laid down in each sectoral food law, unless they are included on a Community list.
EU countries must notify the Commission of any substances that are not on the list. If the Commission receives an application for an authorisation it must inform the EEA of the reasons for the decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
   
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Food additive: a substance used in the production and handling of food to enhance the flavour, colour or texture of foods.
* Flavouring agent: an ingredient that adds colour, texture or other characteristics to foods to make them more attractive, appealing or appealing to the eye, mouth or other sensory organs of the consumer. It is a substance that gives the appearance of a flavour or texture that is different from the natural flavour of the foods it is intended for. It may be added to the food in order to enhance its attractiveness or to increase the attractiveness of the flavour of foods or food ingredients. It can also be used to colour, enhance the texture or colour of food or to make it more attractive to the eyes, ears, nose or mouth.
Food flavourings: an additive or flavouring agent that adds flavour or colour to food to improve the texture, colour, smell or taste of a food. They may be used as a colouring agent or as a flavour enhancer. They can be used in combination with other flavourings, such as colouring agents, colourants or colourants. They must be added in accordance with Regulation (EU) No 1082/2013 on the use of flavourants in food and may be accompanied by a warning label or a warning sign.

Summary 169:
Agreement between the EU and Iceland and Norway on the surrender procedure for suspects and convicts (REACHING THE AGREEMENTS) — Declarations
Agreement concerning the surrender of suspects and convicted persons by the Member States of the European Union (EU) and Iceland (Iceland) and Norway (Norway)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2006/1021/EC on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European EU and the Republic of Iceland and the Kingdom of Norway on a surrender procedure between the Member countries of the EU, Iceland, Norway and Norway
Decisions 2006/12/EC and 2006/13/EC concluding the Agreement on the European Arrest Warrant for the purpose of prosecution or execution of sentence between Iceland and Iceland, and Norway, and on the Commission’s proposal for an EU-Iceland extradition agreement between the two countries
Agreements between the parties to improve judicial cooperation in criminal matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISIONS AND OF THE REGULATION?
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the Council of Europe (now the EU).
The agreement aims to speed up the procedure for the extradition of suspected or convicted persons from the EU countries to the EU country where they have been arrested.
KEY POINTS
The EU countries are parties to a number of international conventions in the field of extradition, including the EU Convention on extradition of 13 December 1957 and the European Convention on the suppression of terrorism of 27 January 1977. The EU countries have uniform extradition laws with a common concept of extradition.
An EU country may refuse to extradite a suspect or convicted person to another EU country if there is a serious risk that he or she would be subjected to the death penalty, torture or other inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment in that country.
The surrender procedure is based on a warrant issued by the country where the requested person has been arrested and the decision on his or her surrender must be made by the judicial authority of the country of arrest.
A judicial authority in the country which issued the warrant must take the decision to surrender the requested suspect or convict to the competent authorities of the other EU country. The decision must be based on the following principles:
the person has the right to a fair trial;
the warrant has been issued by a country of nationality or ethnic origin,
the requested person is a national of an EU country and has been convicted of a criminal offence;
there is no risk of the person being subjected to torture or to other cruel or inhuman treatment and punishment in the other country;
no person who has been surrendered to a country where there is serious risk of death or is subject to torture is liable to be extradited to that country for the purposes of the execution of the warrant.
In the event of refusal to surrender, the person concerned must be informed of the reasons for the refusal and of the conditions under which the refusal may be made. The requested person may also refuse to surrender to the country for which the warrant was issued.
If the request for surrender is made by a person of a nationality other than that of the requested country, the country in which the request is made must inform the requesting country of the reason for refusal. The requesting country must also inform the other parties of the grounds for refusal and the reasons on which it may decide to refuse the surrender.
Where the requested persons has not been informed of reasons for refusal, the request may be refused on the grounds that:
he or she is a threat to public order or public security;
it is contrary to the principles of fundamental rights; or
there are grounds to believe that the person has committed a crime punishable by death or by imprisonment;
he/she is a danger to public security or public order, public security, public health or public safety, public order and public health, or is a risk to the public or private security, or to public health and public order.
Each EU country has the power to decide whether to grant or refuse to grant the surrender if it considers that the refusal is in the interest of the public interest, public policy, public safety or the protection of the general public or the public, or if the refusal would be contrary to public policy or the interests of the common interest of public security.
Under the agreement, the parties agree to improve, in accordance with the provisions of this Agreement, the cooperation in the area of extradition between, on the one hand, the countries which have signed the EU convention on extradition, and the countries that have not signed the convention, by taking account of, as minimum standards, the terms of the Convention of 27 September 1996 relating to extradition between EU countries. The agreement does not prevent a country from applying its constitutional rules relating to due process, freedom of association, the freedom of the press and freedom of expression in other media and freedom fighters. It also does not preclude the exercise of the right of a judicial authority to apply its national law in the context of the implementation of this agreement.

Summary 170:
Rail passengers’ rights and obligations — rail passenger information and information — rail passengers' information
Rail passengers' rights and information – rail passenger Information and information
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on rail passenger rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to safeguard the rights of rail passengers and to improve the quality and effectiveness of rail passenger services in order to help increase the share of rail transport in relation to other modes of transport.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down a number of rights for rail passengers, including the right to:
information on the accessibility of rail services, access conditions of rolling stock and the facilities on board;
access to the ticketing system;
the right to buy a ticket on board a train without extra charges;
refusal to charge for the transfer of a ticket from one railway company to another.
Railway undertakings and station managers must take into account the needs of disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility when providing information on rail services. They must also ensure that:
railway services are accessible to passengers with sensory impairment, whether caused by disability, age or any other factor;
railways are designed to be accessible to all passengers, irrespective of their physical or mental capacity;
there is a minimum level of protection for passengers in the event of a major accident or major renovation work on the railway system.
Passengers are entitled to a minimum amount of compensation for any delay of more than 30 minutes in connection with a rail service. The amount of the passenger is calculated on the basis of the total number of minutes of the rail service and the duration of the delay.
The rights of passengers must not be less than those of other passengers.
In addition, the regulation sets out a procedure for the compensation of passengers who are the weaker party to the transport contract and who do not have the right of refusal to travel on the rail system. The regulation does not apply to passengers who have a disability or who are not entitled to compensation for the loss of mobility caused by a major renovation of a railway system or to the removal of a passenger’s seat.
Amendments to Regulation (EU countries
Amending Directive (EU) 2018/852 amends the regulation to ensure that passengers with disabilities have the same rights as other EU citizens when travelling by rail. These include:
the possibility to buy tickets on board the train;
a right to transfer a ticket to another railway company without the need to pay a transfer fee;
an obligation for railway companies to provide information on the conditions of the carriage of passengers on the way to and from the railway station;
free access to the information and reservation system on the train.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
    дарска нарка  бакова  харна граша 
It has applied since 3 December 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
Access to rail services
The European Commission has adopted a common transport policy for the European Union (EU). It aims to increase the number of passengers using rail services in the EU and to encourage the use of other forms of transport, such as buses and light rail.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission's website.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to improve the quality of education and training statistics in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries to collect and compile data on the activities of EU countries’ educational and training systems and to monitor their implementation.

Summary 172:
Energy statistics
energy statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1099/2008 on energy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and dissemination of European Union (EU) energy statistics.
It aims to improve the availability of accurate, up-to-date data on energy quantities, their forms, sources, generation, supply, transformation and consumption, for the purpose of monitoring the impact and consequences of its policy work on energy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are exempt from the regulation.
EU countries must provide the European Commission with:
data on:
energy production and consumption;
electricity generation and supply;
energy consumption; and
energy supply and demand.
The Commission must ensure that the information provided by EU countries is comparable to those provided by the European Energy Market Observatory (EEMO).
The data must be provided in the form of:
total energy supplied and consumed by each EU country;
total electricity generated and supplied by each energy source;
the total electricity generated by each source, including nuclear energy and renewable energy;
actual consumption of electricity and gas;
average energy consumption by each type of energy source.
National authorities must also provide the Commission with the following information:
actual energy production and use of electricity by each electricity source by each of the EU countries;
power generation and consumption of gas and natural gas by each European Union country; andactual energy consumption of all energy sources by each individual EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
From 14 November 2008.
BACKGROUND
Energy statistics have traditionally been focused on energy supply and on fossil energies. In the coming years, however, greater focus is needed on increased knowledge and monitoring of final energy consumption, renewable energy and nuclear energy. This information is essential for assessing the impact of energy consumption on the environment, in particular in relation to the emission of greenhouse gasses.
For more information, see:
EU energy statistics (European Commission).

Summary 173:
Rights of passengers in bus and coach transport — protection in the event of an accident (Regulation (EU) No 181/2011)
Regulations of the European Parliament and of the Council on the rights of passengers and amending Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It aims to ensure that passengers have the same rights as all other citizens with regard to free movement, freedom of choice and non-discrimination.
It lays down rules for the protection of passengers who are the weaker party to the transport contract.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all passengers travelling by bus or coach in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
passengers who have a legal duty to maintain their own physical or mental health;
passenger who are not covered by a contractual obligation to carry out the transport of goods or passengers.
Rights
The legislation applies to passengers who:
have a disability or reduced mobility;
have the right to use a bus or a coach without discrimination;
are not refused access to a bus and/or a coach on the grounds of their disability, age or any other factor, except for reasons which are justified on grounds of safety or of the design of the vehicle or infrastructure.
Passengers are entitled to compensation for death, including reasonable funeral expenses, or personal injury as well as for loss of or damage to luggage due to accidents arising out of the use of the bus.
They are also entitled to assistance in the immediate practical needs following an accident, including first aid, accommodation, food, clothes and transport.
Disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility may not be refused transport on the basis of their mobility or their age.
The European Commission must ensure that:
disabled persons have access to the terminal of the terminal where the service is operated;
the disabled persons have a right to a seat on board the bus and a seat in the coach;
disabled persons have the right of refusal to use the bus or the coach if they do not have the necessary mobility to do so;
disability is not a reason for refusal of carriage.
Transport bodies must notify disabled persons of any refurbishment or refurbishment of existing buses or coaches and of any new ones that are to be refurbished or refurbished.
In addition, the Commission must take into account the needs of disabled persons and their reduced mobility when designing and managing new buses and coaches.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2011/181/2011 has applied since 28 February 2011 and had to become law in the EU countries by 28 February 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Passengers’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 175:
North-East Atlantic fisheries — control and enforcement — North-east Atlantic fisheries (SEA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1236/2010 laying down a scheme of controls and enforcement applicable in the area covered by the Convention on future multilateral cooperation in the North-E Atlantic fisheries and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 2791/1999
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the implementation of the EU’s fisheries control system (SEA), which aims to ensure that fishing vessels flying the flag of a non-EU country do not engage in illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing activities in the waters of the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation (NATO) Convention Area.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to fishing vessels operating in the maritime area of the Convention Area which lies beyond the waters under the fisheries jurisdiction of the Contracting Parties (the Regulatory Area). It repeals and repeals the previous regulation (Regulation No 2790/1999) which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 1999.
It lays down the rules for fishing vessels in the zone and for fishing activities outside the zone which are authorised to be carried out by vessels flying their flag.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any fishing vessels that do not comply with the rules of the scheme. The Commission must inform the relevant EU country of the vessel's authorisation and notify the other contracting parties of any infringements.
The Commission may also suspend or cancel the authorisation of a fishing vessel if it considers that the fishing vessel is in breach of the rules.
the fishing vessel has not complied with the requirements of the regulation.
Inspections
The EU country which authorises the vessel to fish in the convention area must designate an inspector to carry out inspections at sea. The inspector is responsible for:
monitoring and surveillance of the fishing activities of the vessels flying its flag;
checking that the vessel is carrying out fishing activities within the scope of its authorisation;
carrying out inspections of vessels that are not covered by this regulation;
ensuring that vessels that fail to comply with this regulation are informed of the reasons for the suspension or cancellation of their authorisation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO ENTER INTO FORCE?
The scheme entered into force on 1 May 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into their national law by 1 May 2011.
BACKGROUND
The convention was approved by the Council in Decision 81/608/EEC on 17 March 1982.
For more information, see:
Fisheries and Fisheries (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* North-Atlantic Fisheries Commission (NEAFC): an international organisation responsible for the rational conservation and management of fishery resources in the Area defined by the convention (the Convention Area).
* Fishing vessel: a vessel flying a flag of one of the contracting parties to the convention.
* Fishing vessel: any fishing vessel that is not a member of a contracting party to the Convention.

Summary 176:
Ecolabel — European Union (EU) eco-label (EU Ecolabel)
EColabel – European Union ecolabel (European Union)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 66/2010 on the EU Ecolabels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the criteria for the award of the EU eco-labels (ecolabels) to products that have a reduced environmental impact during their entire life cycle.
It aims to provide consumers with accurate, non-deceptive, science-based information on the environmental impact of products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products in the EU, including food and feed products, as well as unprocessed agricultural products that lie within the scope of the organic production and labelling of organic products. It does not apply to non-food products, such as pharmaceuticals, cosmetics, medical devices and cosmetics.
The criteria for awarding the ecolabs are based on the best environmental performance achieved by products on the European Union market. They must be based on scientific evidence, taking into account the latest technological developments.
Eligible products
Products must meet the following criteria:
have a minimum environmental performance of at least 0.5% by weight of the product during its whole life cycle;
have at least 1% by volume of the material used in the production, processing, packaging or transport of the products;
not use of hazardous substances (e.g. mercury, lead, mercury, arsenic, cadmium, lead and mercury, hexavalent chromium, hexafluorocarbons, hexamethyllead and hexamethylfluoridea, hexacarbons and hexavalentalfluoride;
be manufactured in a way that minimises the impact of these substances on the environment, health and the environment.
Products that fulfil all of these criteria are eligible for the Ecolabs. However, the European Commission may decide to exclude certain products from the list of eligible products if they do not meet the criteria. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for this decision within 3 months of receiving a proposal for an application for an ecolabel. If the Commission considers that a product does not meet all the criteria, it may withdraw from the scheme.
In addition, the Commission may grant an award to a product that does not comply with the criteria set out in the regulation if it considers that the product has a significant environmental impact and that its environmental performance does not correspond to the criteria laid down in the legislation.
Awards are awarded to products which fulfil the criteria listed in Annex I of the regulation. The award is valid for a period of 5 years from the date on which the product is placed on the market. The period of validity of the award is determined by the Commission’s decision to grant the award.
Annex II sets out a procedure for the revision of the criteria and for the withdrawal of an award for products that do not conform with the new criteria. It is up to the Commission to decide whether to withdraw the award for a product which does not conform.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Егалка барка  блака
  гарша  
 
It has applied since 30 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
  EU EColabels are part of the sustainable consumption and production policy of the Community, which aims at reducing the negative impact of consumption and the production of goods and services on human health, the environment and the climate.
For more information, see:
‘Ecolabs’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —
  The European Commission's European Union eco-label programme (European Commission).
  Its website is accessible on the website of the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (ESA) and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).

Summary 177:
Supplementary protection certificate for medicinal products (SPCs)
Supplementary support for pharmaceutical research
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 469/2009 on the supplementary protection certificates for medicines
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the granting of a supplementary protection certificate to the holder of a patent relating to a medicinal product for which marketing authorisation has been granted in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any substance or combination of substances presented for treating or preventing disease in human beings or animals and any substance which may be administered to humans or animals with a view to making a medical diagnosis or to restoring, correcting or modifying physiological functions in humans or in animals.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 1768/92, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1992.
The protection granted by the regulation is for a maximum of 15 years of exclusivity from the time the medicinal product in question first obtains authorisation to be placed on the market in the Community. It does not apply to the period of effective protection under a basic patent. The protection granted is limited to the product which obtained authorisation for the first time. The duration of the protection granted does not exceed 5 years.
Benefits of the certificate
The holder of both a patent and a certificate may enjoy an overall maximum of 25 years of exclusive protection from the date on which they first applied for the authorisation.
to be granted by each EU country
The duration is set out in the regulation’s national law.
Each EU country may grant the certificate for a period of up to 5 years, but the duration may be reduced to 3 years if the holder has already been granted a national or European patent for a new medicinal product.
A certificate may be renewed for an additional period of 5 years if:
the holder of the patent has applied for an extension of the validity of the existing patent
the product has been placed on sale in an EU country for more than 3 years
the medicinal product has not yet been authorised to be marketed in the country
the authorisation is granted by a national authority
the certificate has been renewed for a further 5 years
The certificate is valid for 5 years after the end of the 5-year period of protection.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 16 June 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 June 2011.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Patent: a patent which protects a product as a result of long, costly research and is designated by its holder.
* Medicinal product: a substance which is used to treat or prevent disease in humans and/or animals and which may also be administered for the purpose of restoring or correcting physiological functions.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  The regulation is codified and replaced by Regulation (EU) 2018/852 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 6 May 2018.
  Its original text is of documentary value only.
last update 16.05.2019



Summary 178:
Judgments in civil and commercial matters — jurisdiction and recognition in civil, commercial and other legal matters
Judgments — jurisdiction in civil or commercial matters
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EC) No 44/2001 on jurisdiction and the recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil matters
Decision (EU) 2017/852 on the conclusion of the Lugano Convention on jurisdiction on the enforcement and recognition of judgments on behalf of the European Community (now the EU) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) Member States
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 on the accession of the EU to the Convention on the jurisdiction of the courts of the High Contracting Parties to the Accession Conventions on jurisdiction, recognition and enforceability of judgments, authentic instruments and court settlements
Decisions (EU and Euratom), Euratom and the EFTA Member States on the entry into force of the convention on the right of persons to be sued in another Member State’s courts for the purpose of enforcing a judgment on their behalf
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE CONVENTION?
The regulation sets out the rules of jurisdiction for judgments and authentic instruments issued by the high contracting parties to the convention. The convention aims to ensure that judgments are enforced in another country where the defendant is a national of that country.
The convention establishes a procedure for the recognition of a judgment issued by a high contracting party to the court of another country that is bound by the convention, and for the purposes of enforcing the judgment.
It also sets out procedures for the enforcement of the judgment by the defendant.
recognising and enforcing judgments issued by another country in the territory of the country of which the judgment was issued.
KEY POINTS
   
The Convention applies to:
the status or legal capacity of natural persons, rights in property arising out of a matrimonial relationship, wills and succession;
bankruptcy, proceedings relating to the winding-up of insolvent companies or other legal persons, judicial arrangements, compositions and analogous proceedings;
social security;
arbitration.
A judgment may only be enforced in the country in which it was delivered. It may not extend, in particular, to revenue, customs or administrative matters.
If a defendant is not domiciled in a country bound by this convention, he or she may be sued by a person domiciling in that country, regardless of his or her nationality, in the court or tribunal of that state. The jurisdiction of that court is determined by the law of that State.
Defendants who are not nationals of the Member State in which they are domiced may, however, be sued only by virtue of the rules set out in Sections 2 to 7 of this Title. The rules of national jurisdiction are not applicable as against them.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The treaty entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The Treaty on European Union (TEU) is a free trade agreement between the EU and the EU countries of the Free Trade Area (FTA). It aims to strengthen the legal protection of persons established in the territories of the countries which are members of the FTA.
For more information, see:
‘Judgments’ on the European Commission's website.
